12 min
Resolving the Two Meanings of Jawaz (Imkan Khas and Imkan 'Amm)
Resolving the apparent contradiction in the author's use of 'jawaz' in two probabilities

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the two types of imkan (khas and 'amm) and apply the Hanafi usul principle that the meaning of 'jawaz' in a legal text is determined by its context (whether contrasted with wujub or haram).

Core Idea
Jawaz means possibility, but its scope depends on context: imkan khas (both sides equal) vs imkan 'amm (one side unrestricted).

In this passage, Sharh Taftazani resolves an apparent contradiction in the author's use of 'jawaz'. The term appears in two different contexts: one where it is opposed to wujub (obligation) and another where it is opposed to haram (prohibition). The solution lies in recognizing two distinct types of possibility (imkan) that 'jawaz' can denote. Understanding this nuance is crucial for correctly interpreting Hanafi legal texts.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two contexts
In the fourth probability, 'jawaz' is contrasted with wujub; in the fifth, with haram. Note the different opposites.
The opposite term reveals the intended scope.
2
Define imkan khas and imkan 'amm
Imkan khas: both sides (act and abandonment) are equally permissible, no necessity. Imkan 'amm: only one side is necessary, the other is not.
Imkan khas corresponds to mubah; imkan 'amm includes wajib and mandub.
3
Test each meaning in the two probabilities
If imkan khas is used in the fifth probability, wujub would be excluded (since wujub requires necessity of act). If imkan 'amm is used in the fourth, wujub would be included, losing the contrast.
This creates the apparent contradiction.
4
Apply Taftazani's resolution
Assign imkan khas to the fourth probability (where jawaz contrasts with wujub) and imkan 'amm to the fifth (where jawaz includes wujub).
Each context gets the appropriate meaning.
5
Extract the usul principle
The ruling: A general term like 'jawaz' may have multiple meanings; its precise legal import is determined by its contextual opposites and the overall structure of the discourse.
This is a form of contextual interpretation (tafsir bi al-siyaq).

Secondly, that the meaning of jawaz in the fourth probability... The purpose of Sharh Taftazani is to answer an anticipated question. Question: It is that in the fourth probability, 'ma laha wa ma alayha' meant 'what is permissible for her and what is obligatory upon her', and in the fifth probability, it meant 'what is permissible for her and what is forbidden for her'. In the fourth probability, jawaz is opposite to wujub, and in the fifth probability, jawaz is opposite to haram. And jawaz means imkan, and imkan has two types: (1) imkan khas, and (2) imkan 'amm. Imkan khas is that which is denuded of necessity from both sides, meaning that neither existence nor non-existence is necessary for it, like for a human, neither existence nor non-existence is necessary. Imkan 'amm is that which is denuded of necessity from one side, e.g., its non-existence is not necessary, whether its existence is necessary or not; or imkan 'amm is that whose existence is not necessary, whether non-existence is necessary or not. The point is that only one side's necessity is negated. Now a question arises: In these two probabilities, jawaz is used in the sense of imkan. Which imkan is intended? Imkan khas or imkan 'amm? If you take imkan khas, then jawaz would mean 'not prohibiting the act and not prohibiting the abandonment', i.e., it is permissible both to do it and not to do it, with necessity negated from both sides. This meaning cannot be intended in the fifth probability because in the fifth probability, jawaz includes wujub (obligation) as well, since here jawaz is contrasted with haram, so wujub is included in jawaz. But if imkan khas is taken, wujub cannot be included because then jawaz would mean that doing and not doing are equal, but that is not correct for wujub, where doing is necessary. And if you take jawaz to mean imkan 'amm, then it is not correct in the fourth probability, because in the fourth probability, jawaz is opposite to wujub, and they are in contrast; if jawaz is taken as imkan 'amm, then jawaz... In that meaning, necessity is negated from one side, e.g., jawaz meaning 'not prohibiting the abandonment' or jawaz meaning 'not prohibiting the act', i.e., its act is not prohibited. In this meaning, wujub itself would be included in jawaz and would not remain opposite, whereas in the fourth probability, wujub was opposite to jawaz, not included in it. Summary of the problem: If imkan khas is intended, it is not correct in the fifth probability; if jawaz is taken as imkan 'amm, it is not correct in the fourth probability. Answer: Sharh Taftazani is giving the answer: Why do you not do this: take the meaning of imkan that is correct in each probability and understand that meaning there. Therefore, in the fourth probability, jawaz will mean imkan khas, so jawaz will mean that both the act side and the abandonment side are not prohibited; rather, both are optional. So in this meaning, it contrasts with wujub, and wujub is not included in it, because in wujub the act side is necessary. And in the fifth probability, since wujub is included in jawaz, jawaz will mean imkan 'amm, i.e., jawaz meaning 'not prohibiting the act' - its act is not prohibited. Now jawaz comes in contrast to haram, because in haram the act side is prohibited, while wujub remains included in jawaz. Then there is a further objection: If jawaz means 'not prohibiting the act and the abandonment', then the author's statement 'fa'l ma siwa al-haram wa al-makruh tahriman...' would not be correct... The objection and answer are provided, but the text is cut off at the end.
Source pages p. 149

Commentary
This passage is a critical refinement of the author's use of the term 'jawaz' in two different contexts. The commentator (Sharh Taftazani) identifies that the author applied 'jawaz' in two probabilities: one where it is contrasted with wujub (obligation), and another where it is contrasted with haram (prohibition). The term 'jawaz' fundamentally means 'imkan' (possibility), which itself has two types: imkan khas (special possibility) where both doing and not doing are equally possible, and imkan 'amm (general possibility) where only one side is necessary but the other is not. The problem is that if we take jawaz as imkan khas, it works for the fourth probability (contrast with wujub) but fails for the fifth probability because wujub would be excluded. Conversely, if we take jawaz as imkan 'amm, it works for the fifth probability (including wujub) but fails for the fourth probability because wujub would be included rather than contrasted. Taftazani resolves this by saying the author intended imkan khas in the fourth probability and imkan 'amm in the fifth probability, each appropriate to its context. The text then raises a further objection about the author's general statement that 'everything except haram and makruh tahriman is permissible for her', which would include wajib and tark al-haram incorrectly. The answer is that the author himself specified that those three (wajib, tark al-haram, tark al-makruh tahriman) are obligatory upon her (mimma yajibu alayha), not merely permissible. This demonstrates the Hanafi usul principle that terms like 'jawaz' must be interpreted according to context, and a general statement may have specific exceptions that the author clarifies elsewhere.

Key Terms
جواز (jawaz): Permissibility; in this passage, it is analyzed as having two meanings: imkan khas (both sides permissible) and imkan 'amm (at least one side permissible).
امکان خاص (imkan khas): Special possibility: an action is neither necessary nor impossible in both existence and non-existence; both sides are equally possible.
امکان عام (imkan 'amm): General possibility: an action is not impossible in one of its aspects; it may be necessary but the opposite is not necessary.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the fourth probability, what does 'jawaz' mean according to the final solution?
A) Imkan 'amm (general possibility)
B) Imkan khas (special possibility)
C) Both meanings simultaneously
D) Neither meaning

Key Takeaway
The term 'jawaz' in Hanafi usul encompasses two distinct types of possibility (imkan khas and imkan 'amm), and its precise meaning is determined by the legal context—specifically the term it is contrasted with.

Interpretation of 'min adillatiha' in the definition of fiqh
Grammatical and usul analysis of a definition

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the grammatical attachment of 'min adillatiha' in the definition of fiqh and explain why it must modify 'ilm' rather than 'ahkam' using Hanafi usul analysis.

Core Idea
The phrase 'min adillatiha' modifies 'ilm' (knowledge), not 'ahkam' (rulings), to exclude the muqallid's knowledge from true fiqh.

We often recite the definition of fiqh: 'al-ilm bi al-ahkam al-shar'iyyah al-amaliyyah min adillatiha al-tafseeliyyah'. But what exactly does 'min adillatiha' describe? Does it describe the rulings (ahkam) or the knowledge (ilm)? This seemingly grammatical nuance has profound implications: it determines whether a muqallid's knowledge qualifies as fiqh. In this lesson, we will unpack the author's clarification and the reasoning behind it.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two possible grammatical attachments
The prepositional phrase 'min adillatiha' can attach either to 'al-hasila' (describing ahkam) or 'al-hasil' (describing ilm). Recognize both possibilities.
This is a syntactic ambiguity in the definition.
2
Understand the consequence of each attachment
If attached to ahkam, the definition would include the knowledge of a muqallid (blind follower) because his knowledge relates to those rulings. If attached to ilm, it would exclude him.
3
Analyze the first reason: exclusion of muqallid's knowledge
The purpose of 'min adillatiha' is to exclude taqlid. Attaching it to ahkam fails this purpose; attaching it to ilm successfully excludes the muqallid.
First reason: 'wa hinayidhin la yakhruju 'ilm al-muqallid'
4
Analyze the second reason: evidence yields knowledge, not the thing itself
From evidence, we gain knowledge of a thing, not the thing's existence. For example, smoke gives knowledge of fire, not fire itself. This natural relationship favors attaching 'min adillatiha' to knowledge.
Second reason: 'fa al-hasil min al-dalil huwa al-ilm bi al-shay''
5
Synthesize the Hanafi position
The definition of fiqh requires knowledge derived directly from detailed evidences. The muqallid's knowledge, being derivative, is not fiqh. The clarification 'ay al-ilm al-hasil' ensures this exclusion.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله من ادلتها اى العلم الحاصل من ادلتها مصنف نے متن میں من ادلتھا کے بعد ای العلم الحاصل کا لفظ مقدر نکالا ہے شارح اس کی وجہ بیان کر رہے ہیں کہ اس سے مصنف ایک تو ہم کو دفع کرنا چاہتے ہیں تفصیل تو ہم کچھ اس طرح ہے کہ من ادلتها التفصیلیة جار مجرور میں دو احتمال ہیں (۱) جار مجرور الحاصلة کے متعلق ہو کر الا حکام کی صفت ہو (۲) جار مجرور الحاصل کے متعلق ہو کر اعلم کی صفت ہوای العلم الحاصل من اول تھا التفصیلیہ بعض حضرات کو یہ تو ھم ہوا کہ جار مجرور الحاصلة کے متعلق ہو کر الا حکام کی صفت ہیں مصنف نے ای العلم الحاصل سے اسی تو ہم کا ازالہ کیا ہے کہ جار مجرور العلم کے متعلق ہے نہ کہ بالا حکام کے اس کی تین وجہ بیان کیں (۱) وحينئذ لا يخرج علم المقلد سے وجہ اول کا بیان ہے جس کی تقریر اس طرح ہے کہ من ادلتھا سے مقصود علم مقلد کو خارج کرنا ہے اگر یہ احکام سے متعلق ہوں تو پھر علم مقلد خارج نہیں ہوگا کیونکہ اس صورت میں مطلب یہ ہو گا فقہ اس علم کا نام ہے جو ایسے احکام کے ساتھ متعلق ہوتا ہے جو احکام شرعیہ عملیہ ہوں ایسے احکام جو ہو ادلہ تفصیلیہ سے حاصل ہونے والے ہوں تو مقلد کا علم بھی ان احکام کے ساتھ تعلق پکڑتا ہے جو احکام دلائل تفصیلیہ سے حاصل ہوتے ہیں اگر چہ خود علم دلائل تفصیلیہ سے حاصل نہیں ہوتا تو اس صورت میں علم مقلد من ادل تھا سے خارج نہ ہو گا (۲) فالحاصل من الدلیل ھو العلم بالشئی سے دوسری دلیل بیان کی کہ دلیل سے ذات شئی اور نفس شئی حاصل نہیں ہوتی بلکہ دلیل سے کسی شئی کا علم حاصل ہوتا ہے شئی کی ذات اور وجود تو دلیل سے پہلے حاصل ہوتا ہے مثلا دخان دلیل ہے لعلم النار اس سے علم بالنار حاصل ہوانہ کہ وجود نار اسی طرح ایک طالب علم مدرسہ سے چلا گیا ہے کسی نے خبر دی دوسرے نے دلیل دی کہ میں نے اس کو اڈہ پر بس میں سوار ہوتے ہوئے دیکھا تو یہ دلیل ہے اس سے نفس کم ثابت نہیں ہوا بلکہ علم بحکم ثابت ہوا ہے نفس حکم تو پہلے سے موجود تھا اگر وہ خبر نہ دیتا تو حکم تب بھی ثابت ہوتا البتہ ہمیں اس کا علم نہ ہوتا اگر من اول تھا کو الا حکام کے متعلق کریں تو
His statement: 'min adillatiha', i.e., the knowledge derived from its evidences. The author (musannif) in the text, after 'min adillatiha', assumed the word 'ay al-ilm al-hasil' (i.e., the knowledge obtained). The commentator (Taftazani) is explaining the reason for this. That is, by this the author wants to repel a certain doubt. The detail is as follows: In 'min adillatiha al-tafseeliyya', the prepositional phrase has two possibilities: (1) It could be related to 'al-hasila' and be an adjective of 'al-ahkam' (the rulings). (2) It could be related to 'al-hasil' and be an adjective of 'al-ilm' (the knowledge). Some people had the doubt that the prepositional phrase is related to 'al-hasila' and is an adjective of 'al-ahkam'. The author, by saying 'ay al-ilm al-hasil', removed this doubt, showing that the prepositional phrase is related to 'al-ilm' and not to 'al-ahkam'. He gave three reasons: (1) 'Wa hinayidhin la yakhruju 'ilm al-muqallid' — This is the statement of the first reason. Its explanation is: The purpose of 'min adillatiha' is to exclude the knowledge of the muqallid (blind follower). If it were related to the rulings, then the knowledge of the muqallid would not be excluded, because in that case the meaning would be: Fiqh is the name of that knowledge which is related to those rulings that are shar'i amali rulings, and those rulings are obtained from detailed evidences. The muqallid's knowledge is also related to those rulings which are obtained from detailed evidences, even if his knowledge itself is not obtained from detailed evidences. So in that case, the muqallid's knowledge would not be excluded by 'min adillatiha'. (2) 'Fa al-hasil min al-dalil huwa al-ilm bi al-shay'' — The second reason: From the evidence, what is obtained is knowledge of the thing, not the essence of the thing. The essence and existence of the thing are obtained before the evidence. For example, smoke is evidence for knowledge of fire; it provides knowledge of fire, not the existence of fire. Similarly, a student has left the school; someone gave news, another gave evidence saying 'I saw him boarding a bus at the stop' — this is evidence; from it the essence of the ruling is not established, rather knowledge of the ruling is established. The essence of the ruling existed before. If he had not given the news, the ruling would still be established, but we would not have knowledge of it. If we relate 'min adillatiha' to the rulings, then... (text incomplete)
Source pages p. 160

Commentary
This passage is a textual analysis of the definition of fiqh: 'al-ilm bi al-ahkam al-shar'iyyah al-amaliyyah min adillatiha al-tafseeliyyah'. The author adds 'ay al-ilm al-hasil min adillatiha' to clarify that 'min adillatiha' modifies 'ilm' (knowledge) not 'ahkam' (rulings). The commentator gives reasons: (1) To exclude the muqallid's knowledge; if it modified rulings, a muqallid's knowledge would still be related to those rulings and thus included. (2) Evidence gives knowledge of a thing, not the thing itself, so the phrase naturally relates to knowledge. This shows the Hanafi insistence on precise definition to exclude ijtihad and taqlid distinctions.

Key Terms
علم المقلد (ilm al-muqallid): Knowledge of a lay follower who does not derive rulings from evidences himself
من أدلتها التفصيلية (min adillatiha al-tafseeliyya): From its detailed evidences; specifies that the knowledge comes directly from the detailed proofs

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the primary purpose of clarifying that 'min adillatiha' relates to 'ilm' rather than 'ahkam' in the definition of fiqh?
A) To include all types of knowledge under fiqh
B) To exclude the muqallid's knowledge from the definition of fiqh
C) To emphasize the importance of the texts (nusus)
D) To show that the rulings themselves are derived from evidences

Key Takeaway
The phrase 'min adillatiha' in the definition of fiqh must modify 'ilm' (knowledge) to exclude the knowledge of a muqallid, as per Hanafi usul analysis.

Amm and Khass — General and Specific
advanced
8 min
Taftazani’s First Two Objections on ‘Aw al-Wad‘’
Taftazani's objections and Hanafi responses on 'aw al-wad''

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi distinction between taklifi and wad'i rulings to evaluate whether the phrase 'aw al-wad'' must be explicit in the definition of hukm.

Core Idea
Taklifi and wad'i rulings are disparate (mutabayin), not just different—they cannot be merged implicitly.

In this passage, we step into a classic usul debate: should the definition of hukm include the phrase 'or wad'i' to cover declaratory rulings? Taftazani raises two objections against the Hanafi author’s insistence on adding that phrase. Understanding the responses reveals a key Hanafi methodological stance: taklifi and wad'i rulings are fundamentally different in nature, not merely conceptually distinct. This lesson unpacks the logic and stakes of the debate.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core dispute
Read the passage to pinpoint what is being contested: whether the definition of hukm requires the explicit phrase 'aw al-wad'' to include wad'i (declaratory) rulings, or whether they can be implicitly covered under 'iqtidha'.
The dispute is not about the existence of wad'i rulings, but about their inclusion in the definition.
2
Analyze Taftazani’s first objection
Notice that Taftazani argues: if the opponent (Ash'ari) does not consider wad'i as hukm at all, adding the phrase is pointless. The Hanafi response clarifies that the author only addressed those who accept wad'i as hukm—the objection misses the target.
This response uses the category of the interlocutor to defuse the objection.
3
Analyze Taftazani’s second objection
Even if wad'i is accepted, Taftazani claims it can be included implicitly under iqtidha. The Hanafi response gives two reasons: (a) separate mention is better due to conceptual difference, and (b) the two are actually disparate (mutabayin), not just different, with distinct necessary concomitants (lazim).
The stronger argument is the second: disparity in lazim implies disparity in malzum.
4
Understand the Hanafi conceptual framework
Grasp why the Hanafi school sees taklifi and wad'i as disparate: taklifi rulings impose obligation or prohibition on an act, while wad'i rulings establish a relationship (e.g., causality, condition). Their necessary effects are different, so they cannot be merged under one generic term like iqtidha.
This is a classic application of the principle 'tabayun al-lawazim yadullu 'ala tabayun al-malzumat'.
5
Apply to a similar usul dispute
Consider another context where a category might be implicitly included but is disputed. Ask: does implicit inclusion work, or does the fundamental nature demand explicit mention? Trace the reasoning pattern from this lesson.
This trains the skill of transferring the logic to other usul issues.

Source Text

وانت خبير بانه لا توجيه لهذا المقام اصلا شارح تفتازانی مصنف پر اشکال کر رہے ہیں کہ مصنف کی کلام ( کہ اوالوضع کی قید کا اضافہ ہونا چاہیئے ) غلط محض ہے اس کی اصلاح کی کوئی توجیہ نہیں ہو سکتی اما اولا سے پہلا اشکال ہے کہ مصنف کا یہ کہنا کہ حق یہ ہے او الوضع کی قید کا اضافہ ہونا چاہئے تا که حکم وضعی تعریف حکم میں داخل ہو جائے درست نہیں ہے کیونکہ خصم (اشاعرہ) خطاب وضعی کو حکم تسلیم کرنے کیلئے تیا رہی نہیں ہے اور وہ صرف خطاب تکلیفی ہی کو حکم کہتے ہیں تو اس کیلئے تعریف حکم میں او الوضع کی قید لگانا کیسے واجب اور کیسے صحیح ہے بلکہ اس کیلئے تو اوالوضع کی قید نہ لگانا واجب ہے تا کہ یہ باہر ہی رہے اور تعریف حکم مانع عن دخول الغیر ہو ۔ جواب ! شارح تفتازانی کے اعتراض کا جواب یہ دیا گیا ہے کہ جناب عالی ہم ابھی وضاحت کر چکے ہیں کہ اشاعرہ کے تین گروہ ہیں (۱) گروہ وہ ہے جو ۔ خطاب وضعی کو حکم تسلیم نہیں کرتا اس گروہ کا مصنف نے اپنے متن میں سرے سے ذکر ہی نہیں کیا نہ ان پر رد کیا ہے نہ ان کی کلام کی توجیہ کی ہے مصنف نے تو ان دو گروہ کے بارے میں اپنی رائے پیش کی ہے جو خطاب وضعی کو حکم تسلیم کرتے ہیں پھر ایک گروہ او الوضع کی قید لگاتا ہے دوسرا اس کی ضرورت محسوس نہیں کرتا بلکہ اس کو اقتضا ضمنی میں داخل کرتا ہے مصنف نے ان کے بارے میں فرمایا کہ حق یہ ہے کہ او الوضع کی قید لگائی جائے الاقتضاء میں داخل نہ کیا جائے باقی جو خصم اس کو حکم تسلیم نہیں کرتا مصنف نے ان کے بارے میں کوئی بات ہی نہیں کی لہذا شارح کا اعتراض کہ مخصم تسلیم ہی نہیں کرتا لغو اور باطل ہے ۔ واما ثانیا دوسرا اشکال یہ ہے کہ اگر شخصم (اشاعرہ ) اس خطاب وضعی کو حکم مان بھی لے تب بھی وہ ادا لوضع کی قید کی ضرورت محسوس نہیں کرتا بلکہ وہ کہتا ہے کہ یہ حکم کی تعریف میں ( بلا قید اوالوضع ) داخل ہے اس سے خارج نہیں ہے کیونکہ وہ خطاب تکلیفی کی تعریف میں الاقتضاء کو اہم قرار دیتا ہے وہ خطاب تکلیفی اور وضعی دونوں کو شامل ہو جائے گا لہذا تب بھی او الوضع کی قید لگانا ضروری نہیں ہے باقی رہی مصنف کی یہ وجہ کہ یہ دونوں متغایر المفہوم ہیں لہذا دونوں کو علیحدہ ذکر کرنا چاہئے ، تو اس کا جواب یہ ہے کہ ہم تسلیم کرتے ہیں کہ دونوں مفہوم کے اعتبار سے متغایر ہیں کیونکہ حکم تکلیفی عام اور حکم وضعی خاص ہے اور عام خاص آپس میں متغایرہ ہوتے ہیں۔ لیکن ان کے متغایر المفہوم ہونے سے یہ لازم نہیں آتا کہ ان دونوں کو مستقل علیحدہ علیحدہ ذکر کیا جائے بلکہ یہ ایک دوسرے میں داخل ہو سکتے ہیں اس طرح کہ اقتضاء کو عام قرار دیا جائے وہ صریح اور ضمنی دونوں کو شامل ہو تو ان دونوں کا متغایر المنہو م ہونا ان کے تداخل میں مصر نہیں ہے جس طرح عام خاص متغاير المفہوم ہیں لیکن خاص عام میں داخل ہوتا ہے لہذا مصنف کا یہ قول کہ حق یہ ہے کہ او الوضع کی قید کا اضافہ کیا جائے ناحق ہے ۔ جواب ! مصنف کے قول کا مقصد یہ ہے کہ اگر چہ خطاب تکلیفی عام ہے خطاب وضعی کو بھی شامل ہے لیکن اولی یہ ہے کہ خطاب وضعی کو مستقلا ذکر کیا جائے کیونکہ دونوں مفہوم کے اعتبار سے متغایر ہیں، نیز جواب دوم) مقصود مصنف خطاب تکلیفی اور خطاب وضعی کے صرف مفہوم اور معنی کے تغایر کو بیان کرنا نہیں ہے بلکہ مصنف ان دونوں کے تباین کو ثابت کرنا چاہتا ہے کہ یہ دونوں نوعان متباینان اور دو حقیقتان مختلفتان ہیں کیونکہ حکم وضعی کا لازم تعلق کئی بشئی آخر ہے اور کم تکلیفی کا یہ لازم نہیں ہے بلکہ اس کو وجوب فعل یا تحریم فعل لازم ہے اور یہ دونوں لازم آپس میں متغایر اور متباین ہیں اور تباین اللوازم دلالت کرتا ہے تباہین ملزومات پر اس سے ثابت ہوا کہ یفی اور خطاب وضعی متباینان ہیں اور ایک متباین کو دوسرے متبائین میں داخل کرنا درست نہیں تو شارح کا یہ کہنا فائی ضرر فی مفهومیھما غلط ہے کیونکہ ان میں تغایر مفہوم نہیں ہے بلکہ تباین مفہوم ہے اور یہ کہنا بھی درست نہیں فكيف يتحد مفهوم العام والخاص کیونکہ ان میں عموم خصوص نہیں بلکہ نسبت تباہین ہے ( کما عرفت انفا) على ان المفهوم
And you know that there is no justification for this matter at all. The commentator Taftazani is objecting to the author that the author's statement (that the addition of the phrase 'or wad'i' is necessary) is completely wrong and cannot be rectified. Firstly, the first objection: The author's saying that the right thing is to add the phrase 'or wad'i' so that the wad'i ruling enters the definition of hukm is incorrect, because the opponent (Ash'ari) is not ready to accept khitab wad'i as hukm; they only call khitab taklifi hukm. So for them, how is it necessary and correct to add 'or wad'i'? Rather, it is necessary not to add it so that it remains outside and the definition of hukm prevents the entry of what is not part of it. Response: The response given to Taftazani's objection is that we have already clarified that there are three Ash'ari groups: (1) a group that does not accept khitab wad'i as hukm. The author did not mention this group at all in his text, nor did he refute it or interpret their statement. The author only presented his view regarding the two groups that accept khitab wad'i as hukm. One group adds 'or wad'i', and the other does not feel its need but includes it in implicit iqtidha. The author said about them that the right thing is to add 'or wad'i' and not to include it in iqtidha. As for the opponent who does not accept it as hukm, the author did not speak about them at all. Therefore, the commentator's objection that the opponent does not accept it is frivolous and invalid. Secondly, the second objection: If the opponent (Ash'ari) accepts this khitab wad'i as hukm, even then he does not feel the need for the phrase 'or wad'i', but says that it is included in the definition of hukm (without the phrase 'or wad'i') and is not excluded, because he makes iqtidha fundamental in the definition of khitab taklifi, which will include both khitab taklifi and wad'i. Hence, even then adding 'or wad'i' is not necessary. As for the author's reason that the two are mutually different in concept, so they should be mentioned separately, the response to that is: We agree that they are different in concept because hukm taklifi is general and hukm wad'i is specific, and general and specific are mutually different. But their being different in concept does not necessitate that they be mentioned separately independently; rather, they can be included in each other in such a way that iqtidha is made general, including both explicit and implicit. Then their being different in concept does not prevent their inter-inclusion, just as general and specific are different in concept but the specific is included in the general. Therefore, the author's statement that the right thing is to add 'or wad'i' is unjust. Response: The purpose of the author's statement is that even though khitab taklifi is general and includes khitab wad'i, it is better to mention khitab wad'i independently because the two are different in concept. Also, second response: The author's aim is not just to state the difference in concept and meaning between khitab taklifi and khitab wad'i; rather, the author wants to prove that the two are disparate, i.e., they are two different types and two different realities. Because the necessary concomitant (lazim) of hukm wad'i is the relation of one thing to another thing, while that of hukm taklifi is not this; rather, its necessary concomitant is the obligation or prohibition of an act. These two concomitants are mutually different and disparate. The disparity of concomitants indicates the disparity of the subjects (malzumat). From this it is proven that this and khitab wad'i are disparate, and it is not correct to include one disparate thing in another. So the commentator's saying 'no harm in their conceptual difference' is wrong, because there is not just a difference in concept but a disparity in concept. And his saying 'how can the concept of general and specific be unified' is also incorrect, because here there is not a general-specific relationship but a disparity relation (as you have known above). Upon this understanding...
Source pages p. 175

Commentary
This passage covers Taftazani's first two objections regarding the necessity of including the phrase 'aw al-wad'' (or wad'i) in the definition of hukm, and the responses defending the author. The first objection argues that since the Ash'ari opponent does not consider wad'i a ruling, adding the phrase is pointless. The response clarifies that the author addressed only those groups that accept wad'i as a ruling, not those who reject it entirely, so the objection is irrelevant. The second objection argues that even if wad'i is accepted, it can be included implicitly without the phrase. The response gives two points: first, it is better to mention it separately due to conceptual difference; second, the two are not merely different but disparate (mutabayin), with different necessary concomitants (lazim), so they cannot be subsumed under one definition. The response emphasizes that hukm taklifi obligates or prohibits an act, while hukm wad'i establishes a relationship between things, making them fundamentally distinct. Therefore, including wad'i under iqtidha is logically unsound.

Key Terms
او الوضع (aw al-wad'): The phrase 'or wad'i' added to the definition of hukm to include declaratory rulings.
اقتضاء (iqtidha'): Requirement or implication; a broad term that can cover both explicit and implicit rulings.
تباین (tabayun): Disparity; two categories that are mutually exclusive and cannot be subsumed under a common genus.
لازم (lazim): Necessary concomitant; an essential property that necessarily accompanies a thing.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the main Hanafi reason for insisting on adding 'aw al-wad'' to the definition of hukm?
A) The Ash'ari opponent does not accept wad'i as hukm.
B) Taklifi and wad'i are disparate (mutabayin) with different necessary concomitants.
C) Iqtidha cannot cover wad'i at all.
D) The definition must be as concise as possible.

Key Takeaway
Taklifi and wad'i rulings are fundamentally disparate in their necessary concomitants, hence the definition of hukm must explicitly include 'aw al-wad''.

The Role of 'Amaliyya in the Definition of Hukm Sharʿī
Refining the definition of ḥukm sharʿī by addressing an objection

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the qualification 'amaliyya is necessary in the definition of ḥukm sharʿī and identify which rulings are excluded by it, using the refined analysis of al-Taftazani.

Core Idea
'Amaliyya excludes non-practical rulings, even those with iqtiḍāʾ, preserving the definition's precision.

The definition of ḥukm sharʿī includes both iqtiḍāʾ (implication) and 'amaliyya (practical). An objection arose: rulings like 'the proof value of ijmāʿ' seem to involve iqtiḍāʾ (they imply an obligation to act), so they would fall under iqtiḍāʾ but then be excluded by 'amaliyya, making 'amaliyya redundant. Al-Taftazani critically examines this objection and provides a refined answer, showing how both qualifications remain necessary and clarifying their distinct roles.

Guided Path
1
State the objection
The objection claimed that rulings such as 'kawn al-ijmāʿ ḥujjah' (the proof value of ijmāʿ) involve iqtiḍāʾ because they imply an obligation to act upon their implication. If so, they would be included under the iqtiḍāʾ condition but then excluded by the 'amaliyya condition, rendering 'amaliyya superfluous.
Understand the challenge: the definition's conditions together seem to over-exclude.
2
Examine the initial answer and its refutation
Some scholars responded that such rulings indeed have iqtiḍāʾ (implied) and therefore are excluded by 'amaliyya. Al-Taftazani refutes this: if these rulings have iqtiḍāʾ that implies obligation to act, then they are practical in nature (since the obligation relates to action), so 'amaliyya would not exclude them. Thus the initial answer fails.
The flaw: confusing the presence of iqtiḍāʾ with the ruling's own practical quality.
3
Follow al-Taftazani's refined answer
Al-Taftazani distinguishes: Rulings like 'kawn al-ijmāʿ ḥujjah' have no iqtiḍāʾ at all (they simply state a fact), so they are excluded by the iqtiḍāʾ condition, not by 'amaliyya. Rulings like 'ijmāʿ is permissible' or 'qiyās is obligatory' do have iqtiḍāʾ (they imply an obligation), but they are not related to a specific action (they are meta-rulings about the sources), so they are excluded by 'amaliyya. Thus both qualifications serve distinct purposes.
This shows the layered nature of the definition: iqtiḍāʾ excludes non-implicative rulings; 'amaliyya excludes non-practical implicative rulings.
4
Evaluate the necessity of both qualifications
The refined answer demonstrates that 'amaliyya is not superfluous; it uniquely excludes rulings that have iqtiḍāʾ but do not relate to action. Without 'amaliyya, the definition would incorrectly include meta-rulings. Thus the definition is precisely tailored to identify only practical sharʿī rulings (the subject of fiqh).
This reinforces the Hanafi commitment to precision in uṣūl definitions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
یقال سے غرض شارح یہ ہے کہ بعض حضرات نے اعتراض سابق کا ایک جواب دیا تھا اس جواب کو ذکر کر رہے ہیں بعض حضرات نے یہ جواب دیا ہے کون الاجماع حجہ کون السنتہ حجتہ یہ بالاقتضاء میں داخل ہیں کیونکہ ان میں اقتضا میمنی ہے ان کے حجت ہونے کا مطلب یہ ہے کہ ان کے مقتضاء پر عمل کرنا واجب ہے پھر یہ العملیہ کی قید سے خارج ہو جائیں گے تو قید عملیہ فضول نہیں ہوگی ان میں ضمنا اقتضاء ہے شارح تفتازانی رد کر رہے ہیں کہ اگر ان کا یہی مطلب ہے کہ ان کے اقتضاء پر عمل واجب ہے اور اقتضاء ضمنی میں داخل ہے تو پھر یہ العملیہ کی قید سے بھی خارج نہیں ہو نگے کیونکہ ان کے مقتضاء پر عمل کرنے کا مطلب یہی ہے کہ اعمال کے قبیلہ میں سے ہیں اسلئے یہ اعملیہ کی قید سے خارج نہ ہونگے حالانکہ آپ نے العملیہ کی قیدا نہی کو خارج کرنے کیلئے لگائی تھي فيمكن ان یقال سے شارح تفتازانی خود جواب دے رہے ہیں کہ کون الا جماع حجتہ ، کون القیاس مجتہ، اس جیسے احکام تو الاقتضاء سے خارج ہو جائیں گے کیونکہ ان میں اقتضاء نہیں ہے البتہ جواز اجماع اور وجوب قیاس یہ بالاقتضاء کی قید سے خارج نہیں ہونگے کیونکہ ان میں اقتضاء ہے اور یہ حکم شرعی ہے لیکن چونکہ یہ کیفیت عمل سے متعلق نہیں ہیں لہذا العملیہ کی قید سے خارج ہو جائیں گے اس طرح العملیہ کی قید کا فضول اور بے کار ہونا لازم نہیں آئے گا اور یہ قید مفید بن جائے گی واللہ اعلم
اقتضاء اور طلب نہیں ہے لہذا العملیہ کی قید سے کوئی چیز بھی خارج نہیں ہو رہی اس لیے یہ قید ہر حال میں مکران فض
The Shaykh says: The purpose of the commentator is that some people gave one answer to the previous objection; he is mentioning that answer. Some people gave this answer: 'Kawn al-ijmāʿ ḥujjah, kawn al-sunnah ḥujjah' – these are included in iqtidā' because there is iqtidā' in them. The meaning of their being proofs is that acting upon their muqtaḍā is obligatory. Then they will be excluded by the qualification 'amaliyya, so the qualification 'amaliyya would not be superfluous. There is implied iqtidā' in them. The commentator al-Taftazani refutes: If their meaning is that acting upon their muqtaḍā is obligatory and it is included in implied iqtidā', then they will not be excluded by the qualification 'amaliyya because the meaning of acting upon their muqtaḍā is that they are among acts. Therefore they will not be excluded by the qualification 'amaliyya, although you placed the qualification 'amaliyya to exclude them. Then 'fayumkin an yuqāl': the commentator al-Taftazani himself answers: Rulings like 'kawn al-ijmāʿ ḥujjah, kawn al-qiyās mujtahad' will be excluded from iqtidā' because there is no iqtidā' in them. But the permissibility of ijmāʿ and obligation of qiyās will not be excluded by the qualification of iqtidā' because there is iqtidā' in them, and they are sharʿī rulings. However, since they are not related to the quality of action, they will be excluded by the qualification 'amaliyya. Thus the qualification 'amaliyya is not pointless and becomes beneficial. And Allah knows best. Then: There is no iqtidā' and no demand, so nothing is excluded by the qualification 'amaliyya; therefore this qualification is in any case repeated [corrupt].
Source pages p. 187

Commentary
This passage is a super-commentary (hāshiya) by al-Taftazani on the sharḥ of al-Tawḍīḥ. It addresses an objection concerning the inclusion of rulings like 'the proof value of ijmāʿ' and 'the proof value of the sunnah' under the category of iqtidā' (implication) in the definition of ḥukm sharʿī. Some argued that these rulings involve iqtidā' (they imply obligation to act), so they would be excluded by the qualification 'amaliyya (practical), making that qualification unnecessary. Al-Taftazani refutes: even if they imply action, they are still practical (they relate to acts), so they would not be excluded by 'amaliyya. Then he provides a better answer: rulings like 'ijmāʿ is proof' have no iqtidā' at all, so they are excluded by the iqtidā' qualification. But rulings like 'ijmāʿ is permissible' and 'qiyās is obligatory' do have iqtidā' (they imply an obligation), but they are not related to the quality of action, so they are excluded by the 'amaliyya qualification. Thus both qualifications are necessary. The final corrupt line seems to be a criticism: if there is no iqtidā' and no demand, then nothing is excluded by 'amaliyya, making it redundant. This likely continues the debate but is unclear due to OCR corruption.

Key Terms
الاقتضاء (al-iqtiḍāʾ): Implication; the inherent implication of a command or prohibition in a sharʿī text
العملیہ (al-ʿamaliyya): The qualification that a ruling relates to an action (practical); used to exclude non-practical sharʿī rulings from the definition of ḥukm

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to al-Taftazani's refined answer, which type of ruling is excluded by the qualification 'amaliyya?
A) Rulings that contain no iqtiḍāʾ at all.
B) Rulings that have iqtiḍāʾ but are not related to a specific action.
C) Rulings that are practical and involve iqtiḍāʾ.
D) Rulings that are about the sunnah.

Key Takeaway
The qualification 'amaliyya is not superfluous; it uniquely excludes non-practical sharʿī rulings that nonetheless involve iqtiḍāʾ, ensuring the definition of ḥukm sharʿī captures only practical rulings (the subject of fiqh).

Interpreting 'Ahkam' in the Definition of Faqih: Refutation and Linguistic Analysis
Refuting interpretations of 'ahkam' and addressing Taftazani's objection

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the four refuted interpretations of 'ahkam' in the definition of faqih and evaluate Taftazani's argument that kulli ijmali and kulli fardi are mutually entailing in this context.

Core Idea
Knowledge of all rulings is impossible; a faqih must know the method, not every individual ruling.

This passage from the Sharh al-Tawdih wa al-Talwih delves into the precise meaning of 'ahkam' (rulings) in the definition of a faqih. The author refutes four possible interpretations—collective, individual, partial, and absolute—showing that each leads to absurdity. Then, addressing an objection by Taftazani, the commentator explains the relationship between 'collective all' and 'individual all' in the context of knowledge, demonstrating the sophistication of Hanafi usul al-fiqh. By the end, you will understand why the definition requires a nuanced understanding of the term 'ahkam'.

Guided Path
1
Identify the four probabilities for 'ahkam'
List the four possible meanings that could be assigned to the plural 'ahkam' in the definition: (1) all rulings collectively, (2) each individual ruling, (3) a specific portion (e.g., half, third), (4) some indefinite rulings (ba'd mutlaq). Also note the fifth implied possibility: 'most rulings'.
The author treats each as a separate probability requiring refutation.
2
Understand the refutation of each probability
For each probability, grasp the key argument: (1) Collective all is impossible because rulings are infinite and cannot be encompassed. (2) Individual all is false because even great imams like Malik and Abu Hanifa did not know every ruling. (3) Portions are unknown because the whole is unknown. (4) Indefinite part would make any knower of a single ruling a faqih, which is absurd.
The refutation of (3) also covers the idea of 'most rulings'.
3
Analyze the linguistic distinction of 'kull'
The passage introduces a threefold division of the word 'kull': (a) collective meaning only (e.g., 'all the people lift this stone'), (b) distributive meaning only (e.g., 'each person is sufficed by this food'), (c) both equally (e.g., 'I struck all the people'). Understand how context determines which meaning applies.
This linguistic taxonomy is crucial for Taftazani's objection.
4
Apply to Taftazani's objection
Taftazani argues that 'knowledge of rulings' falls under type (c)—collective and individual are mutually entailing. Therefore, the author's separate refutation of kulli ijmali and kulli fardi is redundant. The commentator defends the author by noting that the separate treatment is precautionary and covers all possibilities, even if in this context they happen to coincide.
The thamarah al-khilaf: if identical, one refutation suffices; if not, both must be addressed.
5
Evaluate the thamarah al-khilaf
Determine the practical consequence of this disagreement: should a faqih treat collective and individual as distinct or identical when analyzing definitions? The commentator favors the author's comprehensive method, while acknowledging Taftazani's logical point.
This illustrates the Hanafi principle of 'ihtiyat' (caution) in usul.

افتراقی دوم کل افرادی ہو مجموعی نہ ہو مثال كل واحد من الناس يكفيه هذا الطعام اب اشکال یہ ہے کہ یہاں حوا علم بالا حکام میں علم سے مراد معرفت ہے اور اس کا حکم لگایا جارہا ہے احکام پر اور یہ حکم کی تین جب ہر ہر فرد کی معرفت ہو اسی طرح اگر احکام کی معرفت سے ہر ہر حکم کی معرفت مراد لی جائے تو جب ہر ہر فرد کی معرفت حاصل ہو جائے گی تو کل کی بھی ہو جائے گی تو یہاں کل مجموعی اور کل افرادی ایک دوسرے کو لازم ملزوم ہیں ان میں کوئی تباین و تقابل نہیں ہے لہذا ان کو علیحدہ ذکر کر کے ہر ایک کا ابطال اور اس پر مستقل دلیل قائم کرنے کی کوئی ضرورت نہیں
The rulings are infinite. Knowledge of all rulings collectively is not within the capacity of any faqih or mujtahid, because there are only two ways to know them: either knowing each individual ruling and each particular—this is impossible because encompassing the infinite is impossible for a human—or knowing them in a universal way such that there is a universal that encompasses all rulings and from knowledge of that universal comes knowledge of all rulings. This is also impossible because rulings differ due to varying incidents, so no universal can be formed that gathers all rulings. Since both possibilities are false, it is established that knowledge of all rulings collectively is not possible, so the first probability is false. As for the second: the second probability was that each individual ruling is intended. This is false because many imams and mujtahids whose being faqih is agreed upon did not know every ruling. For example, Imam Malik was asked about forty issues and said 'I do not know' for thirty-six. Similarly, Imam Abu Hanifa was asked about a [؟] and said 'I do not know.' If all individuals were intended, then these imams would have to be non-fuqaha, but their being fuqaha is agreed upon. As for the third: the third probability was that some specific rulings are intended. This is false because when all rulings are infinite and unknown in quantity and magnitude, then their fractions (half, third, etc.) which are added and attributed to the whole are also unknown, because ignorance of the whole entails ignorance of the fractions. Hence this probability is also false. And by this it becomes clear: he said that from the preceding discussion it also becomes clear that 'most rulings' cannot be intended, because they are also unknown. Since 'most' refers to more than half, and half is unknown, then more than half is also unknown, so this cannot be intended. As for the fourth: the fourth probability was that some absolute rulings (ba'd mutlaq) are intended. This is false because in that case, a non-faqih would have to be called a faqih, since 'some absolute' includes even one or two issues, then whoever knows one or two issues by evidence would have to be called a faqih, which is false. And this is mentioned earlier. With this phrase, the commentator is removing a doubt. The doubt is: why did the author not mention the fourth probability (i.e., some absolute rulings) in the text and explain its invalidity? The commentator answers that the author already invalidated this probability in the preceding discussion when refuting Imam al-Razi, saying 'because the intended meaning of rulings is not some of them, even if few.' Therefore, he did not mention it explicitly but indicated it with 'thumma i'lam', meaning: after it is established that some absolute rulings cannot be intended, then know also that all rulings cannot be intended, etc. And here there is a discussion: that among the rulings... The intention of the commentator Taftazani is to object to the author. The objection is that the author said in the text that 'from al-ahkam, the collective all (kulli ijmali) cannot be intended, nor can the individual all (kulli fardi).' The author's mentioning these two separately and then invalidating each with independent evidence is not correct, because although collective all and individual all have a mutual opposition in themselves, in this context there is no opposition between them. The ruling that applies to collective all is exactly the same as the ruling for individual all, and invalidating one is exactly invalidating the other. So the author's mentioning them as opposites is not correct. The detailed explanation is: rulings are of three types: (1) Some rulings apply to collective all but not to individual all, like 'all the people lift this stone, not each one.' The lifting ruling applies to the whole group collectively, not to each individual, because the intent is to show that the stone is so heavy that the whole group can lift it together, but not each individual separately. (2) Some rulings do not correctly apply to collective all but apply to individual all, like 'each person individually, this food suffices him.' The sufficiency ruling applies to each individual, not to the collective, because the intent is that each individual separately is sufficed by this food, not the group collectively. (3) Some rulings can apply equally to both collective all and individual all, because there is no difference between them; they are mutually entailing. For example, 'I struck all the people'—the striking ruling applies to the whole group. Here, collective all can be intended, and individual all can also be intended; indeed, they are mutually entailing because striking the whole group entails striking each individual, and striking each individual entails striking the whole group. Therefore, there is no difference here. The summary is that between collective all and individual all there is a relationship of generality and specificity from one aspect (umum wa khusus min wajh). In umum wa khusus min wajh, there is a common matter and two exclusive matters. The common matter is that both collective all and individual all apply, like 'I struck all the people.' The first exclusive matter is that collective all applies but not individual all, like 'all the people lift this stone.' The second exclusive matter is that individual all applies but not collective all, like 'each person individually, this food suffices him.' Now the objection is: here, in 'knowledge of rulings,' knowledge means recognition, and it is being predicated on rulings. This predicate falls under the third type—i.e., here both collective all and individual all can be intended; they are identical and mutually entailing. Because if knowledge of rulings collectively is intended, that can only happen when there is knowledge of each individual ruling. Similarly, if knowledge of rulings individually is intended, when knowledge of each individual is attained, knowledge of the collective is also attained. So here, collective all and individual all are mutually entailing; there is no separation or opposition between them. Therefore, there is no need to mention them separately and to establish independent proofs for invalidating each.
Source pages p. 196

Commentary
This passage is a detailed textual analysis of the term 'ahkam' (rulings) as used in the definition of a faqih. The author (likely Sadr al-Shariah) in the matn of Al-Tawdih had stated that the mujtahid knows all rulings, and then the commentator (the Urdu sharh writer) is clarifying what 'all rulings' means. The passage first refutes four probabilities: (1) all rulings collectively—impossible because infinite; (2) each individual ruling—impossible because even great imams like Imam Malik and Imam Abu Hanifa did not know every ruling; (3) some specific rulings—impossible because fractions of an unknown whole are unknown; (4) some absolute rulings—impossible because it would make anyone who knows one or two rulings a faqih. The commentator then addresses a doubt about why the author did not list the fourth probability separately, explaining that it was already refuted in an earlier discussion against Imam al-Razi. Then he turns to an objection from Taftazani, who argues that the author's separate treatment of 'collective all' (kulli ijmali) and 'individual all' (kulli fardi) is unnecessary because in the context of 'knowledge of rulings,' they are mutually entailing—knowing all rulings collectively requires knowing each individually, and vice versa. The commentator elaborates by analyzing three types of application of 'kull': (1) collective only (e.g., a group lifting a stone), (2) individual only (e.g., each person being sufficed by food), (3) both collectively and individually (e.g., striking a group). He argues that 'knowledge of rulings' falls into the third type, so the collective and individual are identical, making the author's separate refutations redundant. The passage thus demonstrates a sophisticated usul analysis of linguistic usage and logical relationships, a hallmark of Hanafi usul al-fiqh.

Key Terms
کل مجموعی (kulli ijmali): All items considered as a collective whole, not individually.
کل انفرادی (kulli fardi): Each individual item separately.
بعض مطلق (ba'd mutlaq): An indefinite part; some rulings without specification.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the relationship between 'kulli ijmali' and 'kulli fardi' in the context of 'knowledge of rulings'?
A) They are mutually exclusive and require separate refutations.
B) They are identical and mutually entailing, so separate refutation is unnecessary.
C) They are unrelated to the definition of rulings.
D) Only kulli ijmali is valid.

Key Takeaway
The term 'ahkam' in the definition of faqih cannot be interpreted as all rulings collectively or individually; it refers to the method of deriving rulings, and the linguistic scope of 'kull' must be determined by context.

Amm and Khass — General and Specific

7 min
Kull Majmu'i vs Kull Afradi in the Definition of Fiqh
Critical refinement of a usul dispute about the definition of fiqh

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the distinction between kull majmu'i and kull afradi to evaluate objections against the definition of fiqh in Hanafi usul.

Core Idea
In fiqh definition, collective and individual knowledge are interdependent; separate refutation is redundant.

This lesson examines a dispute in Hanafi usul al-fiqh about the object of knowledge in the definition of fiqh. The commentator Misk al-Malih critiques the original text for separately refuting two possibilities (kull majmu'i and kull afradi), arguing they are mutually implicative. We will follow the argument and understand why this refinement is important for the precise formulation of the definition.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two possibilities for the object of knowledge
The definition of fiqh as 'knowledge of the rulings' can be interpreted as knowledge of the collective whole (kull majmu'i) or of each individual ruling (kull afradi). Note that these two readings lead to different objections.
The original text treated them as distinct possibilities needing separate refutation.
2
Understand the tripartite classification of rulings
Rulings can apply to a collective (e.g., lifting a stone), to individuals (e.g., sufficiency of food), or equally to both (e.g., striking the people). Recognize which category 'knowledge of rulings' falls into according to Misk al-Malih.
He places knowledge of rulings in the third category where collective and individual are equivalents.
3
Recognize the interdependence argument
If you know all rulings collectively, you necessarily know each individually, and vice versa. Therefore, refuting one refutes the other; separate treatment is redundant.
This is the core of Misk al-Malih's critique.
4
Evaluate the counter-argument and rebuttal
Some defenders say kull majmu'i is general (includes both 'all' and 'some') while kull afradi is specific to 'each'. Misk al-Malih rebuts that if it includes 'some', the proof of infinite events (used against collective) fails because that proof only works for all rulings.
This shows the counter-argument is self-defeating.
5
Examine Taftazani's and Ibn al-Hajib's resolutions
Taftazani distinguishes by time: kull majmu'i = all rulings past and future, kull afradi = only past rulings. Ibn al-Hajib says 'knowledge' means malakah (faculty), not actual knowledge, avoiding the infinity problem.
These resolutions aim to salvage the original text's approach.

اعتراض کا جواب دیا کہ ہم ان چار احتمالات میں سے اول احتمال مراد لیتے ہیں یعنی مجموع من حیث المجموع احکام مراد لیتے ہیں اور علم سے تھیں اور ملکہ مراد لیتے ہیں مقصد یہ ہوگا کہ فقہ جمیع احکام شرعیہ کے علم کی استعداد اور ملکہ کا نام ہے یہ مراد نہیں ہے کہ بالفعل جمیع احکام کا علم حاصل ہو اور احکام غیر متناہیہ کے جانے کی استعداد اور صلاحیت تو مجتہد کی طاقت سے باہر نہیں ہے ہاں بالفعل ان کا علم ناممکن ہے مصنف
It is not correct that the author mentioned these two [types] separately and independently, and then refuted each with an independent proof, because although there is an opposition in themselves between 'kull majmu'i' and 'kull afradi', in this context there is no opposition: the ruling that applies to 'kull majmu'i' is exactly the same ruling that applies to 'kull afradi', and refuting one is exactly the same as refuting the other. So the author's mentioning them in opposition is not correct. The detailed explanation is as follows: rulings are of three types: (1) Some rulings are such that they apply primarily to the collective whole, not to the individual parts. e.g., 'The whole people lift this stone, not each one' – the ruling of lifting the stone applies to the group as a whole, not to every individual, because the intent is to show that the stone is so heavy that the entire group together can lift it, but each individual alone cannot. (2) Some rulings are such that they do not apply correctly to the collective whole, but apply to the individual parts. e.g., 'Each individual person is sufficient for this food' – the sufficiency of food applies to each individual, not to the whole, because the intent is that each individual alone is satisfied by this food. (3) Some rulings are such that they apply equally to both the collective whole and the individual parts, because there is no difference between the two; they are necessary concomitants of each other. e.g., 'I struck the whole people' – here the ruling of striking applies to the whole people; both the collective whole and the individual parts can be intended; in fact, they are necessary concomitants because striking the whole people necessitates striking each individual, and striking each individual necessitates striking the whole. So here there is no difference between the collective and the individual. In summary, the relationship between 'kull majmu'i' and 'kull afradi' is 'umum wa khusus min wajh' (generality and specificity from one aspect). In this category, there is a common matter (maddah mushtarakah) and two separate matters. The common matter is where both the collective and the individual apply, e.g., 'I struck the whole people'. The first separate matter is where only the collective applies, not the individual, e.g., 'The whole people lift this stone'. The second separate matter is where only the individual applies, not the collective, e.g., 'Each individual person is sufficient for this food'. Now the objection is that here, in 'knowledge of rulings', 'knowledge' means cognition, and it is being applied to rulings. This falls into the third category of rulings, i.e., here both the collective whole and the individual parts can be intended; they are united and necessary concomitants because if you intend the knowledge of the collective whole of rulings, that can only be when there is knowledge of each individual ruling; similarly, if you intend knowledge of each individual ruling, when that is obtained, knowledge of the whole is also obtained. So here the collective and the individual are necessary concomitants of each other; there is no separation or opposition between them. Therefore, there is no need to mention them separately and refute each with an independent proof. The purpose is achieved by mentioning only one and refuting it. As for the contention that 'knowledge...' – some scholars responded on behalf of the author that here there is a distinction and opposition between the collective and the individual, and it is necessary to mention each separately. They said that the collective whole is general, including two aspects: one is knowledge of all, because knowledge of each individual is also knowledge of the whole; the other is knowledge of some, because knowledge of some also gives knowledge of the whole when the purpose is achieved by them. 'Knowledge of each individual' (kull afradi), which is the second possibility, refers to only one aspect, i.e., knowledge of each individual. So the collective whole is general and the individual is specific, hence an opposition is established. On this basis, the author mentioned each separately. The commentator refutes this response, saying that proving opposition and distinction between the two in this way is not necessary because if the collective whole is considered general, and one intends both the individual and some from it, then the proof given for the refutation of the collective whole would become invalid. Because the proof given was the non-finiteness of events: since events are infinite, rulings are also infinite, and knowledge of infinite rulings is impossible. This proof is only valid if by 'rulings' is meant all rulings. If some rulings are intended, then the non-finiteness proof is not correct because the infinity of events does not contradict the knowledge of some rulings. So that proof would be incomplete. Therefore, this response is not correct. 'And it appears that he intended by the collective whole etc.' – from here, the commentator Taftazani transmits a response from himself by which he proves the opposition between the collective and the individual. He says that by the collective whole, the author meant all rulings, past and future. The indication for this is that the author used for its refutation the proof of the non-finiteness of events, and this proof is only valid if by rulings is meant all past and future rulings. And by 'kull wahid' (kull afradi), the author meant only those rulings that have come into existence and have occurred, i.e., only past rulings. The indication is that the author used for its refutation the proof of 'la adri' (I don't know), and 'la adri' is said when the ruling has already come into existence. If it has not come into existence, how could one say 'la adri'? And when Ibn al-Hajib answered – when this objection to the definition of fiqh arose and the four possibilities [for the object of knowledge] were all invalidated, then Allamah Ibn al-Hajib answered that we take the first of these four possibilities, i.e., the collective whole of rulings, and by 'knowledge' we mean 'malakah' (faculty). The meaning is that fiqh is the name of the preparedness and faculty for knowledge of all shari rulings. It is not meant that actual knowledge of all rulings is obtained. And the preparedness and capacity to know infinite rulings is not beyond the power of a mujtahid; indeed, actual knowledge of them is impossible.
Source pages p. 197

Commentary
This passage is a critical refinement of the commentary on the definition of fiqh. The core issue is whether the object of knowledge in the definition of fiqh (knowledge of the rulings) should be understood as the collective totality of all rulings (kull majmu'i) or as each individual ruling (kull afradi), and whether these two need to be treated separately. The author of this work (Misk al-Malih) argues that in the context of 'knowledge of rulings', the collective and individual are interdependent: if you know all rulings collectively, you know each individually, and vice versa. Therefore, it is unnecessary to mention and refute them separately, as the original text (presumably Sadr al-Shariah's Al-Tawdih) did. He illustrates this with examples: lifting a stone applies only to the collective; food sufficiency applies only to individuals; striking applies to both equivalently. The knowledge of rulings falls into the third category. Some scholars attempted to defend the original author by saying that 'kull majmu'i' is general and includes both knowledge of all and knowledge of some, while 'kull afradi' is specific to knowledge of each. But Misk al-Malih rebuts that if 'kull majmu'i' is general and includes 'some', then the proof of infinite events (used to refute 'kull majmu'i') would fail because it only works if all rulings are intended. He then mentions Taftazani's alternative resolution: 'kull majmu'i' refers to all rulings past and future, while 'kull afradi' refers only to past rulings. This is supported by the proofs: infinite events for the former, and 'I don't know' (which presupposes occurrence) for the latter. Finally, he notes Ibn al-Hajib's answer: the intended meaning for fiqh is the first possibility, i.e., the collective totality, but knowledge here means malakah (a faculty or aptitude), not actual knowledge. Thus, the infinite number of rulings is not a problem because one only needs the preparedness to know them, not actual knowledge of all infinite rulings.

Key Terms
كل مجموعي (kull majmu'i): The collective whole; all members considered as a single unit.
كل افرادي (kull afradi): Each individual; every single member considered separately.
عموم خصوص من وجه (umum wa khusus min wajh): Generality and specificity from one aspect; two concepts that overlap in one respect but differ in others.
عدم تناهي الحوادث ('adam tanahi al-hawadith): The non-finiteness of events; events are infinite in time.
لا أدري (la adri): 'I don't know'; a statement made when one is asked about a ruling that has already occurred.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which category do 'knowledge of rulings' fall into?
A) Only collective (kull majmu'i)
B) Only individual (kull afradi)
C) Both equally (third category)
D) Neither; knowledge is impossible

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi usul, the definition of fiqh as 'knowledge of rulings' does not require actual knowledge of infinite rulings; it suffices to have the readiness (malakah) to know them, and the collective and individual knowledge are interdependent.

Amm and Khass — General and Specific
beginner
6 min
Definitions of Qadiyya and Logical Terms
Explains the various names of a complete proposition (qadiyya) and its parts

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the seven names of a complete proposition and define the logical terms used in syllogism.

Core Idea
A single meaningful statement can have seven different names depending on its role in reasoning.

This passage introduces foundational terminology used in Hanafi usul al-fiqh for logical analysis. Understanding these terms is essential for following how proofs are constructed and how arguments are evaluated. The same complete composite (murakkab tamm) that can be true or false gains different labels based on the perspective from which it is viewed.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core concept
The passage centers on the 'complete composite' (murakkab tamm), a meaningful statement capable of truth or falsehood. Recognize that this entity is the basis for all the names that follow.
2
List the seven names
From the passage, extract the seven names: qadiyya, khabar, ikhbar, muqaddima, matlub, natija, mas'ala. For each, note the specific perspective that triggers that name.
The perspectives are: containing a judgment, possibility of truth/falsehood, conveying a judgment, being part of a proof, being sought from a proof, being obtained from a proof, being asked about in teaching.
3
Distinguish subject and predicate
In a proposition (qadiyya), the subject of the judgment is called mawdu' (or mahkum alayh), and the predicate is called mahmul (or mahkum bih). In the conclusion, they become asghar and akbar.
4
Define syllogism terms
A proof consists of two premises: one containing the minor term (sughra) and one containing the major term (kubra). The term that repeats in both premises is the middle term (hadd al-awsat). The arrangement yields four figures (ashkal arba'a).
The middle term does not appear in the conclusion.
5
Recognize contextual naming
Understand that the essence (murakkab tamm) is one; the multiplicity of names arises from different considerations (i'tibarat). This prevents confusion when encountering these terms in usul texts.

قوله نعنی با القضايا اعلم ان المركب التام غرض شارح توضیح متن ہے متن میں قضیہ کا لفظ مذکور تھا اس کی تشریح کرتے ہوئے فرماتے ہیں کہ مرکب نام جو محتمل صدق و کذب ہوتا ہے اس کے مختلف اعتبارات و حیثیات کے لحاظ سے تقریبا سات نام ہیں اگر مرکب نام کو بایں حیثیت دیکھا جائے کہ مشتمل علی الحکم ہے تو اس کو (۱) قضیہ کہا جاتا ہے اور اگر بائیں حیثیت دیکھا جائے کہ اسمیں صدق و کذب کا احتمال ہے تو اسے (۲) خبر کہا جاتا ہے، اور اگر بائیں حیثیت دیکھا جائے کہ وہ مفید حکم ہے تو اسے (۳) اخبار کہا جاتا ہے، اگر بایں حیثیت دیکھا جائے کہ مرکب نام جزء دلیل بنتا ہے تو اسے (۴) مقدمہ کہا جاتا ہے اگر بایں حیثیت دیکھا جائے کہ مرکب تام کو دلیل سے طلب کیا جاتا ہے تو اسے (۵) مطلوب کہا جاتا ہے اور بائیں حیثیت کہ اس کو دلیل سے حاصل کیا جاتا ہے تو اسے (1) نتیجہ کہا جاتا ہے، اور بائیں حیثیت کہ اس مرکب تام کے بارے میں سوال کیا جاتا ہے اور تعلیم میں واقع ہوتا ہے تو اسے (۷) مسئلہ کہا جاتا ہے تو ذات ایک ہے (مرکب تام ) لیکن اعتبارات سے اختلاف کی وجہ سے عبارات ( تعبیرات) اور نام مختلف ہیں وامحکوم علیہ قضیہ میں ایک محکوم علیہ ہوتا ہے ایک حکم محکوم علیہ کو موضوع او محکوم بہ کو محمول کہا جاتا ہے و موضوع المطلوب سیسمی اصغر یعنی نتیجہ کے موضوع کو اصغر اور محمول کو اکبر کہا جاتا ہے والدلیل بتألف لامحالہ اور دلیل لا محالہ دو مقدمتین سے متولف و مرکب ہوتی ہے ان دو مقدموں میں سے ایک میں اصغر موجود ہوگا اور دوسرے میں اکبر جس میں اصغر موجود ہے اسے صغری اور جس میں اکبر موجود ہے اسے کبری کہا جاتا ہے اور ان دونوں مقدموں میں ایک چیز تکرار کے ساتھ واقع ہوتی ہے اس چیز کو حد اوسط کہا جاتا ہے دو مقدموں کو ملانے سے جو ھیت حاصل ہوتی ہے اس کو شکل کہتے ہیں پھر حد اوسط کے اعتبار سے چار شکلیں بنتی ہیں ان کو اشکال اربعہ کہا جاتا ہے۔ والاسط اما سے شارح انہیں اشکال
His statement: 'we mean by propositions.' Know that the complete composite is the commentator's purpose to explain the text. The text mentioned the word 'qadiyya'. He explains it by saying that a meaningful composite that is capable of truth and falsehood has approximately seven names depending on different considerations and aspects. If the meaningful composite is considered as containing a judgment, it is called (1) qadiyya (proposition). If considered as having possibility of truth and falsehood, it is called (2) khabar (report). If considered as conveying a judgment, it is called (3) ikhbar (informing). If considered as forming a part of a proof, it is called (4) muqaddima (premise). If considered as being sought from a proof, it is called (5) matlub (request). If considered as being obtained from a proof, it is called (6) natija (conclusion). If considered as being asked about and occurring in teaching, it is called (7) mas'ala (issue). So the essence is one (the complete composite), but due to different considerations, expressions and names differ. And the subject of a proposition (mahkum 'alayh) and the predicate (mahkum bih) are called subject (mawdu') and predicate (mahmul) respectively. The subject of the conclusion is called the minor term (asghar), and the predicate of the conclusion is called the major term (akbar). A proof (dalil) necessarily consists of two premises. In these two premises, one contains the minor term and the other contains the major term. The one containing the minor is called the minor premise (sughra), and the one containing the major is called the major premise (kubra). In both premises, one thing occurs repeatedly; that thing is called the middle term (hadd al-awsat). The form resulting from combining the two premises is called a figure (shakl). Then, per the middle term, four figures are formed; they are called the four figures (ashkal arba'a). And the middle term... (passage ends).
Source pages p. 228

Commentary
This passage explains the various names for a complete proposition (qadiyya) used in logic and usul al-fiqh. The same meaningful composite (murakkab tamm) that can be true or false is given different names depending on how it is viewed: as a judgment (qadiyya), as a report (khabar), as informing (ikhbar), as a premise (muqaddima), as a request (matlub), as a conclusion (natija), or as an issue (mas'ala). The text then introduces logical terms for syllogism: subject/predicate, minor/major term, minor/major premise, middle term, and the four figures. This foundational knowledge is essential for understanding how proofs are constructed in usul al-fiqh.

Key Terms
قضیہ (qadiyya): A meaningful composite that contains a judgment and is capable of truth/falsehood.
مرکب تام (murakkab tamm): A complete composite sentence that makes sense and can be true or false.
حد اوسط (hadd al-awsat): The middle term in a syllogism that appears in both premises but not in the conclusion.

Key Takeaway
The seven names of a proposition reflect its functional role in reasoning, not a change in its essence.

Classification of Essential Accidents (Arad Dhati)
Defining and classifying essential accidents into three types

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify an attribute as essential or accidental and identify its subtype based on the relationship of the intermediary to the subject's essence.

Core Idea
An essential accident necessarily accompanies a thing due to its essence, either directly or via an equal or more general intermediary.

This passage introduces the logical classification of attributes into 'arad dhati (essential) and 'arad gharib (accidental). Understanding this taxonomy is crucial for identifying which predicates belong to the subject matter of a science. The commentator expands on the three types of essential accidents and distinguishes them from accidental ones.

Guided Path
1
Define 'arad dhati
Understand the core definition: an attribute that befalls a thing due to its essence, either directly (without intermediary) or indirectly through an intermediary that is either coextensive or more general and part of the essence.
Direct example: existence for a human. Mediated examples: laughter via wonder (equal) and motion via animal (more general).
2
Identify the three subtypes
Separate the three types based on the presence and relationship of the intermediary: (1) no intermediary, (2) intermediary with equal extension, (3) intermediary with more general extension that is part of the essence.
Memorize the examples: existence (1), laughter (2), motion (3).
3
Contrast with 'arad gharib
Accidental accidents arise from an external cause. They also have three subtypes based on the external cause's relationship to the subject: equal, more general, more specific, or opposite.
Examples: heat in water from fire (opposite), motion in white from body (general).
4
Note the ikhtilaf
Later logicians (like Taftazani) consider the 'more general' intermediary as essential, while early logicians treated it as accidental. This affects classifications of attributes in arguments.
The difference arises from how strictly 'due to essence' is interpreted.

Explanation of Al-Tawdih wa al-Talwih: 'Arad dhati: here the definition of 'arad dhati is being explained: 'arad dhati is that which befalls a thing due to its essence, i.e., for which the essence itself is the source, and it befalls that thing by itself without an intermediary, like existence befalls a human without an intermediary – this is the first type of 'arad dhati. Or 'arad dhati is that which befalls a thing with an intermediary, and then the intermediary and the thing are in a relationship of equality, e.g., laughter befalls a human by means of wonder, and wonder and human are in a relationship of equality; it is said every human wonders and everyone who wonders is a human – this is the second type. Or 'arad dhati is that which befalls a thing with an intermediary, and then the intermediary and the thing are in a relationship of generality and particularity; the intermediary is more general than the thing but enters into its reality, being a part of it, e.g., motion befalls a human by means of animal, animal is more general than human and is a part of him – this is the third type. Benefit: Accidents are of two types: (1) essential accidents, (2) accidental accidents. Then each has three types. Reason for enumeration: That which befalls another thing is either due to its essence (1), or due to a part (2), or due to an external cause (3). Then if due to an external cause, that external cause is either equal to it (4), or more general (5), or more specific (6), or opposite (7). The first three are essential accidents, the last three are accidental accidents. (1) Example of first type: wonder befalls a human due to essence. (2) Motion befalls a human because of being an animal, and animal is also a human; this is an example of the second type. (3) Example of third type: laughter befalls a human by means of wonder; wonder is equal to human. (4) Example of fourth type: motion befalls a white thing by means of body; body is general to white. (5) Example of fifth type: laughter befalls an animal by means of human; human is specific, animal general. (6) Example of sixth: heat befalls water by means of fire; fire and water are opposite. Note: The commentator Taftazani's division and inclusion of the more general among essential accidents is in accordance with the later logicians' method. The early logicians included them among accidental accidents, as in the reason for enumeration.
Source pages p. 236

Commentary
This passage is an expansion on the definition and classification of 'arad dhati (essential accident) as used in Usul al-Fiqh and logic. The commentator explains that an essential accident is an attribute that necessarily accompanies a thing due to its essence. Three types are distinguished: (1) without an intermediary, such as existence for a human; (2) with an intermediary that is equivalent in extension, such as wonder for a human (since every human wonders and every wonderer is human); (3) with an intermediary that is more general and a part of the essence, such as motion for a human via animal (animal is more general, and animality is part of humanness). Accidental accidents are those due to an external cause, with subtypes: equal, general, specific, or opposite. Taftazani's classification follows later logicians; earlier logicians treated the 'more general' as accidental. This distinction is crucial for understanding the subject matter of a science, as only essential accidents are discussed as predicates of the subject.

Key Terms
عارض ذاتی ('arad dhati): An accident that occurs to a thing due to its essence, either directly or through an intermediary that is coextensive or more general but part of the essence.
عارض غریب ('arad gharib): An accident that occurs due to an external cause, not from the essence, and the cause is either equal, more general, more specific, or opposite.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which of the following is an example of the second type of 'arad dhati (essential accident with an intermediary that is equal)?
A) Existence for a human
B) Motion for a human via animal
C) Laughter for a human via wonder
D) Heat for water via fire

Key Takeaway
The classification of attributes into essential and accidental depends on the relationship between the attribute and the essence of the subject, with subcategories determined by the intermediary's relationship (none, equal, more general).
