Umum Majaz: The Hanafi Defense Against Combining Literal and Metaphorical
Defense of umum majaz against the charge of jam bayn al-haqiqah wa al-majaz

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between umum majaz and jam bayn al-haqiqah wa al-majaz, and apply the Hanafi principle of umum majaz to resolve apparent conflicts of meaning.

Core Idea
Umum majaz includes both literal and metaphorical in one general sense—no need to combine them.

This passage defends the Hanafi usul concept of 'umum majaz' (general metaphorical usage) against the charge that it entails the impossible act of simultaneously intending both literal and metaphorical meanings (jam bayn al-haqiqah wa al-majaz). The author argues that the intended meaning is a single general meaning that subsumes both senses, thus avoiding the logical impossibility. Understanding this distinction is crucial for correctly interpreting texts that carry multiple layers of meaning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core problem
Why would using a word to refer to both a literal and a metaphorical meaning be problematic? Understand the classical Usuli objection: it is impossible to intend two distinct meanings with a single utterance without a unifying concept.
2
Understand the Hanafi solution: umum majaz
Recognize that Hanafis propose a single general meaning that is broad enough to include both the literal and the metaphorical instances. This is not an attempt to combine two separate meanings.
3
Analyze the example in the text
The word 'idrak' (perception) is used. Its literal meaning is certain knowledge (ak jazim), and its metaphorical meaning is predominant opinion (idrak rajih = ghalabat al-zann). The general metaphorical meaning includes both under the concept of 'perception'.
4
Distinguish from jam bayn
Note that jam bayn would require the speaker to intend both the literal and metaphorical meanings as separate, simultaneous intentions. Umum majaz avoids this by having a single intention that covers both. No logical problem.
5
Apply to other usul contexts
Consider how this principle applies to other ambiguous words in the Quran and Hadith, e.g., 'lams' (touch) or 'qur' (menstrual period), where Hanafis often use umum majaz to encompass multiple valid interpretations.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
عموم مجاز مراد لے رہے ہیں کیونکہ ہم اثبات سے اور اک مراد لیتے ہیں اب اس میں معنی حقیقی اور مجازی دونوں شامل ہو جائیں گے اگر ادراک سے اور اک جازم مراد لیں تو یہ اثبات علم کا حقیقی معنی ہوگا اور اگر ادراک سے ادراک راج مراد لیں تو اس میں معنی مجازی داخل ہوگا کیونکہ ادراک راجح وہی غلبہ نظمن ہے لہذا ہم نے ایسا معنی مراد لیا جس میں حقیقی اور مجازی معنی دونوں داخل ہونگے اس لیے یہ عموم مجاز ہوگا نہ کہ جمع بین الحقیقتہ والمجاز فلا اشکال۔
We are taking a general metaphorical meaning because we take from 'ithbat' and 'ak' meaning. Now in this, both real and metaphorical meanings will be included. If from 'idrak' we take 'ak jazim' meaning, then this would be the real meaning of ithbat al-ilm. And if from 'idrak' we take 'idrak raj' meaning, then a metaphorical meaning enters into it because 'idrak rajih' is the same as 'ghalabat al-zann'. Therefore we adopted a meaning in which both real and metaphorical meanings are included. Hence this will be general metaphorical, not combination of literal and metaphorical, so no problem.
Source pages p. 243

Commentary
This passage is a defense of the Hanafi position that when a word like 'idrak' is used, it can carry both a literal and a metaphorical meaning through the device of 'umum majaz' (general metaphorical usage). The objection was that this would entail 'jam bayn al-haqiqah wa al-majaz' (combining literal and metaphorical in one usage). The Hanafi response is that they are not combining the two; rather, they are using a single meaning that is general enough to encompass both the literal (certain knowledge) and metaphorical (predominant opinion) senses. This is a standard Hanafi usul technique to avoid the impossibility of combining multiple meanings in one use.

Key Terms
عموم مجاز (umum majaz): A general meaning that encompasses both the literal and metaphorical senses of a word
جمع بین الحقیقة والمجاز (jam bayn al-haqiqah wa al-majaz): Using one word to intend both its literal and metaphorical meanings simultaneously

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

B) They allow jam bayn
C) They take only literal meaning

Key Takeaway
Umum majaz is a single general meaning that subsumes both literal and metaphorical senses, avoiding the impossibility of jam bayn.

Multiplicity of a Science's Subject: Conditions and Cases
Analyzing when a single science can have multiple subjects

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify when a single science can have multiple subjects and apply the Hanafi criteria to classify any science as having a single or multiple subjects.

Core Idea
A science can have multiple subjects only when its subject is relational and essential attributes arise from both relata.

This passage from Sharh al-Tawdih tackles a foundational question in the definition of a science: can a single discipline have more than one subject? The author critiques the absolute position of later scholars who claimed multiplicity is always permissible, and provides a nuanced analysis based on the nature of the subject (mawdu') and its essential attributes (awarid dhatiyya). Understanding this will sharpen your ability to define the scope and structure of any Islamic science.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two positions
Distinguish the absolute claim of the mutakhkhirin (later scholars) that multiplicity is always permissible, from the author's qualified view that it is only permissible under certain conditions.
2
Analyze the concept of mabhuth 'anhu (subject of investigation)
Understand that the subject is that whose essential states and attributes are studied. It can be a relation (idafah) or non-relational.
The term mawdu' is synonymous with mabhuth 'anhu.
3
Distinguish between relational and non-relational subjects
A relational subject involves a connection between two things (e.g., establishment of proofs to rulings). A non-relational subject is a single entity (e.g., act of mukallaf in fiqh).
Relational subjects are those involving an idafah.
4
Identify the origin of essential attributes
Determine whether the essential attributes (awarid dhatiyya) of the relational subject arise from both relata (both sides of the relation) or only one.
This is the key criterion for multiplicity.
5
Apply the three-case classification
Case 1: Subject is relational and attributes arise from both relata → multiple subjects (e.g., usul al-fiqh). Case 2: Subject is relational but attributes arise from one relatum → single subject (e.g., logic). Case 3: Subject is non-relational → single subject (e.g., fiqh).
Only in Case 1 is multiplicity permissible.

متن توضیح که واعـلـم انـي لـمـا وقعت في مباحث الموضوع والمسائل اردت ان اسمعك بعض مباحثها التي لا يستغنی الخ مصنف فرماتے ہیں کہ جب میں موضوع اور مسائل کے مباحث میں پڑ گیا ہوں تو میرا ارادہ یہ ہے کہ موضوع کے متعلق چند مباحث بیان کر دوں اگر چہ ان مباحث کا اس فن کے ساتھ کوئی تعلق نہیں ہے لیکن محصل اور طالب علم ان مباحث سے مستغنی نہیں ہو سکتا اس لیے افادہ محصل کیلئے بیان کر رہا ہوں مصنف نے تین مباحث ذکر کی ہیں اور تینوں جمہور علماء کے اقوال کے خلاف ہیں ( الجمث الاول) یہ ہے کہ علم واحد کیلئے متعدد موضوع ہو سکتے ہیں یا نہ مصنف فرماتے ہیں کہ علماء متقین نے یہ فرمایا. ہے کہ ایک علم کیلئے اشیاء متعددہ موضوع بن سکتی ہیں یہ جائز ہے وہ دلیل یہ بیان کرتے ہیں علم طب ایک علم ہے اس کے موضوع دو ہیں ایک بدن الانسان اور دوسرا ادویہ کیونکہ علم طب میں بدن انسان اور ادویہ دونوں کے احوال سے بحث ہوتی ہے ثابت ہوا کہ علم واحد کیلئے اشیاء متعددہ موضوع بن سکتی ہیں وهذا غير صحيح مصنف اپنی تحقیق بیان فرما رہے ہیں کہ میرے . نزدیک علی الاطلاق یہ کہنا کہ ہر علم کا موضوع متعدد ہو سکتے ہیں یہ میچ نہیں ہے بلکہ بعض صورتوں میں علم واحد کا موضوع اشیاء متعددہ ہوسکتی ہیں اور بعض صورتوں میں جائز نہیں ہے والتحقیق سے مصنف اس کی تفصیل بیان کر رہے ہیں تحقیق یہ ہے که محوت عنہ (یعنی وہ چیز جس کے احوال اور عوارض ذاتیہ سے علم میں بحث کی جارہی ہے بعنوان دیگر موضوع ) دو جال سے خالی نہیں ہے یا تو مسحوث عنها اضافت شئی الی شئی آخر ہو گا (یعنی موضوع ایسی چیز بن رہی ہوگی جس کی اضافت اور نسبت ہوگی کسی دوسری چیز کی طرف) یا موضوع اضافت شئی الی آخر نہیں ہوگا اگر موضوع اضافہ شئی الی شئی آخر ہے تو پھر دو صورتیں ہیں یا تو وہ عوارض جن کو مجوث عنھا میں دخل ہے دونوں مضافین سے ناشی ( پیدا) ہونگے یعنی بعض عوارض و مباحث واحوال کا تعلق ایک اضافت ( مضاف) سے ہو گا ور بعض کا دوسری اضافت ( مضاف ) کے ساتھ یا تمام عوارض و مباحث کا تعلق صرف ایک مضاف کے ساتھ ہوگا اگر عوارض کا تعلق دونوں مضافین سے ہے تو اس صورت میں علم واحد کا موضوع متعدد ہو سکتا ہے اور دونوں مضافین علم کا موضوع ہوں گے مثلا اصول فقہ اس میں مسجوث عنها ( مضاف ) اضافہ شئی الی شکی آخر ہے کیونکہ اس میں اثبات ادله محکم سے بحث ہوتی ہے تو اثبات ادلہ کی اضافت و نسبت ہے الی الحکم اس کو اضافت شئی الی شیئی آخر کہا جاتا ہے پھر وہ عوارض جن کو اس اضافت ( اثبات) میں دخل ہے ان میں سے بعض کا تعلق ادلہ سے ہے جیسے عموم و خصوص اشتراک وغیرہ اور بعض کا تعلق احکام سے ہے جیسے عبادة و عقوبت وغیرہ لہذا اس علم میں دونوں کے احوال سے بحث ہوگی اور دونوں اس علم کا موضوع بنیں گے ادلہ بھی اور احکام بھی اگر مجوث عنها (موضوع) اضافت شئی الی شئی آخر ہے لیکن عوارض ذاتیہ جن کو محوت عنھا میں دخل ہے وہ دونوں مضافین سے ناشی نہیں ہیں بلکہ ان کا تعلق صرف ایک مضاف سے ہے اس صورت میں موضوع علم ایک ہوگا تعدد موضوع جائز نہیں ہے جیسے منطق کا موضوع ایصال تصویر الی تصور آخر ہے اس میں محبوث عنھا ایصال ہے جو کہ مضاف و منسوب ہے الی تصور اخر لیکن جو عوارض ذاتیہ محوث عنھا کے ساتھ متعلق ہیں ان سب کا تعلق تصویر موصل سے ہے جو کہ احد المضافین ہے (جیسا کہ مصنف متن میں اس کی وضاحت کر چکے ہیں لہذا منطق کا موضوع صرف ایک ہوگا وہ ہے تصور موصل اسی طرح اگر محبوث عنها اضافت شئی الی شئی آخر نہ ہو بلکہ موضوع مفرد ہو تب بھی تعدد موضوع جائز نہیں ہے بلکہ موضوع واحد ہوگا جیسے فقہ کا موضوع فعل المکلف ہے حاصل ہیں کہ کل تین صورتیں ہیں (۱) مجوث عنھا اضافہ شئی الی شئی ہو اور عوارض ناشی من کلا المصافین ہوں درمیں صورت تعدد موضوع جائز ہے (۲) محبوث منها اضافت شئی ہو اور ( عوارش) ناشی من احد المضافین ہوں اس صورت میں تعدد موضوع جائز نہیں (۳) مجوث منها اضافت شئی نہ ہو اس صورت میں بھی تعدد
Matn of Tawdih: 'Know that when I came across the discussions of the subject and the issues, I wanted to make you hear some of those discussions that cannot be dispensed with.' The author says: When I fell into the discussions of the subject and issues, my intention is to mention a few discussions related to the subject, even if these discussions have no connection with this discipline, but the seeker and student cannot dispense with them, so for the benefit of the seeker I am mentioning them. The author mentioned three discussions, and all three are contrary to the views of the majority of scholars. The first discussion is: Can a single science have multiple subjects? The author says: The scholars of the later generations said that a single science can have multiple subjects; this is permissible. They present the evidence: Medicine is one science, its subjects are two: the human body and medicines, because in medicine the states of both are discussed. So it is established that a single science can have multiple subjects. And this is not correct. The author is presenting his research: In my view, to say absolutely that every science can have multiple subjects is not correct; rather, in some cases a single science can have multiple subjects, and in some cases it is not permissible. And by research, the author is explaining its detail. The research is: The maḥmūl 'alayhi (that whose states and essential accidnts are discussed in the science, also called subject) has two states: either it is an addition of one thing to another (i.e., the subject is something that has a relation to another thing), or it is not. If the subject is a relation of one thing to another, then there are two further states: either those attributes that enter into the maḥmūl 'alayhi arise from both relata—i.e., some attributes and discussions relate to one relatum and some to the other—or all attributes relate only to one relatum. If the attributes relate to both relata, then in that case a single science can have multiple subjects, and both relata will be subjects of the science. For example, Usul al-Fiqh: its maḥmūl 'alayhi is a relation of one thing to another, because it discusses the establishment of proofs from rulings. So the establishment of proofs has a relation to rulings; this is called a relation of one thing to another. Then the attributes that enter into this relation (establishment) include some that relate to proofs, like generality, particularity, commonality, etc., and some that relate to rulings, like worship, punishment, etc. Hence, in this science the states of both are discussed, and both will be subjects of this science: proofs and rulings. If the maḥmūl 'alayhi (subject) is a relation of one thing to another, but the essential attributes that enter into the maḥmūl 'alayhi do not arise from both relata, rather they relate only to one relatum, then in that case the subject of the science is one; multiplicity of subjects is not permissible. For example, the subject of logic is the connection of an image to another conception; its maḥmūl 'alayhi is the connection, which is related to another conception. But all the essential attributes related to the maḥmūl 'alayhi relate to the connecting image, which is one of the relata (as the author clarified in the text). Therefore, the subject of logic is only one: the connecting image. Similarly, if the maḥmūl 'alayhi is not a relation of one thing to another, but rather the subject is simple, then multiplicity is also not permissible; rather the subject is one. For example, the subject of fiqh is the act of the mukallaf. In summary, there are three cases: (1) The maḥmūl 'alayhi is a relation of one thing to another and the attributes arise from both relata: here multiplicity of subjects is permissible. (2) The maḥmūl 'alayhi is a relation but attributes arise from one relatum: here multiplicity is not permissible. (3) The maḥmūl 'alayhi is not a relation: here multiplicity [is not permissible].
Source pages p. 246

Commentary
This passage is a discussion on whether a single science (like usul al-fiqh) can have multiple subjects. The author presents the view of later scholars (mutakhkhirin) who claim that multiple subjects are permissible, giving the example of medicine with two subjects: the human body and medicines. The author disagrees absolutely and provides a detailed analysis. He explains that the subject (al-mabhuth 'anhu) can be either relational or non-relational. If relational, and the essential attributes (al-awarid al-dhatiyya) of that relation arise from both relata (like in usul al-fiqh where attributes relate to both proofs and rulings), then the science can have two subjects. But if the attributes only arise from one relatum (like in logic, where attributes only relate to the connecting image), then the subject is single. If the subject is non-relational (like fiqh with the act of the mukallaf), it also has a single subject. Thus, the author identifies three scenarios and concludes that only in the first scenario is multiple subjects permissible. This is a key usul definitional point about the structure of a science.

Key Terms
موضوع (mawdu'): The subject matter of a science; that whose states and essential accidents are studied.
اضافت (idafah): Relation; the subject is relational if it involves a relation to another thing.
عوارض ذاتیہ (awarid dhatiyyah): Essential attributes that are intrinsic to the subject and discussed in the science.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In which scenario does the author say a single science can have multiple subjects?
A) When the subject is non-relational.
B) When the subject is relational and all attributes arise from one relatum.
C) When the subject is relational and attributes arise from both relata.
D) When the subject is the act of the mukallaf.

Key Takeaway
The possibility of multiple subjects in a science is conditional: the subject must be relational and essential attributes must arise from both relata.

Amm and Khass — General and Specific
intermediate
15 min
Can a Single Science Have Multiple Subjects?
Analyzing a usul dispute on the subject of a science

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on multiplicity of a science's subject and evaluate Taftazani's critique using the distinction between essential accidents and subject.

Core Idea
A science's unity requires a single subject; convention alone cannot override necessity.

This passage from Misk al-Maleeh's commentary on al-Tawdih addresses a foundational question: can one science have multiple subjects? Sadr al-Shariah argues no, while Taftazani allows it in relational sciences. The author dissects the arguments, clarifying when multiplicity is genuinely permissible. Understanding this clarifies the scope of usul al-fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core issue
What is the dispute about? The question is whether a single science ('ilm) can have more than one subject (mawdu'). The author of al-Tawdih (Sadr al-Shariah) denies this, while Taftazani and many verifying scholars allow it in certain cases.
This is a methodological dispute in usul al-fiqh about the nature of a science.
2
State Sadr al-Shariah's position and his proof
Sadr al-Shariah argues that the unity of a science depends on the unity of its issues and objects, which in turn depends on the unity of its subject. If the subject is multiple, the science would be multiple. Therefore, a single science can have only one subject.
The proof is a logical chain: science → issues/objects → subject.
3
Analyze Taftazani's objection
Taftazani objects that convention (istilah) can unite a science. For example, if we define a science as one, it is one even if its subjects differ, as long as there is a relation between them. He cites the analogy of medicine, where both the human body and medicines are discussed.
This challenges the necessity of a single subject.
4
Examine the author's response to the convention argument
The author rejects convention without a genuine common aspect (jihah mushtarakah). If accepted, it would allow arbitrary groupings (e.g., fiqh and geometry as one science), which is absurd. Thus, convention alone is insufficient.
The key is that there must be a real connection, not just a label.
5
Evaluate the analogy from medicine and the two scenarios of relational sciences
The author shows that in medicine, the subject is still the human body; medicines are essential accidents, not the subject. Then he explains two types of relational sciences: (1) if essential accidents arise from both relata, multiplicity is allowed (e.g., usul al-fiqh: evidences and rulings); (2) if they arise from only one, the subject remains single (e.g., logic).
This clarifies the exact conditions for permissible multiplicity.

مسک الملیح شرح التوضيح موضوع جائز نہیں ہے لان اتحاد العلم الخ سے مصنف عدم تعدد موضوع کی دلیل بیان کر رہے ہیں خلاصہ یہ ہے علم کا اتحاد و اختلاف مسائل اور معلومات کے اتحاد اور اختلاف پر موقوف ہے اگر معلومات ومسائل ایک جیسے ہیں تو علم بھی واحد اگر معلومات مختلف ہیں تو علم بھی مختلف ہوں گے پھر مسائل اور معلومات کا اتحاد و اختلاف موقوف ہے موضوع کے اتحاد و اختلاف پر اگر موضوع ایک ہے تو معلومات و مسائل بھی ایک اور اگر موضوع مختلف ہے تو مسائل بھی مختلف اور ضابطہ یہ ہے کہ کسی شئی کے موقوف علیہ کا موقوف علیہ بھی اس شئی کیلئے موقوف علیہ ہوتا ہے لہذا علم کا اتحاد و اختلاف موضوع کے اتحاد و اختلاف پر موقوف ہوگا اب اگر ایک علم کے موضوع متعدد ہوں تو تعدد و اختلاف موضوع تقاضا کرے گا تعدد اختلاف علم کا حالانکہ ہم تو اس کو علم واحد فرض کر چکے ہیں اسی بناء پر کہا گیا ہے کہ علم واحد کیلئے صرف ایک موضوع ہوگا ایک سے زیادہ نہیں کیونکہ اختلاف موضوع سے اختلاف علم واجب ہے وان ارید سے اشکال کا رفیعہ ہے کہ کوئی شخص کہ سکتا ہے کہ آپ کا یہ قول کہ اختلاف اور تعدد موضوع سے علم متعدد ہو جاتا ہے قابل تسلیم نہیں ہے کیونکہ ہم ایک علم کے بارے میں یہ اصطلاح بنا لیتے ہیں کہ وہ علم واحد ہے تو ہماری اصطلاح کے مطابق تو وہ علم واحد ہے اگر چہ اس علم کے مسائل و معلومات میں کوئی جہت مشتر کہ نہ ہو اگر چہ موضوع متعدد بھی کیوں نہ ہوں لہذا یہ کہنا کہ تعدد موضوع سے تعدد علم واجب ہے صحیح نہیں۔ مصنف نے جواب دیا کہ اگر کوئی شخص ایسی اصطلاح بنالیتا ہے تو اس کا کوئی اعتبار نہیں ہے کیونکہ مسائل و معلومات کے اختلاف کے باوجود اس علم کو واحد قرار دید بنا بغیر کسی جھت مشترکہ کے اس کا کوئی شخص بھی قائل نہیں ہے لہذا یہ اصطلاح معتبر نہیں ہے۔ علی ان لکل سے دوسری وجہ بطلان بیان کی اگر اس کو تسلیم کر لیا جائے پھر تو ہر شخص کو اصطلاح بنانے کا حق حاصل ہو گا کہ وہ مختلف علوم کو جمع کر کے اصطلاح بنالے کہ یہ علم واحد ہے کیونکہ ان مسائل و معلومات کا کسی جہت واحد میں مربوط ہونا تو کوئی ضروری نہیں ہے اب اگر کوئی شخص یہ اصطلاح بنالے کہ علم فقہ اور علم ہندسہ ایک ہی علم ہیں اور اس کے موضوع متعدد ہیں فعل مکلف اور مقدار تو آپ کو اس کی بات مانی پڑے گی کیونکہ ہر ایک کو اصطلاح بنانے کی آپ نے آزادی دے دی ہے۔ وما اور دوا من النظیر سے مصنف جمہور محققین کی دلیل کا جواب دے رہے ہیں کہ آپ نے جو علم طلب کی نظیر پیش کی ہے وہ درست نہیں ہے کیونکہ علم طب کا موضوع واحد ہے وہ ہے بدن الانسان ادویہ علم طب کا موضوع نہیں ہے بلکہ اس کے عوارض ذاتیہ میں سے ہے کیونکہ ادویہ سے بحث اس حیثیت سے ہوتی ہے کہ کوئی دوائی بدن کو صحت مند بناتی ہے اور کوئی دوائی بیمار بناتی ہے تو ان ادویات کا تعلق احوال بدن سے ہے لہذا یہ عوارض ذاتیہ میں سے ہیں نہ کہ موضوع سے یہ دلیل درست نہیں ہے مصنف نے تعدد موضوع کی تحقیق میں اضافت شئی الی شئی کی دو مثالیں بیان کی ہیں ایک اصول فقہ کے موضوع اثبات الادلة للا حکام کی اور دوسری منطق کے موضوع ایصال تصور الی تصور کی ان دونوں کو اکٹھا بیان کرنے سے یہ نہ سمجھ لیا جائے کہ ان دونوں میں تعدد موضوع جائز ہے بلکہ تعدد موضوع اول صورت میں جائز ہے اور ثانی صورت ( ایصال تصور ابلی تصور ) میں تعدد موضوع جائز نہیں ہے اس صورت کو مائن نے ذکر نہیں کیا کیونکہ موضوع اگر چه اضافت شئی الی شئی ہے لیکن عوارض ناشی من احد المضافین ہیں ان دونوں کو اکٹھا بیان کرنے کی وجہ صرف یہ ہے کہ دونوں کا محبوث عنها اضافت دیگی الی شتی آخر ہے تفصیل یہ ہے مبحوث عنہ فی اعلم اضافت بین الشیئین ہوگی یا نہ اگر مجوث عند اضافت بین شیمین ہو تو اس کی دو صورتیں ہیں یا وہ عوارض جن کو مبحوث عنہ میں دخل ہے ان میں سے بعض ناشی ہوں گے ایک مضاف نے اور بعض ناشی ہوں گے من المضاف الاخر یا ایسا نہیں ہوگا بلکہ تمام عوارض ناشی ہوں گے ایک ہی مضاف سے اگر مبحوث عند اضافت بین شیئین ہے اور بعض عوارض ایک مضاف سے پیدا ہور ہے ہیں اور بعض دوسرے سے تو اس صورت میں موضوع متعدد ہوگا جیسے عموم داشتراک اور تو اتر وغیرہ اور بعض احوال ناشی من الحکم ہیں مثلا عقوبت عبادت وغیرہ لہذا یہاں موضوع متعدد ہوگا اور دونوں مضافین ( ادلہ اور احکام ) اصول فقہ کا موضوع بینگے یہ پہلی صورت ہے اور اس Misk al-Maleeh Sharh al-Tawdih: The subject (mawdu') is not permissible because of the unity of science... The author is presenting the proof for the non-multiplicity of the subject. The summary is that the unity and difference of a science depends on the unity and difference of its issues (masa'il) and objects of knowledge (ma'lumat). If the objects and issues are the same, the science is one; if they differ, the science differs. Then the unity and difference of issues and objects depends on the unity and difference of the subject. If the subject is one, the objects and issues are one; if the subject differs, the issues also differ. And the rule is: the thing on which something depends, that thing's own dependency is also dependency for the first thing. Therefore, the unity and difference of the science depends on the unity and difference of the subject. Now if a single science has multiple subjects, the multiplicity and difference of the subject would necessitate the multiplicity and difference of the science, even though we have assumed it to be a single science. On this basis, it is said that a single science can have only one subject, not more, because difference in subject necessarily causes difference in science. (وان ارید) is the refutation of an objection: someone might say that your statement that difference and multiplicity of subject results in multiplicity of science is not acceptable because we can make a convention (istilah) that it is one science; according to our convention it is one science even if its issues and objects have no common aspect (jihah mushtarakah), even if the subjects are multiple. So it is not correct to say that multiplicity of subject necessarily causes multiplicity of science. The author replied: if someone makes such a convention, it is not valid because no one holds that a science can be considered one despite differences in issues and objects without any common aspect. Thus this convention is not valid. (علی ان لکل) is the second proof of invalidity: if this is accepted, then every person would have the right to make a convention, collecting different sciences and calling them one science, because there is no necessity for those issues and objects to be connected by a single aspect. If someone makes a convention that fiqh and geometry are one science with multiple subjects (actions of the mukallaf and quantity), you would have to accept it because you have given everyone freedom to make conventions. (وما أوردوا من النظیر) The author is responding to the proof of the majority of verifying scholars that the example of medical science (al-tibb) you presented is not correct, because the subject of medicine is one, which is the human body; medicines are not the subject of medicine but rather among its essential accidents ('awarid dhatiyyah) because medicines are discussed insofar as they make the body healthy or sick, so their relation is to the states of the body; hence they are among the essential accidents, not the subject. This proof is not correct. The author, in investigating multiplicity of subject, gave two examples of the relation (idafah) of one thing to another: first, the subject of usul al-fiqh is the proof of evidences for rulings (ithbat al-adillah li al-ahkam), and second, the subject of logic is the transmission of one concept to another (isal tasawwur ila tasawwur). From presenting both together, it should not be understood that multiplicity of subject is permissible in both; rather, multiplicity of subject is permissible in the first case and not permissible in the second case (isal tasawwur ila tasawwur). The author did not mention this second case because although the subject is a relation of one thing to another, the accidents arise from only one of the two relata. The reason for presenting both together is solely that the discussed matter (mabhuth 'anha) in both is a relation of one thing to another. Detail: The discussed matter (mabhuth 'anhu) is either a relation (idafah) between two things or not. If it is a relation between two things, there are two scenarios: either the accidents that intervene in the discussed matter arise partly from one relatum and partly from the other, or not, rather all accidents arise from one relatum. If the discussed matter is a relation between two things and some accidents arise from one relatum and some from the other, then in this case the subject is multiple, and both relata become the subject. For example, the subject of usul al-fiqh is the proof of evidences for rulings; it is a relation between two things: evidences (adillah) insofar as they are related to rulings (ahkam). The states (ahwal) that intervene in the proof are partly arising from the evidence, such as 'umum, ishtirak, tawatur, etc., and partly arising from the ruling, such as 'uqubah, 'ibadah, etc. Therefore here the subject is multiple, and both relata (evidences and rulings) are the subject of usul al-fiqh. This is the first scenario, and in it multiplicity of subject is permissible. (2) The second scenario is that the discussed matter is a relation, but the states that intervene in the discussed matter do not arise from both relata, but rather arise from only one relatum. Then in this scenario, one...
Source pages p. 248

Commentary
This passage is a commentary (sharh) by Misk al-Maleeh on Al-Tawdih, analyzing a key usul al-fiqh dispute about whether a single science ('ilm) can have more than one subject (mawdu'). The author of Al-Tawdih, Sadr al-Shariah, argues that the unity of a science requires a single subject, and any multiplicity would necessitate multiple sciences. He refutes the objection that convention alone could unite a science, showing that such convention has no basis and leads to absurdity. He also critiques the analogy from medicine, clarifying that medicines are not part of the subject but rather essential accidents. The passage then details two scenarios of relational sciences: first, where accidents arise from both relata, multiplicity of subject is allowed (as in usul al-fiqh, where both evidences and rulings are subjects); second, where all accidents arise from one relatum, multiplicity is not allowed (as in logic). This internal Hanafi dispute between Sadr al-Shariah (author of Tawdih) and Taftazani (commentator of Talwih) revolves around the nature of subject and the role of convention. The commentary follows the author's reasoning step by step, emphasizing that a genuine common aspect (jihah mushtarakah) is necessary for the unity of a science, not mere conventional labeling.

Key Terms
موضوع (mawdu'): The subject matter of a science; that which the science studies in terms of its essential accidents ('awarid dhatiyyah).
عوارض ذاتیہ ('awarid dhatiyyah): Essential accidents; attributes or states that necessarily attach to the subject of a science and are studied in that science.
اضافت (idafah): Relation; when the subject of a science is a relationship between two things (e.g., evidences and rulings in usul al-fiqh).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the analysis in this passage, in which scenario is multiplicity of subject (ta'addud al-mawdu') permissible for a single science?
A) When the discussed matter is a relation between two things and all essential accidents arise from one relatum.
B) When the discussed matter is a relation between two things and essential accidents arise from both relata.
C) When the science has no subject at all.
D) When the science is purely conventional.

Key Takeaway
A science can have multiple subjects only if its discussed matter is a relation and essential accidents arise from both relata, as in usul al-fiqh.

Can One Thing Be Subject of Multiple Sciences? The Hanafi Position
Arguing that one entity can be subject of multiple sciences via different accidents

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the Hanafi position on whether one thing can be the subject of multiple sciences and explain the proof using the concept of diverse essential accidents.

Core Idea
One thing can be subject to multiple sciences if it has different types of essential accidents.

This passage from al-Tawdih tackles a fundamental issue in the classification of sciences: can a single entity serve as the subject for multiple disciplines? The majority of scholars argue it cannot, as distinction between sciences requires distinct subjects. Sadr al-Shariah, however, defends the Hanafi position that it is possible and actually occurs. He offers two proofs: a rational argument based on diverse essential accidents, and a theological example from the Divine Essence.

Guided Path
1
Identify the issue
The central question is whether one entity (shay' wahid) can be the subject of two or more sciences. This affects how we demarcate disciplines.
2
State the majority position
The majority hold that if the subject is the same, the sciences cannot be distinguished, so one thing cannot be subject to multiple sciences.
3
State the Hanafi claims
The author makes two claims: (1) it is rationally possible, and (2) it actually occurs in reality.
4
Examine the rational proof
The rational proof: one subject can have multiple types of essential accidents (awarid dhatiyya). Each type can be studied separately, yielding distinct sciences with the same subject.
5
Examine the actual proof
The actual proof: the Divine Essence (al-Wahid al-Haqiqi) has multiple attributes like knowledge and power, which are essential accidents of different types, studied in different Islamic sciences (e.g., Ilm al-Kalam and Usul al-Din).

Text of al-Tawdih: 'And among them is that the well-known view is that one thing cannot be the subject of two sciences.' Here the author is presenting the third discussion. The summary of the discussion is whether one thing (which is determined in essence and consideration) can be the subject of two or several sciences. The majority hold that one thing cannot be the subject of multiple sciences, because if one thing in essence and consideration becomes the subject of two sciences, then those two sciences cannot be distinguished, since the distinction between sciences is due to the subject. If the subject becomes one, distinction cannot be established. The author, refuting the majority, says that there is no prohibition for one thing to become the subject of multiple sciences; rather it is possible and actually occurs in reality. So the author makes two claims: (1) That one thing can be the subject for multiple sciences is rationally possible. (2) It is not only rationally possible but also actually occurs in reality. The author proves both claims with evidence. From 'inna al-shay' al-wahid' [if the one thing has different types of essential accidents] is the evidence for the first claim. The presentation of the evidence is that it is possible for one thing to become the subject of two or several sciences, because sometimes one thing (subject) has several types of essential accidents. So it happens that in one science, one type of essential accidents of that subject thing is expounded, and in another science, another type of essential accidents of the same subject thing is expounded. So in that respect, the subject for both sciences is one, but due to the difference of essential accidents, those two sciences differ from each other. The question arises: what proof do you have that one thing has diverse essential accidents? The author answers with 'fa-inna al-wahid al-haqiqi' [for the truly one (the Divine Essence) is described with many attributes]. Our proof is that the truly one (the Essence of Allah Most High) is described with many attributes. These many attributes are called essential accidents of the Divine Essence, and these attributes of Allah (accidents) are of different types: (1) some are real attributes (attribute real...
Source pages p. 260

Commentary
This passage introduces the third issue in the discussion of the subject of a science: can one entity serve as the subject for multiple sciences? The majority (جمہور) say no, because distinction between sciences derives from distinct subjects. The author (Sadr al-Shariah) counters that it is possible and actually occurs, giving two proofs: rational possibility (one subject can have multiple types of essential accidents, each type treated in a different science) and actual occurrence (the Divine Essence, though one, has multiple attributes like knowledge and power, each studied in different sciences). The passage explains the first proof in detail, then begins the second proof using the Divine Essence as an example of a single subject with diverse essential accidents.

Key Terms
شئی واحد (shay' wahid): A single entity determined in essence and consideration (بالذات والاعتبار), capable of having multiple types of essential accidents
عوارض ذاتیہ (awarid dhatiyya): Essential accidents that inhere in a subject and are studied in a science; they can be of different types (انواع)
واحد حقیقی (wahid haqiqi): The truly one, i.e., the Divine Essence (ذات باری تعالی), which is one but possesses multiple attributes (صفات) considered essential accidents

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) The Divine Essence having multiple attributes
C) The consensus of early scholars

Key Takeaway
One entity can be subject to multiple sciences if it possesses diverse essential accidents, each studied separately.

Resolving Common Usage in the Definition of Quran
Addressing the objection of common usage from dual meaning

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the Hanafi school rejects the charge of umum al-ishtirak in the definition of Quran, and apply the distinction between a single general meaning (kulli) and multiple independent placements.

Core Idea
The Quran's meaning is a single general concept covering whole and part — no common usage required.

A key objection in Hanafi usul is that the word 'Quran' cannot simultaneously refer to the entire revelation and a single verse without becoming a common term with two distinct meanings. This lesson unpacks the Hanafi response by al-Taftazani, which shows that the Quran has a single general (kulli) meaning that legitimately applies to both the whole and its parts. Understanding this distinction is crucial for grasping the Hanafi approach to defining foundational Islamic terms.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Objection
Recognize the hypothetical question: if 'Kitab' in the second sense (general meaning) is interpreted as 'Quran', and 'Quran' is said to apply to both the whole and the part, does that not make 'Quran' a common term with two meanings?
The objector assumes that applying to whole and part requires two separate linguistic placements (wad').
2
State the Hanafi Position
The Hanafi school, as explained by Taftazani, holds that the Quran has a single general meaning (ma'na kulli) that includes both the entire book and any portion of it. This is like the word 'insan' applying to all humans.
The key is that the general meaning is one, not multiple.
3
Distinguish Two Levels of Placement
Taftazani clarifies that there are two placements: one for the whole as a whole (e.g., the complete Quran), and another placement that is general, covering both whole and part. When defining 'Quran' in usul, it is the general placement that is intended.
This avoids the need for two independent specific placements.
4
Apply the Principle to the Objection
Since the intended meaning is the general one, when we say 'Quran' we mean the general concept that applies to both the complete revelation and a single verse. Thus, there is no simultaneous intention of two distinct meanings.
Common usage (umum al-ishtirak) would require two independent placements, which is not the case here.
5
Determine the Thamarah al-Khilaf
The practical consequence: Hanafis can define the Quran as both the whole and the part without violating the prohibition of umum al-ishtirak. This affects how they derive rulings from the Quran — each verse is considered part of the same general meaning.

فان قيل فالكتاب بالمعنى الثانى الخ فرض سوال و جواب سوال یہ ہوتا ہے کہ اگر کتاب کا معنی ثانی یعنی معنی کلی مراد لیا جائے جو کل اور بعض دونوں کو شامل ہوتا ہے تو کیا اس کی تفسیر القرآن کے ساتھ کرنا صحیح ہوگا جبکہ قرآن صرف مجموع شخص کو کہا جاتا ہے تو مجموع شخصی معنی کلی کی تفسیر کیسے بن سکتا ہے یہ تو تفسیر الضد بالضد لازم آتی ہے۔ جواب! شارح تفتازانی نے جواب دیا کہ تفسیر ہوسکتی ہے کیونکہ جس طرح کتاب کی وضع عام تھی کل مجموع کیلئے بھی اور جز ء کیلئے بھی اسی طرح قرآن پاک بھی ایک معنی کلی ہے جو کل پر بھی صادق آتا ہے اور بعض پر بھی کل میں بھی حقیقت ہے ، رجض میں بھی تو اب قرآن کا معنی بھی کھلی ہے اور الکتاب کا معنی بھی کلی ہے تو معنی کلی تفسیر بن رہا ہے معنی کئی کیلئے یہ بالکل جائز ہے فان قيل يلزم عموم المشترک اعتراض کا جواب دینا مقصود ہے اشکال یہ ہوتا ہے کہ جب آپ نے یہ کہا کہ قرآن مجید کل میں بھی حقیقت ہے اور جزء میں بھی تو یہ قرآن کے دو معنی ہوں گے اور قرآن دونوں میں مشترک ہو گا پھر آپ نے یہ کہا کہ القرآن سے معنی کھلی مراد ہے جو کل اور جزء دونوں کو شامل ہے تو عموم اشتراک لازم آئے گا دونوں معنی بیک وقت مراد لے لیں گے اور عموم اشتراک عند الاحناف ممنوع ہے قبلنا سے جواب دیا کہ ہم نے یہ کہا ہے کہ قرآن کل میں بھی حقیقت ہے اور جزء (بعض) میں بھی حقیقت ہے اس کا یہ مقصد ہرگز نہیں ہے کہ کل کیلئے بھی مستقل وضع ہے اور جزء کیلئے بھی اس کی مستقل وضع ہے بلکہ مراد یہ ہے کہ قران کی دو وضع ہیں ایک وضع مجموع من حیث المجموع یعنی کل کیلئے ہے فقہ اور دوسری وضع وہ ہے جوکل اور جزء دونوں کو شامل ہے یعنی معنی کلی کے اعتبار سے وضع ہو جیسے ما نقل الينا الخ اب ہم نے جو یہ کہا ہے کہ القرآن سے مراد وہ قرآن ہے جو کل کیلئے بھی موضوع ہے اور جزء کیلئے بھی اس سے مراد ایک ہی وضع ہے اور قرآن پاک کل اور جزء کو شامل ہے باعتبار وضع واحد اور اسی اعتبار سے یہ الکتاب کی تغییر واقع ہو رہا ہے لہذا یہاں کوئی عموم مشترک نہیں ہے عموم مشترک تو جب ہوتا جب کہ کل کیلئے مستقل علیحدہ وضع ہوتی اور جزء کے لئے وضع ہوتی حالانکہ ایسا نہیں ہے بلکہ ایک ہی وضع کل اور جزء دونوں کیلئے ہے فلا اشکال
If it is said about the Book in the second sense... hypothetical question and answer. The question is: If the second meaning of 'Kitab' is intended, i.e., the general meaning that includes both the whole and the part, is it correct to interpret it with the Quran, while the Quran is only called the particular whole? So how can the particular whole become the interpretation of the general meaning? That would be interpreting opposite by opposite. Answer: The commentator Taftazani answered that it is possible, because just as the word 'Kitab' had a general usage for both the whole and the part, similarly the Holy Quran is also a general meaning that applies to the whole and the part; it is true in the whole and also in the part. So now the meaning of Quran is also general (kulli) and the meaning of Kitab is also general, so the general meaning is interpreting the general meaning, which is perfectly permissible. If it is said that this necessitates common usage (umum al-ishtirak)... the purpose is to answer this objection. The objection is: When you said that the Holy Quran is true for the whole and also for the part, then there would be two meanings of Quran, and Quran would be common between them. Then you said that by 'al-Quran' the general meaning is intended that includes both whole and part, so common usage would be required—both meanings would be intended simultaneously, and common usage is prohibited by the Hanafis. We answer: We said that the Quran is true for the whole and also for the part, but this does not mean that there is an independent placement (wad') for the whole and another for the part. Rather, the meaning is that the Quran has two placements: one placement for the whole as a whole, and another placement that includes both the whole and the part, i.e., in terms of the general meaning, like 'ma nuqila ilayna' etc. Now we said that by 'al-Quran' is meant that Quran which is placed for the whole and also for the part—this refers to a single placement, and the Holy Quran includes the whole and the part by virtue of a single placement, and in this respect it is interpreting 'al-Kitab'. Therefore, there is no common usage here. Common usage would only occur if there were an independent placement for the whole and a separate placement for the part, but this is not the case. Rather, a single placement is for both the whole and the part. Hence, no problem.
Source pages p. 278

Commentary
This passage is a dispute (usul dispute) about the definition of the Quran and the charge of common usage (umum al-ishtirak). The objector argues that if the Quran can refer both to the whole and to a part, then it has two meanings and is a common term. But the Hanafi response, by Taftazani, clarifies that there are two levels of placement: one for the whole as a whole (the particular whole) and another a general placement that applies to both whole and part. However, when using the term 'Quran' in definitions, it is the general meaning that is intended, and this general meaning includes both whole and part under a single placement. Therefore, it is not a case of common usage that would require intending two distinct meanings simultaneously; rather, it is one meaning that encompasses both. This is an important Hanafi usul point: the Quran is a general (kulli) meaning that applies to all its instances, just like 'kitab' is also a general meaning. The Hanafi school prohibits intending two distinct meanings from a single word (umum al-ishtirak) but allows a single general meaning that covers multiple instances.

Key Terms
عموم المشترک (umum al-ishtirak): The situation where a word has two distinct meanings and both are intended simultaneously.
وضع (wad'): Linguistic placement: the conventional assignment of a meaning to a word.
معنی کلی (ma'na kulli): A general meaning that applies to many particulars without being common.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Taftazani's response, why does the Quran having meanings for the whole and part not constitute 'umum al-ishtirak'?
A) Because the Quran has two independent placements.
B) Because the Quran has a single general meaning that covers both whole and part.
C) Because the Hanafis allow common usage in definitions.
D) Because the part is not considered a meaning.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school resolves the charge of common usage in the definition of Quran by insisting on a single general meaning (kulli) that encompasses both the entire revelation and its parts.

Amm and Khass — General and Specific

Defining Shakhsi: The Hanafi View on the Quran as a Personal Entity
Analyzing whether the Quran is shakhsi and its definability

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between a real definition (hadd haqiqi) and a distinguishing definition (hadd rusumi) for a personal entity (shakhsi), and apply the Hanafi position to the Quran.

Core Idea
A personal entity cannot be defined by a real definition because its accidents can be shared; only direct indication suffices.

This passage is a dense critical refinement (hishiya) from a Hanafi usul commentary. It grapples with whether the Quran, as divine speech, is a personal entity (shakhsi) and whether it can be defined. The commentary clarifies a crucial distinction: a real definition captures essence (dhāt), but a personal entity's essence cannot be captured through universal terms. Instead, only a distinguishing definition (hadd rusumi) is possible. The Hanafi school holds that the Quran is a specific composition (mu'allaf makhsus) that is one in essence, not multiplied by recitations. Understanding this distinction is key to proper usul methodology.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core entity under discussion
Determine what shakhsi means in this context – a specific individual or composition that does not accept multiplicity in essence.
Shakhsi is contrasted with kulli (universal).
2
Understand the two types of definitions
Differentiate between hadd haqiqi (real definition capturing essence) and hadd rusumi (distinguishing definition marking off from others).
Hadd haqiqi requires continuous applicability to the defined; shakhsi fails this.
3
Trace the Hanafi argument against defining shakhsi
Follow why specifying accidents can never make a definition exclusive in the intellect – only direct indication (ishara) can pinpoint an individual.
The intellect can always imagine another instance sharing the same accidents.
4
Apply to the Quran
See how the Quran is a specific composition (mu'allaf makhsus) that is one in essence despite multiple recitations; locus (mahall) does not affect its unity.
This avoids the error of thinking different recitations create different Qurans.

آجائے گا۔ فعلى هذا التقدير الحق مقصد یہ ہے کہ اگر قرآن نام ہو اس متولف مخصوص کا اور حق بھی یہی ہے پھر اشکال یہ تھا کہ اس تقریر حق کے مطابق قرآن شخصی نہیں رہے گا بلکہ تعدد کو قبول کرلے گا تو اس کا جواب مصنف نے یہ دیا کہ اس صورت میں قرآن پاک کے شخصی ہونے کی دو تاویلیں ہوں گی اول یہ کہ قرآن کے شخصی ہونے کا مطلب یہ ہوگا کہ وہ خود تو شخصی نہیں ہے البتہ شخصی کے مشابہ ہے مشابہت یہ ہے کہ جس طرح شخصی حد کو قبول نہیں کرتا اس کی معرفت اشارہ وغیرہ کے ساتھ ہوتی ہے اسی طرح قرآن مجید بھی قابل حد نہیں بلکہ اس کی حقیقت کی معرفت اس طرح ہوگی کہ اس کو اول تا آخر پڑھا جائے اور پھر کہا جائے بالاشارہ کہ ھو ھذہ الکلمات بهذا الترتیب اور دوسری تاویل یہ کی جائے گی کہ وہ مولف مخصوص جونی حد ذاتہ متعدد نہ ہو بلکہ اس کا تعد محل کے تعدد کی وجہ سے ہوا اگر چہ یہ خصی حقیقی نہیں ہے لیکن ہم نے اصطلاح بنالی ہے کہ ہم اس شخصی کو حقیقی کہتے ہیں اور اس پر یہ حکم لگاتے ہیں کہ وہ قابل حد نہیں ہے کیونکہ اس کی معرفت یا اشارہ سے حاصل ہوتی ہے یا قراء من اوله الی آخرہ سے حاصل ہوتی ہے لہذا ی شخصی ہے۔ ولا يخفى ان الکلام یہ ماقبل کا تقہ ہے مقصد یہ ہے کہ یہ جو کہا گیا ہے کہ شخصی کی حد نہیں ہو سکتی اس سے مراد حد حقیقی ہے کہ اس کی حقیقت و ماہیت کی تحدید نہیں ہوسکتی بلکہ قرائت اور اشارہ ضروری ہے اگر معرفت حقیقت من التحدید مقصود نہ ہو بلکہ صرف شخصی کا امتیاز عن الاغیار مقصود ہوتو یہ ممکن ہے مثلا شخص کی حدیوں کی جائے القرآن حوا لمجموع المنقول بین دفتی المصاحف تو اترا تو یہ حد امتیازعن الخیر کا فائدہ دے رہی ہے اسی طرح کشاف کی تعریف کی جائے ھو الکتاب الذى صفہ جار الله زمخشری فی تفسیر القرآن اور نو کی تعریف کی جائے ھو علم بحث فی عن احوال الکلم اعراب و بناء یہ سب شخص کی ایسی حدود ہیں جو اسکوممتاز عن الخیر کر رہی ہیں ۔قوله فان الاعراض تنتهى الخ : غرض شارح توضیح متن ! مقصد یہ ہے کہ اعراض اپنے مشخصات کی وجہ سے ایسے مرتبہ تعین تک پہنچ جاتے ہیں کہ ان میں تعدد کا امکان باقی نہیں رہتا صرف امر واحد پر صادق آتے ہیں ہاں ان کے اندر تعد محل کی وجہ سے تعدد ہو سکتا ہے مثلا امرکی القیس کا قصیدہ ہے، قضا سبک من ذکری حبیب و منزل اب یہ قصیدہ اپنے مشخصات یعنی تالیف مخصوص جو مخصوص حروف کلمات ابیات کی وجہ سے حاصل ہوئی ہے اسی طرح وہ صیت جو حرکات سکنات سے حاصل ہوئی ہے ان مشخصات کی وجہ سے ایسی حد تک پہنچ گیا ہے کہ اس کی ذات میں کوئی تعدد نہیں ہو سکتا اور صدق علی الکثیر ممکن نہیں ہے البتہ تعدد لافظ کی وجہ سے اس میں تعدد ہو سکتا ہے اب اگر اس کے ساتھ تشخص لافظ بھی ملا دیا جائے مثلاً یہ اعتبار کر لیا جائے کہ وہ قصیدہ جو لسان امری القیس کے ساتھ مخصوص وقت میں قائم تھا تو پھر یہ خصی حقیقی بن جائے گا اور اس میں تعدد اصلا ممکن نہیں ہوگا مصنف نے یہ اصطلاح بنالی ہے کہ وہ ایسے مولف مخصوص کو شخصی حقیقی بننے سے قبل اور محل کے ساتھ قائم ہونے سے قبل شخصی کہتا ہے لا مشاحۃ فی الاصطلاح - قوله وقد عرف ابن الحاجب ظاهر تعريفه الخ شارح تفتازانی مصنف کے اعتراض علی ابن

If they become, then the definition would not apply to the limited, so the continuity of applicability would not remain, whereas for a definition continuous applicability is a condition. When both scenarios become invalid, it is established that the personal [shakhsi]... and there is consideration. The commentator Taftazani also raises an objection to this answer: we adopt the second branch, that the definition of the personal is made with essentials and accidents. As for the objection that in this case due to the disappearance of accidents the applicability of the definition would not remain continuously, the answer is that when the specifying accidents disappear, the defined also disappears. So if the definition does not apply to the defined, there is no harm, rather in that case it is necessary that the definition not apply, because the defined no longer exists. The truth is that the personal... After all this refutation, the commentator finally answers: such a definition of the personal that distinguishes it from all others in terms of existence is possible, but such a definition that distinguishes it from all others in the intellect is not possible, because no matter how many specifying accidents are mentioned in the definition, the intellect can still apply them to multiple things; only indication can specify and personalize something. Therefore, the definition of the personal will distinguish it from all others in terms of existence, but in the intellect there remains the possibility of sharing. According to his statement that the truth is this, and that the Qur'an—the author said that the application of the Holy Qur'an is to two meanings: one to the specified [mushakhkhas] and the other to the specific composition [mu'allaf makhsus]. And regarding this, the author said that the truth is this. The commentator explains: the truth is that the Holy Qur'an is the name of this specific composition in which there is no multiplicity in terms of essence, and there is no multiplicity due to difference and multiplicity of pronunciations, because it is clear that whoever among us reads this specific composition, it is the same that Gabriel brought and revealed to the Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him); this specific composition does not change. And if the first branch is adopted, i.e., consideration of the locus [mahall] along with this specific composition, for example it is said that the Holy Qur'an is the name of this specific [mushakhkhas] that subsists in the tongue of Gabriel, then this Qur'an would not be identical to it but similar to it, because the Holy Qur'an is speech and speech is an accident, and accidents become specified by their loci. Then the Holy Qur'an which subsists in different loci would become multiple, whereas that is not the case. Rather, we all recite the same Holy Qur'an that was revealed to the Prophet (peace be upon him). So it is known that in the Holy Qur'an, specification by locus is not considered. Likewise is the case with all poetry. The purpose is that all poems and books attributed to a particular person have the same condition: they are the name of the specific composition, whether Zayd recites them or 'Amr recites them or someone else recites them. Similarly, the case of sciences: there is no multiplicity in them in terms of essence. For example, the science of grammar is the name of specific rules, whether Zayd learns them or 'Amr learns them or someone else. In short, there is no multiplicity in them in terms of essence, but there is multiplicity due to multiplicity of loci. So on this estimation, the truth is that if the Qur'an is the name of this specific composition—and this is the truth—then the objection was that according to this statement of truth, the Qur'an would not remain personal but would accept multiplicity. The author answered that in this case, the Qur'an being personal has two interpretations: first, that the meaning of the Qur'an being personal is that it itself is not personal, but it is similar to the personal. The similarity is that just as the personal does not accept definition and its knowledge is by indication etc., similarly the Holy Qur'an is not definable; rather, knowledge of its reality is such that it is read from beginning to end, and then it is said by indication: 'It is these words in this order.' The second interpretation is that that specific composition is not multiple in itself, but its multiplicity occurred due to the multiplicity of loci. Although it is not truly personal, we have made a convention that we call this personal in reality and apply the ruling to it that it is not definable, because its knowledge is obtained either by indication or by reading from its beginning to its end. Therefore it is personal. And it is not hidden that the speech is the summary of what came before. The purpose is that what was said that the personal cannot be defined refers to a real definition that defines its reality and essence; rather, reading and indication are necessary. If knowledge of reality by definition is not intended, but only distinguishing the personal from others is intended, then this is possible. For example, a definition of the personal is given: 'the Qur'an is the sum of what is transmitted between the two covers of the codices,' so this definition serves the benefit of distinguishing it from others. Similarly, definition of al-Kashshaf: 'It is the book written by Jarallah al-Zamakhshari in tafsir of the Qur'an,' and definition of grammar: 'It is a science that discusses the states of words in terms of declension and construction.' All these are definitions of personal things that distinguish them from others. His statement: 'For accidents reach...' The purpose of the commentator of al-Tawdih on the text is that accidents, due to their specifics, reach a level of determination such that the possibility of multiplicity does not remain; they apply only to one thing. However, multiplicity can occur in them due to multiplicity of loci. For example, Imru' al-Qays's poem: 'Stop, let us weep at the memory of a beloved and an abode.' This poem, due to its specifics, i.e., the specific composition obtained from specific letters, words, and verses, and similarly the sound obtained from movements and pauses, has reached such a level that there can be no multiplicity in its essence, and it cannot apply to many. However, multiplicity can occur in it due to multiplicity of utterances. Now if the specification of the utterer is also added, e.g., it is considered that it was the poem that subsisted in the tongue of Imru' al-Qays at a specific time, then it becomes truly personal and multiplicity is absolutely impossible. The author made a convention that he calls such a specific composition 'personal' before it becomes truly personal and before it subsists in a locus. There is no quarrel in terminology. His statement: 'And Ibn al-Hajib defined... apparent definition of his...' The commentator Taftazani's objection to the author against Ibn al-Hajib...
Source pages p. 286

Commentary
This passage is a critical refinement (hishiya) from a Hanafi usul commentary. It opens with a continuation of a debate about the definition (hadd) of a personal entity (shakhsi). The central issue is whether the Quran, as a divine speech, qualifies as shakhsi and whether it can be defined. The author (likely the matn writer, possibly al-Tawdih) and the commentator (Sharh al-Tawdih) address objections raised by Taftazani. The passage proceeds as follows: First, it refines the notion of shakhsi, distinguishing between definitions that distinguish in existence vs. in the intellect. The commentator asserts that a definition differentiating a personal entity from others in existence is possible, but doing so in the intellect (i.e., capturing its essence through universal terms) is impossible because accidents can be shared. Only direct indication (ishara) can specify an individual. This is a key usul point: the intellect cannot grasp an individual's essence through a universal definition. Then, the discussion shifts to the Quran. The author had stated that the Quran applies to two meanings: a specified individual (mushakhkhas) and a specific composition (mu'allaf makhsus). The commentator clarifies that the truth is that the Quran is the name of the specific composition, which is one in essence, not multiplied by different recitations or loci. If one considers the locus (mahall), then each recitation would be a different accident, leading to multiplicity, which contradicts the fact that we recite the same Quran revealed to the Prophet (PBUH). Thus, specification by locus is not considered for the Quran. This is then generalized: all poems and books attributed to an author, and all sciences like grammar, are shakhsi in essence, not multiplied by being learned or recited by different persons. They only become multiple due to loci, but that does not affect their essential unity. The passage then addresses an objection: if the Quran is a specific composition, it would not be shakhsi but would accept multiplicity. The author gives two interpretations. First, the Quran is not shakhsi in itself but is similar to shakhsi in that it is known by indication (reading from start to end) and not by a descriptive definition. Second, we can adopt a terminological convention: we call the specific composition 'shakhsi' even though it is not truly personal, because its knowledge is obtained only by indication or complete reading. Consequently, it is not 'definable' in the sense of a real definition (hadd haqiqi) that captures its quiddity. However, a definition that merely distinguishes (hadd rusumi) is possible, as exemplified by defining the Quran as 'the sum transmitted between the two covers of the codices,' or defining al-Kashshaf as 'Zamakhshari's tafsir book,' or grammar as 'the science of word states.' These are personal definitions that serve to distinguish, not to explain essence. The passage then comments on a statement about accidents reaching a point where they cannot be multiple in essence, but can be multiple due to loci, using Imru' al-Qays's poem as an example. The author's convention is clarified: he calls the specific composition 'shakhsi' before it becomes truly personal (i.e., before considering the locus). Taftazani's objection to Ibn al-Hajib's definition is mentioned but not fully detailed. Throughout, the commentary follows the Hanafi usul tradition, emphasizing the distinction between real and nominal definitions and the role of indication in personal knowledge.

Key Terms
شخصي (shakhsi): An entity that is specified as an individual, having a particular composition or set of accidents that distinguish it in existence but not necessarily in the intellect.
حد حقيقي (hadd haqiqi): A definition that captures the essence (dhāt) and quiddity (māhiyya) of a thing, applicable universally.
مولف مخصوص (mu'allaf makhsus): A specific composition of words or elements that is attributed to a source (e.g., the Quran, a poem) and is considered one in essence.
حد رسمي (hadd rusumi): A definition that distinguishes the defined from others without capturing its essence; also called descriptive definition.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why cannot a real definition (hadd haqiqi) be given for a personal entity like the Quran?
A) Because personal entities have no essence.
B) Because no matter how many specifying accidents are listed, the intellect can still apply them to multiple things.
C) Because the Quran is divine and beyond human understanding.
D) Because definitions are only allowed for material objects.

Key Takeaway
A personal entity (shakhsi) cannot have a real definition (hadd haqiqi) because its accidents are not exclusive in the intellect; only a distinguishing definition (hadd rusumi) is possible, and the Quran is known through direct indication or complete recitation.

Amm and Khass — General and Specific
advanced
10 min
Defining Surah and Avoiding Circularity in Usul
Response to Ibn al-Hajib on defining surah without circularity

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the two interpretations of Ibn al-Hajib's definition of surah and explain why using the Shari'ah-specific definition leads to circularity in defining the Qur'an.

Core Idea
Defining 'surah' as a Shari'ah-specific term creates circularity; the linguistic meaning must be used to define the Qur'an.

In this passage, a Hanafi commentator (al-Taftazani) attempts to resolve a potential circularity in Ibn al-Hajib's definition of the Qur'an as 'revealed speech consisting of surahs'. The key issue is whether 'surah' is defined in its linguistic sense (any part of a revealed book) or its Shari'ah sense (a part of the Qur'an specifically). As you will see, the commentator's choice of interpretation and the critique that follows reveal a fundamental usul principle: definitions of Shari'ah terms must respect technical usage, and departing from it renders the argument weak.

Guided Path
1
Locate the Potential Circularity
Identify why defining the Qur'an using 'surah' might be circular. The Qur'an is revealed speech; if surah is defined as a part of the Qur'an, then explaining 'Qur'an' through 'surah' assumes knowledge of the Qur'an, creating dependency.
2
Recognise the Two Interpretations
Ibn al-Hajib's phrase 'with a surah from it' can be read in two ways: (1) personal totality (the specific collection of Qur'anic surahs) or (2) universal meaning (a part of the genus of eloquent speech). The commentator shows both avoid circularity.
3
Analyse the Commentator's Argument
The commentator argues that neither interpretation leads to circularity because the quiddity of surah (its essence) does not depend on the Qur'an; 'surah' in language applies to any scripture's sections. Thus knowledge of surah is independent.
4
Evaluate the Objection
The objector points out that in Shari'ah terminology, 'surah' is specifically 'a part of the Qur'an with defined beginning and end'. Using the linguistic meaning departs from the apparent (zahir) and from the real (haqiqah) to the metaphorical (majaz), weakening the definition.
This illustrates the principle that technical terms of Shari'ah must be defined according to their shar'i usage.

الحاجب کا جواب دینے کی کوشش کر رہے ہیں دو احتمال تھے کہ ابن الحاجب کی تعریف یا مجموع شخصی کی ہوگی یا مفہوم کلی کی ہمصنف نے دونوں پر اشکال کیا تھا شارح کہتے ہیں کہ ظاہر عبارة ابن الحاجب سے معلوم ہوتا ہے کہ تعریف مجموع شخصی کی ہے. وجہ یہ ہے کہ اس نے بسورۃ منہ کہا ہے یہ من تبعیضیہ ہے اور منہ کی ضمیر راجع الى الكلام المنزل ہے معنی ہوگا مجر من المجموع اس سے معلوم ہوا کہ تعریف مجموع شخصی کی ہے تو دور لازم نہیں آئے گا اگر مفہوم کلی کی تعریف بنائی جائے یہ بھی ممکن ہے وہ اس طرح کہ منہ کی ضمیر جنس قرآن کی طرف راجع ہو عبارة یوں ہوگی۔ میجر من جنس القرآن یعنی وہ سورۃ فصیح و بلیغ ہو جس طرح قرآن مجید فصیح و بلیغ ہے تو پھر یہ مفہوم کلی کی تعریف ہوگی شارح کہتے ہیں کہ دونوں صورتوں میں ازوم دور نہیں ہوگا کیونکہ معرفت قرآن تو موقوف ہے ماہیت سورۃ پر لیکن ماہیت سورۃ ماہیت قرآن پر موقوف نہیں ہے کیونکہ سورۃ کی تعریف ہے، جو بعض مترجم اولہ و آخرہ تو قیفا من کلام منزل اب اس کی معرفت قرآن پر موقوف ہے کیونکہ یہ عام ہے قران غیر قرآن سب کو شامل ہے کیونکہ تو راۃ، انجیل کے سور کو بھی سور کہا جاتا ہے معلوم ہوا کہ سورۃ کے مفہوم میں خصوصیت قرآن معتبر نہیں ہے یہی وجہ ہے ابن الحاجب نے منہ کا لفظ ذکر کیا ہے تو ضمیر راجع الی الکلام المنزل ہے اگر مفہوم سورہ صرف قرآن کے ساتھ خاص ہوتا تو منہ کے ذکر کرنے کی ضرورت نہ ہوتی۔ جواب ! شارخ نے سورۃ کی جو تعریف کی ہے اور اس سے دور کو رفع کرنے کی کوشش کی ہے یہ سعی لاحاصل ہے کیونکہ یہ تعریف عدول من الظاہر ہے کیونکہ ہر اصطلاح میں اس کے اپنے مفہوم اصطلاحی مراد ہوتے ہیں اور شریعت کی اصطلاح میں سورۃ کا مفہوم۔ وھی بعض من القرآن مترجم اولہ و آخرہ تو قیفا ہے یہی تعریف تفاسیر معتبرہ کشاف وغیرہ میں مذکور ہے لہذا اس سے عدول عدول عن الظاہر الی اٹھی، ومن الحقیقۃ الی المجاز ہوگا۔
Misk al-Malih. [He] is trying to answer Ibn al-Hajib. There were two possibilities: that Ibn al-Hajib's definition is either of the personal totality or of the universal meaning. The author [of al-Tawdih] had objected to both. The commentator [al-Taftazani?] says that from the apparent wording of Ibn al-Hajib, it seems that the definition is of the personal totality. The reason is that he said 'with a surah from it' – 'min' is partitive, and the pronoun in 'minhu' refers back to 'the revealed speech'. The meaning would be 'a part from the totality', so it is known that the definition is of the personal totality. Then circularity does not arise. If it is made a definition of the universal meaning, that is possible such that the pronoun refers to the genus of the Qur'an. The expression would be 'a part from the genus of the Qur'an', meaning that the surah is eloquent and eloquent like the Qur'an. Then it would be a definition of the universal meaning. The commentator says that in both cases, circularity does not arise because knowledge of the Qur'an depends on the quiddity of surah, but the quiddity of surah does not depend on the quiddity of the Qur'an because the definition of surah is 'a part of revealed speech whose beginning and end are defined'. Now knowledge of this depends on the Qur'an because it is general including the Qur'an and non-Qur'an, because the surahs of Tawrah and Injeel are also called surah. So it is known that specificity to the Qur'an is not considered in the meaning of surah. This is why Ibn al-Hajib mentioned the word 'minhu' so that the pronoun refers to the revealed speech. If the meaning of surah were specific to the Qur'an, there would be no need to mention 'minhu'. Response: The commentator's definition of surah and his attempt to remove circularity is fruitless because this definition is a departure from the apparent, because in every discipline its own technical meaning is intended, and in the terminology of Shari'ah the meaning of surah is: 'a part of the Qur'an whose beginning and end are defined'. This definition is mentioned in authoritative tafsirs like Kashshaf. Therefore departing from it is a departure from the apparent to the far-fetched, and from the real to the metaphorical.
Source pages p. 288

Commentary
This passage is a critical refinement (hashiya) on the definition of surah in the context of defining the Qur'an. The author (Taftazani?) is responding to Ibn al-Hajib's definition of the Qur'an as 'a revealed speech consisting of surahs' and the potential circularity. Two interpretations of Ibn al-Hajib's definition are discussed: personal totality (defining the Qur'an as the whole collection of surahs) or universal meaning (defining each surah as a part of the genus of eloquent speech like the Qur'an). The commentator argues that both avoid circularity because the quiddity of surah does not depend on the Qur'an; 'surah' in language applies to parts of any scripture. However, the objector insists that in Shari'ah terminology, 'surah' is specifically a part of the Qur'an, so the definition becomes circular unless one uses the linguistic meaning. Thus the commentator's attempt fails because he departed from the technical meaning. This highlights the usul principle that technical terms of Shari'ah must be respected when defining Shari'ah concepts.

Key Terms
سورۃ (surah): A part of the Qur'an with defined beginning and end (according to Shari'ah terminology).
مجموع شخصی (majmu' shakhsi): Personal totality; the entire collection of surahs as a unique entity.
مفہوم کلی (mafhum kulli): Universal meaning; a general concept applicable to multiple instances.
دور (dawr): Circularity in definition where A depends on B and B depends on A.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the objector in this passage, why is the commentator's definition of surah problematic?
A) It defines surah as part of any revealed book, ignoring Shari'ah specificity.
B) It creates a circular definition between surah and Quran.
C) It uses the linguistic meaning when the technical meaning is required.
D) It depends on knowledge of the Quran.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi usul, definitions of Shari'ah terms must adhere to their technical usage; departing from it for logical convenience is methodologically unsound.

Amm and Khass — General and Specific
intermediate
8 min
The Partitive 'Min' Argument for Reciting Translation in Prayer
Core Hanafi istinbat using partitive 'min'

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the partitive 'min' from Surah al-Muzzammil 73:20 to derive the Hanafi ruling that reciting the meaning of the Quran in prayer is sufficient.

Core Idea
The partitive 'min' in 'min al-Quran' allows reciting 'some' – which includes the meaning alone.

This passage from Misk al-Maleeh presents the Hanafi argument for praying with a translation. The commentator defends Imam Abu Hanifa's view that reciting the meaning in Persian fulfills the obligation of qira'ah. The core proof is the partitive 'min' in the verse 'faqra'u ma tayassara min al-Quran', which indicates that only part of the Quran is required, and 'part' can be either a portion of the Arabic text or the meaning alone.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two definitions of Quran
The commentator presents two possible definitions: (1) Quran is only the meaning (ma'na), (2) Quran is the composite of wording and meaning. The first definition directly supports reciting translation; the second requires additional reasoning.
Both definitions are valid within the Hanafi school.
2
Understand the objection about wording necessity
Objectors argue that if Quran is only meaning, then the Arabic wording becomes irrelevant; if it includes wording, then reciting only meaning seems to omit a required part.
This objection forces Imam Abu Hanifa to show how definition still requires some form of wording.
3
Analyze the partitive 'min' in the verse
The verse 'faqra'u ma tayassara min al-Quran' uses the partitive 'min' (tab'idiyya), meaning 'recite some of the Quran'. This 'some' can be understood in two ways.
The partitive 'min' is the key linguistic evidence.
4
Distinguish 'ba'd tarkibi' from 'ba'd basiti'
Ba'd tarkibi (compositional some): a portion of the entire Quran, e.g., one verse. Ba'd basiti (simple some): a part of the composite of wording and meaning, i.e., either wording alone or meaning alone.
The partitive 'min' includes both types.
5
Conclude that reciting meaning alone suffices
Since the partitive 'min' covers ba'd basiti, reciting only the meaning fulfills the command. Thus, praying with a translation is valid.
This argument does not discard wording entirely; Persian wording is considered a substitute (qaa'im maqam) for Arabic.
