Misk al-Maleeh, commentary on Al-Tawdih and Al-Talwih. And if it is sufficient that it is not Quran, then it becomes known that according to Imam [Abu Hanifa] reciting the Noble Quran in prayer is not an absolute obligation, and likewise it is invalid because it contradicts the text. And His saying: 'So recite what is easy of the Quran' [73:20]. We said: the Persian phrase establishes it. The commentator gives two answers to this objection. (1) First answer: We adopt the first option, that the Noble Quran is only the name of the meaning. There were two objections to this: (a) lack of consideration of knowledge in the Quran, (b) the definition does not apply to the Quran. The answer to both is that this objection would only arise if Imam [Abu Hanifa] had considered the wording (nazm) absolutely unworthy, whether Arabic wording or its substitute. But this is incorrect; rather, Imam considers Persian as a substitute for Arabic wording and calls it 'taqdir al-Quran'. Therefore both objections are removed. Imam also considered the wording, and also the definition applies to non-Arabic like Persian, because although Persian is not in reality the revealed written transmitted word, it is in estimation the written and transmitted between the covers of the Mushaf. Some said that this is the answer to the second option: that the Quran is not only the name of the meaning but the wording is also necessary. Then the objection was that in that case, non-obligation of reciting the Quran would be entailed. The answer was that the objection of non-obligation of Quranic recitation does not necessarily arise because Imam has made the Persian wording a substitute for the Arabic wording; therefore the obligation of Quranic recitation is fulfilled with that Persian wording. First, His saying: from here, the second answer is given: we adopt the second option, that the Noble Quran is the name of that which indicates the meaning. Then the objection was that according to Imam, non-obligation of Quranic recitation in prayer would be entailed. The answer is that Imam took from the verse 'So recite what is easy of the Quran' only the meaning of the Quran, not the wording. Therefore he said: in prayer, only consideration of the meaning is obligatory, not the wording. And Imam had a proof for this: that 'min' in 'min al-Quran' is partitive (tab'idiyya), so the meaning is: recite some of the Quran. And 'some' has two forms: (1) recite some of the entire Quran, e.g., one or two verses — this is called 'some by composition' (ba'd tarkibi). (2) The Quran which is a composite of wording and meaning — recite some of them, i.e., recite only the wording or only the meaning — this is called 'some by simplicity' (ba'd basiti). And the partitive 'min' includes both types of 'some', so it is proven that relying on the meaning alone is permissible! (See footnote 3, p. 79). Then if it is said: In the first case, there is combination of literal and figurative — the objection is that the application of 'Quran' to Arabic wording is literal, to non-Arabic is figurative. You have taken both Arabic wording and non-Arabic wording as intended in 'so recite what is easy of the Quran'. This is a combination of literal and figurative, which according to the Hanafis is invalid. We said: there is no combination of literal and figurative here; rather, from the text only the literal meaning of the Arabic Quran is intended. As for non-Arabic, we prove permissibility of prayer by qiyas on Arabic wording, or we prove Arabic wording by way of 'ibarat al-nass' and non-Arabic by way of 'dalalat al-nass', or we take from 'nazm al-Quran' a general figurative meaning: i.e., the wording that indicates the meaning absolutely, whether Arabic or non-Arabic. Thus the objection of combination of literal and figurative is removed. (See footnote 5, p. 8). His saying 'in other than Arabic' is an indication that Persian and others — the purpose of Taftazani in explaining the text: that the author mentioned the phrase 'bighayr al-arabiyya' and did not mention 'bil-farisiyya', indicating that Persian, non-Persian (Urdu, Siraiki, Pashto) are all included. Recitation in any language — according to Imam the prayer is valid. 'And it is said' — the second saying is mentioned: According to some, non-Arabic only means Persian; recitation is permissible only in Persian, not in Pashto, Punjabi, Urdu, etc., because Persian is close to Arabic, and the language of the people of Paradise, just as it is Arabic, it will also be Persian. (Footnote 7, p. 8). 'Even if he recites a verse' — an indication that it is not permissible to make it a habit and to persist in it. The purpose of the text: the commentator says: the author used the past tense verb 'qara'a' and indefinite 'ayah' to indicate that for a junub or menstruating woman, and even for a pure person, to make a habit of reciting the Quran in any language and to persist in it is not permissible; rather, it is haram, because it contradicts the majesty and reverence of the Noble Quran. Then it is said: the later scholars (mutakhkhirin) are of the opinion that... — the purpose of the objection: it comes upon the word 'khassatan' in the text. The objection is: the later Hanafi scholars like 'Allama Shaykh al-Islam Khaharzada etc. say: if someone recites a verse of sajdah in Persian, sajdah tilawah becomes wajib upon him. (2) If the Quran is written in Persian language, according to the later scholars, it is not permissible for an impure person to touch it. From these two issues, it is proven that outside of prayer, Imam did not consider Arabic wording as part of the Quran or necessary. Therefore, mentioning the word 'khassatan' is invalid. The answer from 'qulna': the author mentioned the word 'khassatan' according to the madhhab of the early scholars (mutaqaddimin), because there is no transmitted narrations (riwaya mansusa) from them on these issues. And the later scholars adopted their opinion in these issues based on caution (ihtiyat), because they saw that the intended essential pillar (rukn maqsudi asli) i.e., the meaning, exists and is confirmed. Therefore, they cautiously stated the obligation of sajdah and prohibition of touching the Mushaf. His saying 'but the most correct is that he returned to their opinion' — the purpose of explaining the text: that Imam [Abu Hanifa] retracted his opinion, as Nuh ibn Abi Maryam narrates from Imam. 'Allama Fakhr al-Islam explained the reason for the retraction: although Imam had his own proofs, apparently his opinion contradicts the Book of Allah because in the Noble Quran in two or three places the Quran is described as 'Arabic tongue'. And Abu al-Yusr, brother of Fakhr al-Islam, said: this issue is extremely difficult because nobody could clarify what Imam meant. And 'Allama Karti wrote a long treatise on this issue.
Source pages p. 298

Commentary
This passage is a Hanafi usul dispute concerning the definition of the Quran and the permissibility of reciting its translation in prayer. The commentator (sharh) addresses objections to Imam Abu Hanifa's ruling that praying in Persian (or any language) with the meaning of the Quran is valid. The core of the argument is the interpretation of the verse 'faqra'u ma tayassara min al-Quran' (Surah al-Muzzammil 73:20). Imam Abu Hanifa argues that the partitive 'min' indicates that what is required is 'some' of the Quran, which can be either a part of the whole text (ba'd tarkibi) or a part of the composite of wording and meaning (ba'd basiti). Thus, reciting only the meaning suffices. The commentator defends this by showing that the wording (nazm) is not entirely disregarded; Persian wording is considered a substitute (qaa'im maqam) for Arabic wording. Later, the passage discusses the opinion of later Hanafi scholars (mutakhkhirin) who, out of caution, hold that sajdah tilawah becomes wajib when reciting a sajdah verse in Persian, and that a Persian Quran is subject to the same prohibition of touching for the impure. However, the text concludes with the narration that Imam Abu Hanifa himself retracted his opinion because it apparently contradicts the Quranic description of the Quran as Arabic. This shows the complexity of the issue and the evolution of the Hanafi position.

Key Terms
القرآن (al-Quran): The Noble Quran: in this text, it is discussed whether it is only the meaning (ma'na) or the meaning and wording (nazm). The Hanafi position allows considering it the meaning alone for the purpose of prayer.
النظم (al-nazm): The wording or composition of the Quran. In this passage, it refers to the Arabic phrasing (nazm 'arabi) and its substitute (like Persian phrasing considered as taqdir al-Quran).
المعنى (al-ma'na): The meaning or content of the Quran. Imam Abu Hanifa considers it the essential part of the Quran; reciting the meaning in prayer fulfills the obligation.
من التبعيضية (min al-tab'idiyya): The partitive 'min' in the verse, indicating that only part of the Quran is required to be recited.
بعض ترکیبی (ba'd tarkibi): Compositional some: a portion of the entire Quran text, e.g., one or two verses.
بعض بسیطی (ba'd basiti): Simple some: a part of the Quran's composite of wording and meaning, i.e., either the wording or the meaning alone.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi argument from Surah al-Muzzammil 73:20, the partitive 'min' (من) indicates that the required recitation in prayer is:
A) The entire Quran in Arabic.
B) Some of the Quran, which can be either a portion of the Arabic text or only the meaning.
C) Only verses that are easy in Arabic.
D) A specific surah like al-Fatihah.

Key Takeaway
The partitive 'min' in the Quranic verse allows reciting only the meaning, which is the essence of the Quran, making translation in prayer valid according to Imam Abu Hanifa.

Amm and Khass — General and Specific
advanced
9 min
Responding to the Haqiqah-Majaz Objection on Translation
Defense against haqiqah-majaz objection and scope of languages

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to evaluate the Hanafi response to the objection that using 'Quran' for translation combines literal and figurative meanings, and determine the scope of permissible languages.

Core Idea
The Hanafi response avoids combining haqiqah and majaz by limiting the literal meaning to Arabic and using qiyas or dalaalat al-nass for non-Arabic.

After establishing the partitive 'min' argument, the commentator addresses a new objection: applying the word 'Quran' to both Arabic and non-Arabic translations is a combination of literal and figurative (haqiqah and majaz), which the Hanafis reject. He offers three alternative ways to justify the permissibility without making that combination. Additionally, he clarifies that the term 'bighayr al-arabiyya' includes all languages, not just Persian, though there is a minority opinion limiting it to Persian due to its proximity to Arabic.

Guided Path
1
Identify the objection of combining haqiqah and majaz
The objector argues that 'Quran' is literally applied to Arabic wording and figuratively to non-Arabic. If both are intended in the verse, that would combine literal and figurative, which is invalid according to Hanafi usul.
The hanafis generally do not allow using a word in both its literal and figurative senses simultaneously (jam' bayna al-haqiqah wa al-majaz).
2
Understand the three Hanafi responses
The commentator gives three solutions: (1) Only literal Arabic is intended from the text; non-Arabic is justified via qiyas. (2) Arabic is taken by 'ibarat al-nass, non-Arabic by 'dalalat al-nass'. (3) The phrase 'nazm al-Quran' is taken in a general figurative sense including all wording that indicates meaning.
Each response avoids combining the literal and figurative in one word.
3
Analyze the scope of permissible languages
The commentator points out that the author used 'bighayr al-arabiyya' (other than Arabic) rather than 'bil-farisiyya' (in Persian), indicating all languages are included. Then he mentions a counter-opinion (qil) that only Persian is allowed because of its closeness to Arabic and being the language of Paradise.
The dominant Hanafi view is all languages; the minority view restricts to Persian.
4
Recognize the practical application
The dispute over language scope affects which translations are valid for prayer. The Hanafi position largely accepts any language, while some scholars restrict to Persian.
Later Hanafi fatwa tends to follow the majority view allowing any language.

Misk al-Maleeh, commentary on Al-Tawdih and Al-Talwih. And if it is sufficient that it is not Quran, then it becomes known that according to Imam [Abu Hanifa] reciting the Noble Quran in prayer is not an absolute obligation, and likewise it is invalid because it contradicts the text. And His saying: 'So recite what is easy of the Quran' [73:20]. We said: the Persian phrase establishes it. The commentator gives two answers to this objection. (1) First answer: We adopt the first option, that the Noble Quran is only the name of the meaning. There were two objections to this: (a) lack of consideration of knowledge in the Quran, (b) the definition does not apply to the Quran. The answer to both is that this objection would only arise if Imam [Abu Hanifa] had considered the wording (nazm) absolutely unworthy, whether Arabic wording or its substitute. But this is incorrect; rather, Imam considers Persian as a substitute for Arabic wording and calls it 'taqdir al-Quran'. Therefore both objections are removed. Imam also considered the wording, and also the definition applies to non-Arabic like Persian, because although Persian is not in reality the revealed written transmitted word, it is in estimation the written and transmitted between the covers of the Mushaf. Some said that this is the answer to the second option: that the Quran is not only the name of the meaning but the wording is also necessary. Then the objection was that in that case, non-obligation of reciting the Quran would be entailed. The answer was that the objection of non-obligation of Quranic recitation does not necessarily arise because Imam has made the Persian wording a substitute for the Arabic wording; therefore the obligation of Quranic recitation is fulfilled with that Persian wording. First, His saying: from here, the second answer is given: we adopt the second option, that the Noble Quran is the name of that which indicates the meaning. Then the objection was that according to Imam, non-obligation of Quranic recitation in prayer would be entailed. The answer is that Imam took from the verse 'So recite what is easy of the Quran' only the meaning of the Quran, not the wording. Therefore he said: in prayer, only consideration of the meaning is obligatory, not the wording. And Imam had a proof for this: that 'min' in 'min al-Quran' is partitive (tab'idiyya), so the meaning is: recite some of the Quran. And 'some' has two forms: (1) recite some of the entire Quran, e.g., one or two verses — this is called 'some by composition' (ba'd tarkibi). (2) The Quran which is a composite of wording and meaning — recite some of them, i.e., recite only the wording or only the meaning — this is called 'some by simplicity' (ba'd basiti). And the partitive 'min' includes both types of 'some', so it is proven that relying on the meaning alone is permissible! (See footnote 3, p. 79). Then if it is said: In the first case, there is combination of literal and figurative — the objection is that the application of 'Quran' to Arabic wording is literal, to non-Arabic is figurative. You have taken both Arabic wording and non-Arabic wording as intended in 'so recite what is easy of the Quran'. This is a combination of literal and figurative, which according to the Hanafis is invalid. We said: there is no combination of literal and figurative here; rather, from the text only the literal meaning of the Arabic Quran is intended. As for non-Arabic, we prove permissibility of prayer by qiyas on Arabic wording, or we prove Arabic wording by way of 'ibarat al-nass' and non-Arabic by way of 'dalalat al-nass', or we take from 'nazm al-Quran' a general figurative meaning: i.e., the wording that indicates the meaning absolutely, whether Arabic or non-Arabic. Thus the objection of combination of literal and figurative is removed. (See footnote 5, p. 8). His saying 'in other than Arabic' is an indication that Persian and others — the purpose of Taftazani in explaining the text: that the author mentioned the phrase 'bighayr al-arabiyya' and did not mention 'bil-farisiyya', indicating that Persian, non-Persian (Urdu, Siraiki, Pashto) are all included. Recitation in any language — according to Imam the prayer is valid. 'And it is said' — the second saying is mentioned: According to some, non-Arabic only means Persian; recitation is permissible only in Persian, not in Pashto, Punjabi, Urdu, etc., because Persian is close to Arabic, and the language of the people of Paradise, just as it is Arabic, it will also be Persian. (Footnote 7, p. 8). 'Even if he recites a verse' — an indication that it is not permissible to make it a habit and to persist in it. The purpose of the text: the commentator says: the author used the past tense verb 'qara'a' and indefinite 'ayah' to indicate that for a junub or menstruating woman, and even for a pure person, to make a habit of reciting the Quran in any language and to persist in it is not permissible; rather, it is haram, because it contradicts the majesty and reverence of the Noble Quran. Then it is said: the later scholars (mutakhkhirin) are of the opinion that... — the purpose of the objection: it comes upon the word 'khassatan' in the text. The objection is: the later Hanafi scholars like 'Allama Shaykh al-Islam Khaharzada etc. say: if someone recites a verse of sajdah in Persian, sajdah tilawah becomes wajib upon him. (2) If the Quran is written in Persian language, according to the later scholars, it is not permissible for an impure person to touch it. From these two issues, it is proven that outside of prayer, Imam did not consider Arabic wording as part of the Quran or necessary. Therefore, mentioning the word 'khassatan' is invalid. The answer from 'qulna': the author mentioned the word 'khassatan' according to the madhhab of the early scholars (mutaqaddimin), because there is no transmitted narrations (riwaya mansusa) from them on these issues. And the later scholars adopted their opinion in these issues based on caution (ihtiyat), because they saw that the intended essential pillar (rukn maqsudi asli) i.e., the meaning, exists and is confirmed. Therefore, they cautiously stated the obligation of sajdah and prohibition of touching the Mushaf. His saying 'but the most correct is that he returned to their opinion' — the purpose of explaining the text: that Imam [Abu Hanifa] retracted his opinion, as Nuh ibn Abi Maryam narrates from Imam. 'Allama Fakhr al-Islam explained the reason for the retraction: although Imam had his own proofs, apparently his opinion contradicts the Book of Allah because in the Noble Quran in two or three places the Quran is described as 'Arabic tongue'. And Abu al-Yusr, brother of Fakhr al-Islam, said: this issue is extremely difficult because nobody could clarify what Imam meant. And 'Allama Karti wrote a long treatise on this issue.
Source pages p. 298

Commentary
This passage is a Hanafi usul dispute concerning the definition of the Quran and the permissibility of reciting its translation in prayer. The commentator (sharh) addresses objections to Imam Abu Hanifa's ruling that praying in Persian (or any language) with the meaning of the Quran is valid. The core of the argument is the interpretation of the verse 'faqra'u ma tayassara min al-Quran' (Surah al-Muzzammil 73:20). Imam Abu Hanifa argues that the partitive 'min' indicates that what is required is 'some' of the Quran, which can be either a part of the whole text (ba'd tarkibi) or a part of the composite of wording and meaning (ba'd basiti). Thus, reciting only the meaning suffices. The commentator defends this by showing that the wording (nazm) is not entirely disregarded; Persian wording is considered a substitute (qaa'im maqam) for Arabic wording. Later, the passage discusses the opinion of later Hanafi scholars (mutakhkhirin) who, out of caution, hold that sajdah tilawah becomes wajib when reciting a sajdah verse in Persian, and that a Persian Quran is subject to the same prohibition of touching for the impure. However, the text concludes with the narration that Imam Abu Hanifa himself retracted his opinion because it apparently contradicts the Quranic description of the Quran as Arabic. This shows the complexity of the issue and the evolution of the Hanafi position.

Key Terms
الحقيقة (al-haqiqah): Literal meaning. In this passage, the literal application of 'Quran' to Arabic wording.
المجاز (al-majaz): Figurative meaning. The application of 'Quran' to non-Arabic wording is considered figurative.
قياس (qiyas): Analogical reasoning. One of the methods to justify non-Arabic translation by analogy to Arabic.
عبارة النص (ibarat al-nass): Direct textual indication. Used to derive the ruling for Arabic wording directly.
دلالة النص (dalalat al-nass): Implied textual indication. Used to derive the ruling for non-Arabic wording from the same text.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi response to the haqiqah-majaz objection, which of the following is NOT one of the methods used to justify non-Arabic translation?
A) Qiyas on Arabic wording
B) Ibarat al-nass for Arabic and dalalat al-nass for non-Arabic
C) General figurative meaning of 'nazm al-Quran'
D) Assigning a new literal meaning to non-Arabic

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school rebuts the haqiqah-majaz objection by using alternative derivations, and the majority view allows prayer in any language, not just Persian.

Later Scholars' Caution and Imam Abu Hanifa's Alleged Retraction
Later caution and retraction regarding translation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to evaluate the later Hanafi scholars' positions on sajdah tilawah and touching Persian mushaf, and assess the claim that Imam Abu Hanifa retracted his opinion on translation in prayer.

Core Idea
Later Hanafi scholars adopted cautious positions (ihtiyat) on sajdah and mushaf for Persian, and Imam Abu Hanifa may have retracted due to apparent conflict with Quranic verses describing the Quran as Arabic.

The passage concludes with two additional discussions: the rulings of later Hanafi scholars on reciting a sajdah verse in Persian (sajdah tilawah becomes wajib) and touching a Persian mushaf (haram for impure). These rulings seem to contradict the original Hanafi position that non-Arabic wording is not Quran. The commentator explains that these are based on caution (ihtiyat) because the essential meaning (rukn maqsudi) is present. Finally, the passage reports that Imam Abu Hanifa retracted his own opinion, as narrated by Nuh ibn Abi Maryam, due to the Quran's description of itself as 'Arabic tongue'.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two issues where later scholars differ
Later scholars (mutakhkhirin) like Khaharzada hold: (a) reciting a sajdah verse in Persian makes sajdah tilawah wajib, (b) a Persian mushaf cannot be touched by an impure person. These imply that Persian is treated like Quran in these rulings.
This seems to conflict with the idea that Persian is not the revealed wording.
2
Understand the basis of these rulings: caution (ihtiyat)
The later scholars based their rulings on caution: the meaning (rukn maqsudi asli) is present, so they applied the rulings of Quran out of precaution, even though Persian wording is not the same as Arabic.
This is an example of ihtiyat overriding strict usul logic.
3
Analyze the report of Imam Abu Hanifa's retraction
The passage states that Imam Abu Hanifa retracted his opinion because his view apparently contradicted the Quranic verses that describe the Quran as Arabic (e.g., 'innā anzalnāhu qur'ānan 'arabiyyan'). Fakhr al-Islam and Abu al-Yusr comment on this.
This shows the importance of textual evidence even for a mujtahid.
4
Evaluate the impact on the original ruling
Despite the retraction, the original Hanafi position is still considered a valid ikhtilaf. Many later scholars continued to follow the ruling that translation in prayer is permissible, although the retraction is noted.
The retraction does not necessarily nullify the validity of the original ruling for those who are unaware or follow the earlier opinion.

Misk al-Maleeh, commentary on Al-Tawdih and Al-Talwih. And if it is sufficient that it is not Quran, then it becomes known that according to Imam [Abu Hanifa] reciting the Noble Quran in prayer is not an absolute obligation, and likewise it is invalid because it contradicts the text. And His saying: 'So recite what is easy of the Quran' [73:20]. We said: the Persian phrase establishes it. The commentator gives two answers to this objection. (1) First answer: We adopt the first option, that the Noble Quran is only the name of the meaning. There were two objections to this: (a) lack of consideration of knowledge in the Quran, (b) the definition does not apply to the Quran. The answer to both is that this objection would only arise if Imam [Abu Hanifa] had considered the wording (nazm) absolutely unworthy, whether Arabic wording or its substitute. But this is incorrect; rather, Imam considers Persian as a substitute for Arabic wording and calls it 'taqdir al-Quran'. Therefore both objections are removed. Imam also considered the wording, and also the definition applies to non-Arabic like Persian, because although Persian is not in reality the revealed written transmitted word, it is in estimation the written and transmitted between the covers of the Mushaf. Some said that this is the answer to the second option: that the Quran is not only the name of the meaning but the wording is also necessary. Then the objection was that in that case, non-obligation of reciting the Quran would be entailed. The answer was that the objection of non-obligation of Quranic recitation does not necessarily arise because Imam has made the Persian wording a substitute for the Arabic wording; therefore the obligation of Quranic recitation is fulfilled with that Persian wording. First, His saying: from here, the second answer is given: we adopt the second option, that the Noble Quran is the name of that which indicates the meaning. Then the objection was that according to Imam, non-obligation of Quranic recitation in prayer would be entailed. The answer is that Imam took from the verse 'So recite what is easy of the Quran' only the meaning of the Quran, not the wording. Therefore he said: in prayer, only consideration of the meaning is obligatory, not the wording. And Imam had a proof for this: that 'min' in 'min al-Quran' is partitive (tab'idiyya), so the meaning is: recite some of the Quran. And 'some' has two forms: (1) recite some of the entire Quran, e.g., one or two verses — this is called 'some by composition' (ba'd tarkibi). (2) The Quran which is a composite of wording and meaning — recite some of them, i.e., recite only the wording or only the meaning — this is called 'some by simplicity' (ba'd basiti). And the partitive 'min' includes both types of 'some', so it is proven that relying on the meaning alone is permissible! (See footnote 3, p. 79). Then if it is said: In the first case, there is combination of literal and figurative — the objection is that the application of 'Quran' to Arabic wording is literal, to non-Arabic is figurative. You have taken both Arabic wording and non-Arabic wording as intended in 'so recite what is easy of the Quran'. This is a combination of literal and figurative, which according to the Hanafis is invalid. We said: there is no combination of literal and figurative here; rather, from the text only the literal meaning of the Arabic Quran is intended. As for non-Arabic, we prove permissibility of prayer by qiyas on Arabic wording, or we prove Arabic wording by way of 'ibarat al-nass' and non-Arabic by way of 'dalalat al-nass', or we take from 'nazm al-Quran' a general figurative meaning: i.e., the wording that indicates the meaning absolutely, whether Arabic or non-Arabic. Thus the objection of combination of literal and figurative is removed. (See footnote 5, p. 8). His saying 'in other than Arabic' is an indication that Persian and others — the purpose of Taftazani in explaining the text: that the author mentioned the phrase 'bighayr al-arabiyya' and did not mention 'bil-farisiyya', indicating that Persian, non-Persian (Urdu, Siraiki, Pashto) are all included. Recitation in any language — according to Imam the prayer is valid. 'And it is said' — the second saying is mentioned: According to some, non-Arabic only means Persian; recitation is permissible only in Persian, not in Pashto, Punjabi, Urdu, etc., because Persian is close to Arabic, and the language of the people of Paradise, just as it is Arabic, it will also be Persian. (Footnote 7, p. 8). 'Even if he recites a verse' — an indication that it is not permissible to make it a habit and to persist in it. The purpose of the text: the commentator says: the author used the past tense verb 'qara'a' and indefinite 'ayah' to indicate that for a junub or menstruating woman, and even for a pure person, to make a habit of reciting the Quran in any language and to persist in it is not permissible; rather, it is haram, because it contradicts the majesty and reverence of the Noble Quran. Then it is said: the later scholars (mutakhkhirin) are of the opinion that... — the purpose of the objection: it comes upon the word 'khassatan' in the text. The objection is: the later Hanafi scholars like 'Allama Shaykh al-Islam Khaharzada etc. say: if someone recites a verse of sajdah in Persian, sajdah tilawah becomes wajib upon him. (2) If the Quran is written in Persian language, according to the later scholars, it is not permissible for an impure person to touch it. From these two issues, it is proven that outside of prayer, Imam did not consider Arabic wording as part of the Quran or necessary. Therefore, mentioning the word 'khassatan' is invalid. The answer from 'qulna': the author mentioned the word 'khassatan' according to the madhhab of the early scholars (mutaqaddimin), because there is no transmitted narrations (riwaya mansusa) from them on these issues. And the later scholars adopted their opinion in these issues based on caution (ihtiyat), because they saw that the intended essential pillar (rukn maqsudi asli) i.e., the meaning, exists and is confirmed. Therefore, they cautiously stated the obligation of sajdah and prohibition of touching the Mushaf. His saying 'but the most correct is that he returned to their opinion' — the purpose of explaining the text: that Imam [Abu Hanifa] retracted his opinion, as Nuh ibn Abi Maryam narrates from Imam. 'Allama Fakhr al-Islam explained the reason for the retraction: although Imam had his own proofs, apparently his opinion contradicts the Book of Allah because in the Noble Quran in two or three places the Quran is described as 'Arabic tongue'. And Abu al-Yusr, brother of Fakhr al-Islam, said: this issue is extremely difficult because nobody could clarify what Imam meant. And 'Allama Karti wrote a long treatise on this issue.
Source pages p. 298

Commentary
This passage is a Hanafi usul dispute concerning the definition of the Quran and the permissibility of reciting its translation in prayer. The commentator (sharh) addresses objections to Imam Abu Hanifa's ruling that praying in Persian (or any language) with the meaning of the Quran is valid. The core of the argument is the interpretation of the verse 'faqra'u ma tayassara min al-Quran' (Surah al-Muzzammil 73:20). Imam Abu Hanifa argues that the partitive 'min' indicates that what is required is 'some' of the Quran, which can be either a part of the whole text (ba'd tarkibi) or a part of the composite of wording and meaning (ba'd basiti). Thus, reciting only the meaning suffices. The commentator defends this by showing that the wording (nazm) is not entirely disregarded; Persian wording is considered a substitute (qaa'im maqam) for Arabic wording. Later, the passage discusses the opinion of later Hanafi scholars (mutakhkhirin) who, out of caution, hold that sajdah tilawah becomes wajib when reciting a sajdah verse in Persian, and that a Persian Quran is subject to the same prohibition of touching for the impure. However, the text concludes with the narration that Imam Abu Hanifa himself retracted his opinion because it apparently contradicts the Quranic description of the Quran as Arabic. This shows the complexity of the issue and the evolution of the Hanafi position.

Key Terms
احتياط (ihtiyat): Caution; a method used by later Hanafi scholars to apply rulings out of precaution when the essential meaning is present, even if the strict definition might not require it.
ركن مقصودي (rukn maqsudi): Essential pillar; the purpose of the act. In this context, the meaning is the rukn maqsudi of Quran recitation.
متقدمين (mutaqaddimin): Early Hanafi scholars; those before the later period.
متأخرين (mutakhkhirin): Later Hanafi scholars; those who often added cautious rulings.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why did later Hanafi scholars require sajdah tilawah for reciting a sajdah verse in Persian?
A) They considered Persian wording to be the literal Quran.
B) They adopted a stricter view out of caution because the meaning is present.
C) They overruled Imam Abu Hanifa's original opinion entirely.
D) They only followed the practice of the Prophet.

Key Takeaway
Later Hanafi caution and Imam Abu Hanifa's reported retraction show the evolution of fiqh, but the original ruling on translation in prayer remains a valid ikhtilaf.

Amm and Khass — General and Specific
intermediate
8 min
The Correct Order of the Four Divisions of Words (Usul al-Fiqh)
Explains the dispute over the order of the four divisions and Taftazani's resolution.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the correct order of the four divisions of words according to Taftazani and explain the rationale behind Fakhr al-Islam's alternative ordering.

Core Idea
The order of divisions follows the logical sequence: wadh' first, then zuhur/khafa, then istimal, then kayfiyya.

This passage from the Sharh al-Tawdih addresses a subtle disagreement about the order of the four divisions of words. The author of the matn presented a certain sequence, but Fakhr al-Islam reordered it. Taftazani steps in to justify Fakhr al-Islam's arrangement by distinguishing two types of tasarruf in speech. Understanding this order is crucial for correctly applying usul categories.

Guided Path
1
Identify the author's order
The author of al-Tawdih lists the divisions as: wadh' (placement), istimal (usage), zuhur/khafa (clarity/hiddenness), kayfiyyat al-dalala (quality of indication).
2
Recognize Fakhr al-Islam's objection
Fakhr al-Islam reorders them, placing zuhur/khafa before istimal, contrary to the author.
This is not a school dispute but an intra-Hanafi difference.
3
Understand Taftazani's justification
Taftazani argues that there are two types of tasarruf: in the word (wadh') and in the meaning (zuhur/khafa). Both relate to wadh', so they precede istimal, which depends on wadh'.
This reasoning explains why zuhur/khafa comes before istimal.
4
State the correct order
The correct order according to Taftazani is: wadh', then zuhur/khafa, then istimal, then kayfiyya.
This order is followed in later Hanafi usul works.

اقسام
His statement: 'with consideration of its placement for it' is an explanation of the four divisions in summary... The purpose of the commentator of al-Tawdih: The author mentioned the four divisions in summary and by mentioning the word 'thamr' (fruit) he pointed to the order of the second and third divisions, that the order mentioned in the text is correct, not that of Fakhr al-Islam. The order is: first is the placement (wadh') of the word for meaning, then the usage (istimal) of the word in meaning, then the clarity (zuhur) or hiddenness (khafa) of meaning from the word, then the quality of indication (kayfiyyat al-dalala) of the word for the meaning in which it is used. And Fakhr al-Islam advanced the third division (zuhur wa khafa) to the second and made usage the third, contrary to the author. The reason, as Taftazani answers, is that Fakhr al-Islam saw that there are two types of disposition (tasarruf) in speech: (1) disposition in the word (tasarruf fi al-lafz), which is making the word such that meaning can be understood from it; this is called wadh' al-lafz. (2) disposition in meaning (tasarruf fi al-ma'na), which is making the meaning such that it is understood from the word through clarity or hiddenness. Both these dispositions are connected to wadh', and wadh' precedes usage because usage depends on wadh'. Therefore, the division according to usage should come after both of them. So it is observed that the clarity and hiddenness of meaning comes first because it is connected to wadh', and usage comes after. And Allah knows best. Then the word in relation to meaning is divided according to the division of the people (qawm). Taftazani now states the divisions of the first division and its exclusive proof: According to the people (qawm), the first division, which is based on placement (wadh'), yields four types: khaas, aam, mushtarak, and muawwal. (He said 'according to the people' because according to the author, there are only three types; muawwal is not included in this division.) The exclusive proof: A word either indicates one meaning or multiple meanings. If it indicates one meaning, then either it indicates individually (ala al-infiraad) or collectively among individuals (ala al-ishtiraak bayn al-afraad). The first is khaas, the second is aam. If it indicates multiple meanings, then either one of its meanings has preference (tarjeeh) or not. The first is muawwal, the second is mushtarak. The author omitted muawwal, i.e., he did not count muawwal among the divisions of the first division; instead he included 'jama munkar' (indefinite plural); the reason will come later. The second division: the explanation of the second division and its exclusive proof. The second division also yields four types: haqiqah, majaz, sarih, kinayah. The exclusive proof: A word is either used in its original meaning (ma'na mawdu' lahu) or in a meaning other than that. The first is haqiqah, the second is majaz. Then each of these is either clear in indicating the speaker's intent or hidden. The first is sarih, the second is kinayah. The third division: the explanation of the third division and its exclusive proof; it yields eight types.
Source pages p. 300

Commentary
This passage from the Sharh al-Tawdih explains the four-fold classification of words according to wadh' (placement). The author of the matn listed the divisions in a specific order: wadh', istimal, zuhur/khafa, kayfiyyat al-dalala. Fakhr al-Islam disagreed and reordered them, placing zuhur/khafa before istimal. Taftazani justifies Fakhr al-Islam's order by distinguishing two types of tasarruf: one in the word (wadh') and one in the meaning (zuhur/khafa). Since both relate to wadh', they precede istimal, which depends on wadh'. Thus, the correct order is wadh', then zuhur/khafa, then istimal, then kayfiyya. Taftazani then elaborates on the first division (bi'tibar al-wadh') which yields four types: khaas (specific), aam (general), mushtarak (homonym), and muawwal (interpreted). He provides an exclusive proof (dalil hasr) for this division. He notes that the author of the matn omitted muawwal and included 'jama munkar' (indefinite plural) instead. Then he briefly explains the second division (bi'tibar al-istimal) which yields haqiqah, majaz, sarih, kinayah. The third division (bi'tibar zuhur wa khafa wa kayfiyya) yields eight types.

Key Terms
وضع (wadh'): The original placement of a word for a meaning.
استعمال (istimal): The usage of a word in a meaning.
ظهور (zuhur): Clarity of meaning from the word.
خفاء (khafa): Hiddenness of meaning from the word.
کیفیت دلالت (kayfiyyat al-dalala): The quality of indication (how the word points to its meaning).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Taftazani, which division comes immediately after wadh'?
A) Istimal
B) Zuhur/khafa
C) Kayfiyyat al-dalala
D) Takhsis

Key Takeaway
The correct order of usul word analysis is wadh', then zuhur/khafa, then istimal, then kayfiyya.

Amm and Khass — General and Specific
intermediate
10 min
Classification of Words by Wadh': Khaas, Aam, Mushtarak, Muawwal
Defines the four types of words by wadh' with exclusive proof and disputes muawwal.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify any word into one of four categories (khaas, aam, mushtarak, muawwal) based on its original placement, using the exclusive proof (dalil hasr).

Core Idea
Every word, by its original placement, is either specific, general, homonymous, or interpreted.

In this lesson, we dive into the first division of words based on their original placement (wadh'). The Hanafi usul framework divides words into four types: khaas, aam, mushtarak, and muawwal. This classification is foundational for interpreting legal texts. We will also examine the exclusive proof (dalil hasr) and a notable disagreement about including muawwal.

Guided Path
1
Understand the two axes
Words either indicate one meaning or multiple meanings. This creates the first branch.
2
Classify one-meaning words
If the word indicates one meaning, it is either khaas (individual, proper name) or aam (collective, like 'men').
Khaas is not shared; aam applies to a class.
3
Classify multiple-meaning words
If the word indicates multiple meanings, it is either muawwal (one meaning is preferred by evidence) or mushtarak (no preference).
Muawwal is a distinct category in Hanafi usul.
4
Apply the exclusive proof
The logical chain: word indicates one or multiple meanings -> one: either individual or collective -> multiple: either preferred or not. This covers all possibilities and yields exactly four types.
This is a form of division by dichotomy (taqseem).
5
Note the dispute on muawwal
The author of al-Tawdih omitted muawwal and replaced it with 'jama munkar' (indefinite plural). The reason is deferred.
This shows that usul classifications can vary among scholars.

Commentary
This passage from the Sharh al-Tawdih explains the four-fold classification of words according to wadh' (placement). The author of the matn listed the divisions in a specific order: wadh', istimal, zuhur/khafa, kayfiyyat al-dalala. Fakhr al-Islam disagreed and reordered them, placing zuhur/khafa before istimal. Key Terms
خاص (khaas): A word indicating one meaning individually.
عام (aam): A word indicating one meaning collectively.
مشترک (mushtarak): A word indicating multiple meanings without preference.
ماؤل (muawwal): A word indicating multiple meanings with one preferred.
جمع منکر (jama munkar): Indefinite plural (e.g., 'men' in an indefinite sense).

The Order of the Four Divisions of Lafz
Dispute about the order of divisions and logical justification.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the correct order of the four divisions of lafz according to Sadr al-Shariah and explain why Fakhr al-Islam's alternative ordering is not accepted in the matn.

Core Idea
Coinage precedes usage; clarity relates to coinage, so it comes second.

In Hanafi usul, words are classified by four aspects: coinage, usage, clarity, and mode of indication. The correct order is debated within the school. This lesson clarifies the author's order and the reasoning behind it, contrasting it with Fakhr al-Islam's proposal.

Guided Path
1
Identify the four divisions
List the four aspects: wad' (coinage), isti'mal (usage), zuhur/khafa (clarity/obscurity), kayfiyat dalalah (mode of indication).
2
State the author's order
The author (Sadr al-Shariah) places coinage first, then usage, then clarity, then mode of indication.
3
State Fakhr al-Islam's alternative
He places clarity before usage.
4
Analyze the reasoning
Taftazani explains that both coinage and clarity relate to coinage, while usage builds on them, so clarity should come second.
5
Determine the mufta bihi
The author's order is the one presented in the matn and is considered correct for the text.

His statement 'by consideration of its coinage' is an explanation of the four divisions in summary. The purpose is that the author mentioned the four divisions in summary, and by mentioning the word 'thamar' he indicated the order of the second and third divisions, that the order mentioned in the text is correct, not that of Fakhr al-Islam. That order is: first, the coinage of a word for a meaning; then, the usage of the word in the meaning; then, the clarity or obscurity of the meaning from the word; then, the quality of the word's indication of the meaning in which it is used. Fakhr al-Islam advanced the third division (clarity/obscurity) to second and made usage the third, contrary to the author. Taftazani answers that Fakhr al-Islam did so because he saw that there are two kinds of disposal in speech: (1) disposal in the word, which is to make the word such that the meaning is understood from it—this is called coinage; (2) disposal in the meaning, i.e., to determine the meaning so that it is understood from the word through clarity or obscurity. Both disposals relate to coinage, and coinage precedes usage because usage depends on coinage. Therefore, the division by usage should come after both, but note that clarity/obscurity comes first because it relates to coinage, and usage comes after. Then he says: The word with respect to meaning is divided into four divisions according to the people. Taftazani now explains the first division and its proof of enumeration. He says: According to the people, the first division—by coinage—yields four types: khas, 'amm, mushtarak, mu'awwal. He said 'according to the people' because according to the author there are three types; mu'awwal is not included in this division. The proof of enumeration: A word either indicates one meaning or multiple meanings. If it indicates one meaning, then either individually or collectively across individuals: first is khas, second is 'amm. If it indicates multiple meanings, then either one meaning is given preference (mu'awwal) or not (mushtarak). The author omitted mu'awwal and included jami' munkar instead, the reason to come later. The second division: by usage, also yields four: haqiqah, majaz, sarih, kinayah. Proof: The word is used either in its original (subject) meaning or in a non-original meaning; first haqiqah, second majaz. Then each either clearly conveys the speaker's intent or hides it; first sarih, second kinayah. The third division: yields eight: zahir, nass, mufassar, muhkam, and their opposites khafi, mushkil, mujmal, mutashabih. Proof: If the meaning is apparent from the word, then either it accepts ta'wil (interpretation) or not. If it accepts ta'wil, then either its clarity is due to the wording itself (zahir) or not (nass). If it does not accept ta'wil, then either it can be abrogated (mufassar) or not (muhkam). If the meaning is hidden, then either the hiddenness is due to something external (khafi) or the wording itself. If due to the wording itself, then either the hiddenness can be removed by deep reflection (mushkil) or not; then either it requires clarification (mujmal) or not (mutashabih). The fourth division: yields four: 'ibarah al-nass, isharah al-nass, dalalah al-nass, iqtida' al-nass. Proof: The meaning is either established by the order of speech or not. If by order, then either the speech is expressly made for it ('ibarah) or implied (isharah). If not by order, then either the ruling is established by linguistic implication (dalalah) or by necessity (iqtida'). The basis of all this is induction (istiqra'), but this is a way of organizing it. Then a question: It is a settled rule that the categories of a division must be mutually exclusive, but here they are not—e.g., a word can be both 'amm and mushtarak. Answer: The rule applies within a single division; across different divisions, exclusivity is not required. Within each division, the categories are exclusive. For example, a name can be mu'rab versus mabni, and also ma'rifah versus nakirah; a single name can be both mu'rab and ma'rifah. Alternatively, if exclusivity across all divisions is intended, it is not essential but by consideration, so a restriction must be added.
Source pages p. 300

Commentary
This passage from Sharh al-Tawdih wa al-Talwih (by Taftazani) explains the fourfold classification of lafz (word) in Hanafi usul al-fiqh. The commentator begins by clarifying the correct order of the divisions as per the author (Sadr al-Shariah), contrasting it with the alternative ordering by Fakhr al-Islam. The order is: (1) coinage (wad'), (2) usage (isti'mal), (3) clarity/obscurity (zuhur/khafa), (4) mode of indication (kayfiyat dalalah). Fakhr al-Islam placed clarity/obscurity before usage based on the relationship with coinage; Taftazani explains and does not reject it outright. Then each division is detailed with a logical proof (burhan hasr) that enumerates all possibilities exclusivity. 

For the first division (by coinage), the types are khas (specific), 'amm (general), mushtarak (homonym), and mu'awwal (interpreted) according to the majority, but the author omits mu'awwal and includes jami' munkar (aggregate indefinite) instead. The proof uses a binary tree: one meaning vs multiple; within one: individual vs collective; within multiple: preferred meaning vs non-preferred. 

Second division (by usage): haqiqah (literal), majaz (metaphorical), sarih (clear), kinayah (allusive). Proof: used in original meaning or not; if original, then clear or hidden intent of the speaker. 

Third division (by clarity): eight categories: zahir (apparent, may accept ta'wil), nass (explicit, does not accept ta'wil but can be abrogated? actually nass is clear but may accept ta'wil? the text says zahir accepts ta'wil, nass does not—so nass is higher clarity than zahir?), mufassar (detailed, can be abrogated), muhkam (firm, cannot be abrogated), and their opposites: khafi (hidden due to external cause), mushkil (hidden due to wording but resolvable by reflection), mujmal (ambiguous requiring clarification), mutashabih (unclear and not resolvable). The proof is based on apparent vs hidden meaning, then within apparent: acceptance of ta'wil, then ability to be abrogated; within hidden: cause of hiddenness and resolvability. 

Fourth division (by mode of indication): 'ibarah al-nass (explicit indication), isharah al-nass (implicit indication), dalalah al-nass (alluded meaning via language), iqtida' al-nass (necessary implication). Proof: meaning established by speech order or not; if by order, express or implied; if not, by linguistic implication or necessity. 

Finally, Taftazani addresses a methodological concern: the categories across divisions are not mutually exclusive (e.g., a word can be both 'amm and mushtarak). He gives two answers: (a) exclusivity is only required within each division, not across different divisions, as each division considers a different aspect (e.g., a single noun can be both mu'rab and ma'rifah); (b) if exclusivity is to be maintained across all, it is not essential (dhati) but by consideration (i'tibari), requiring a qualifying restriction. This distinction is crucial in Hanafi usul to avoid confusion when classifying terms.

The passage is a typical expansion probe in which the commentator (Taftazani) elaborates on the matn of al-Tawdih, providing logical proofs and clarifying scholarly differences.

Key Terms
خاص (khas): A word that indicates one specific individual meaning, e.g., 'Zaid'
عام (amm): A word that indicates a plurality of individuals collectively, e.g., 'men'
مشترک (mushtarak): A word coined for multiple meanings with no preference, e.g., 'ayn (eye, spring, etc.)
مؤول (mu'awwal): A word that indicates multiple meanings but one is given preference by a contextual clue, e.g., 'quru' (menstruation or purity) interpreted as menstruation
حقیقت (haqiqah): A word used in its original coined meaning
مجاز (majaz): A word used in a meaning other than its original, with a relationship and a deterrent to the original meaning
صریح (sarih): A word that clearly conveys the speaker's intended meaning without ambiguity
کنایہ (kinayah): A word that hints at the intended meaning, allowing for the possibility of the literal meaning
ظاہر (zahir): A word whose meaning is apparent but may accept ta'wil (interpretation) that is not obvious
نص (nass): A word whose meaning is so clear that it does not accept ta'wil, according to the text
مفسر (mufassar): A word that is explained in detail and can be abrogated
محکم (muhkam): A word that is firm and cannot be abrogated
خفی (khafi): A word whose meaning is hidden due to an external reason (e.g., a foreign term)
مشکل (mushkil): A word whose meaning is hidden due to the wording itself but can be resolved through deep reflection
مجمل (mujmal): A word that requires external clarification (bayan) to be understood
متشابہ (mutashabih): A word whose meaning cannot be resolved at all (e.g., mutashabihat in Quran)
عبارۃ النص (ibarah al-nass): A meaning directly indicated by the wording of the text through primary intent
اشارۃ النص (isharah al-nass): A meaning indirectly indicated by the wording but not the primary intent
دلالۃ النص (dalalah al-nass): A meaning deduced via linguistic implication (e.g., analogy from the text's logic)
اقتضاء النص (iqtida' al-nass): A meaning that the text necessitates but does not explicitly state (e.g., an omitted condition)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Taftazani, why did Fakhr al-Islam place clarity before usage?
A) Because he thought usage is less important.
B) Because he believed that both coinage and clarity relate to meaning and should precede usage.
C) Because he wanted to contradict the author.
D) Because he confused the order.

Key Takeaway
The correct order of word classification is coinage, usage, clarity/obscurity, then mode of indication.

Amm and Khass — General and Specific
intermediate
12 min
First Division: Khas, 'Amm, Mushtarak, and Mu'awwal
Logical proof and categories of the first division by coinage.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the logical proof (burhan hasr) to classify any word under the first division by coinage.

Core Idea
A word either indicates one meaning (individually or collectively) or multiple meanings (with or without preference).

The first division of lafz categorizes words based on their coinage for meaning. Using a simple binary enumeration, Taftazani derives four categories: khas, 'amm, mushtarak, and mu'awwal (or jami' munkar according to the author). This lesson walks through the proof and the intra-Hanafi dispute about mu'awwal.

Guided Path
1
Start the binary tree
A word either indicates one meaning or multiple meanings. There is no third possibility.
2
Branch for one meaning
If one meaning: it indicates it either individually (khas) or collectively across individuals (amm).
3
Branch for multiple meanings
If multiple meanings: either one meaning is given preference by a contextual clue (mu'awwal) or none is preferred (mushtarak).
4
Note the author's adjustment
The author (Sadr al-Shariah) excludes mu'awwal from this division and includes jami' munkar (aggregate indefinite) instead, considering mu'awwal a matter of interpretation, not coinage.
5
Apply to a word
Given a word, follow the binary tree to classify it under the first division. For example, 'al-rijal' indicates one meaning (male adults) collectively, so it is 'amm.

His statement 'by consideration of its coinage' is an explanation of the four divisions in summary. The purpose is that the author mentioned the four divisions in summary, and by mentioning the word 'thamar' he indicated the order of the second and third divisions, that the order mentioned in the text is correct, not that of Fakhr al-Islam. That order is: first, the coinage of a word for a meaning; then, the usage of the word in the meaning; then, the clarity or obscurity of the meaning from the word; then, the quality of the word's indication of the meaning in which it is used. Fakhr al-Islam advanced the third division (clarity/obscurity) to second and made usage the third, contrary to the author. Taftazani answers that Fakhr al-Islam did so because he saw that there are two kinds of disposal in speech: (1) disposal in the word, which is to make the word such that the meaning is understood from it—this is called coinage; (2) disposal in the meaning, i.e., to determine the meaning so that it is understood from the word through clarity or obscurity. Both disposals relate to coinage, and coinage precedes usage because usage depends on coinage. Therefore, the division by usage should come after both, but note that clarity/obscurity comes first because it relates to coinage, and usage comes after. Then he says: The word with respect to meaning is divided into four divisions according to the people. Taftazani now explains the first division and its proof of enumeration. He says: According to the people, the first division—by coinage—yields four types: khas, 'amm, mushtarak, mu'awwal. He said 'according to the people' because according to the author there are three types; mu'awwal is not included in this division. The proof of enumeration: A word either indicates one meaning or multiple meanings. If it indicates one meaning, then either individually or collectively across individuals: first is khas, second is 'amm. If it indicates multiple meanings, then either one meaning is given preference (mu'awwal) or not (mushtarak). The author omitted mu'awwal and included jami' munkar instead, the reason to come later. The second division: by usage, also yields four: haqiqah, majaz, sarih, kinayah. Proof: The word is used either in its original (subject) meaning or in a non-original meaning; first haqiqah, second majaz. Then each either clearly conveys the speaker's intent or hides it; first sarih, second kinayah. The third division: yields eight: zahir, nass, mufassar, muhkam, and their opposites khafi, mushkil, mujmal, mutashabih. Proof: If the meaning is apparent from the word, then either it accepts ta'wil (interpretation) or not. If it accepts ta'wil, then either its clarity is due to the wording itself (zahir) or not (nass). If it does not accept ta'wil, then either it can be abrogated (mufassar) or not (muhkam). If the meaning is hidden, then either the hiddenness is due to something external (khafi) or the wording itself. If due to the wording itself, then either the hiddenness can be removed by deep reflection (mushkil) or not; then either it requires clarification (mujmal) or not (mutashabih). The fourth division: yields four: 'ibarah al-nass, isharah al-nass, dalalah al-nass, iqtida' al-nass. Proof: The meaning is either established by the order of speech or not. If by order, then either the speech is expressly made for it ('ibarah) or implied (isharah). If not by order, then either the ruling is established by linguistic implication (dalalah) or by necessity (iqtida'). The basis of all this is induction (istiqra'), but this is a way of organizing it. Then a question: It is a settled rule that the categories of a division must be mutually exclusive, but here they are not—e.g., a word can be both 'amm and mushtarak. Answer: The rule applies within a single division; across different divisions, exclusivity is not required. Within each division, the categories are exclusive. For example, a name can be mu'rab versus mabni, and also ma'rifah versus nakirah; a single name can be both mu'rab and ma'rifah. Alternatively, if exclusivity across all divisions is intended, it is not essential but by consideration, so a restriction must be added.
Source pages p. 300

Commentary
This passage from Sharh al-Tawdih wa al-Talwih (by Taftazani) explains the fourfold classification of lafz (word) in Hanafi usul al-fiqh. The commentator begins by clarifying the correct order of the divisions as per the author (Sadr al-Shariah), contrasting it with the alternative ordering by Fakhr al-Islam. The order is: (1) coinage (wad'), (2) usage (isti'mal), (3) clarity/obscurity (zuhur/khafa), (4) mode of indication (kayfiyat dalalah). Fakhr al-Islam placed clarity/obscurity before usage based on the relationship with coinage; Taftazani explains and does not reject it outright. Then each division is detailed with a logical proof (burhan hasr) that enumerates all possibilities exclusivity. 

For the first division (by coinage), the types are khas (specific), 'amm (general), mushtarak (homonym), and mu'awwal (interpreted) according to the majority, but the author omits mu'awwal and includes jami' munkar (aggregate indefinite) instead. The proof uses a binary tree: one meaning vs multiple; within one: individual vs collective; within multiple: preferred meaning vs non-preferred. 

Second division (by usage): haqiqah (literal), majaz (metaphorical), sarih (clear), kinayah (allusive). Proof: used in original meaning or not; if original, then clear or hidden intent of the speaker. 

Third division (by clarity): eight categories: zahir (apparent, may accept ta'wil), nass (explicit, does not accept ta'wil but can be abrogated? actually nass is clear but may accept ta'wil? the text says zahir accepts ta'wil, nass does not—so nass is higher clarity than zahir?), mufassar (detailed, can be abrogated), muhkam (firm, cannot be abrogated), and their opposites: khafi (hidden due to external cause), mushkil (hidden due to wording but resolvable by reflection), mujmal (ambiguous requiring clarification), mutashabih (unclear and not resolvable). The proof is based on apparent vs hidden meaning, then within apparent: acceptance of ta'wil, then ability to be abrogated; within hidden: cause of hiddenness and resolvability. 

Fourth division (by mode of indication): 'ibarah al-nass (explicit indication), isharah al-nass (implicit indication), dalalah al-nass (alluded meaning via language), iqtida' al-nass (necessary implication). Proof: meaning established by speech order or not; if by order, express or implied; if not, by linguistic implication or necessity. 

Finally, Taftazani addresses a methodological concern: the categories across divisions are not mutually exclusive (e.g., a word can be both 'amm and mushtarak). He gives two answers: (a) exclusivity is only required within each division, not across different divisions, as each division considers a different aspect (e.g., a single noun can be both mu'rab and ma'rifah); (b) if exclusivity is to be maintained across all, it is not essential (dhati) but by consideration (i'tibari), requiring a qualifying restriction. This distinction is crucial in Hanafi usul to avoid confusion when classifying terms.

The passage is a typical expansion probe in which the commentator (Taftazani) elaborates on the matn of al-Tawdih, providing logical proofs and clarifying scholarly differences.

Key Terms
خاص (khas): A word indicating one specific individual meaning.
عام (amm): A word indicating a plurality of individuals collectively.
مشترک (mushtarak): A word coined for multiple meanings without preference.
مؤول (mu'awwal): A word indicating multiple meanings with one preferred by context.
جمع منکر (jami' munkar): An indefinite plural, e.g., 'rijalun' (some men).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the logical proof, what is the category of a word that indicates multiple meanings with no preference?
A) Khas
B) 'Amm
C) Mushtarak
D) Mu'awwal

Key Takeaway
The first division by coinage yields khas, 'amm, mushtarak, and mu'awwal (or jami' munkar) based on a simple binary enumeration.

Amm and Khass — General and Specific
intermediate
10 min
Second Division: Haqiqah, Majaz, Sarih, and Kinayah
Logical proof and categories of the second division by usage.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify a word under the second division by usage and identify its haqiqah/majaz and sarih/kinayah status.

Core Idea
A word is used either in its original meaning (haqiqah) or a transferred meaning (majaz); then each is either clear (sarih) or allusive (kinayah).

The second division considers how a word is used in speech. It first distinguishes literal from metaphorical usage, then whether the intended meaning is clear or hidden. This lesson presents the logical proof that yields four categories: haqiqah (literal), majaz (metaphorical), sarih (explicit), and kinayah (allusive).

Guided Path
1
Start the binary tree for usage
A word is used either in its original coined meaning (haqiqah) or in a non-original meaning (majaz).
2
Branch for haqiqah and majaz
Each of these can be either: the speaker's intent is clear (sarih) or hidden (kinayah).
3
Identify haqiqah sarih
A word used literally with clear intent. Example: 'asad' for lion in its literal sense when the speaker intends a lion.
4
Identify haqiqah kinayah
A word used literally but with a hidden intent. Example: 'Zaid is a lion' when lion is used literally? Actually no, that's majaz. For haqiqah kinayah, think of euphemisms like 'he entered' for marital relations.
5
Identify majaz sarih and kinayah
Majaz sarih: metaphor where the intended meaning is clear. Majaz kinayah: metaphor where the intended meaning is alluded to.

لفظ مین ہے یہ عام ہے اس حیثیت سے کہ آنکھ کے تمام افراد کو شامل ہے اور مشترک ہے اس حیثیت سے کہ بھر اور
His statement 'by consideration of its coinage' is an explanation of the four divisions in summary. The purpose is that the author mentioned the four divisions in summary, and by mentioning the word 'thamar' he indicated the order of the second and third divisions, that the order mentioned in the text is correct, not that of Fakhr al-Islam. That order is: first, the coinage of a word for a meaning; then, the usage of the word in the meaning; then, the clarity or obscurity of the meaning from the word; then, the quality of the word's indication of the meaning in which it is used. Fakhr al-Islam advanced the third division (clarity/obscurity) to second and made usage the third, contrary to the author. Taftazani answers that Fakhr al-Islam did so because he saw that there are two kinds of disposal in speech: (1) disposal in the word, which is to make the word such that the meaning is understood from it—this is called coinage; (2) disposal in the meaning, i.e., to determine the meaning so that it is understood from the word through clarity or obscurity. Both disposals relate to coinage, and coinage precedes usage because usage depends on coinage. Therefore, the division by usage should come after both, but note that clarity/obscurity comes first because it relates to coinage, and usage comes after. Then he says: The word with respect to meaning is divided into four divisions according to the people. Taftazani now explains the first division and its proof of enumeration. He says: According to the people, the first division—by coinage—yields four types: khas, 'amm, mushtarak, mu'awwal. He said 'according to the people' because according to the author there are three types; mu'awwal is not included in this division. The proof of enumeration: A word either indicates one meaning or multiple meanings. If it indicates one meaning, then either individually or collectively across individuals: first is khas, second is 'amm. If it indicates multiple meanings, then either one meaning is given preference (mu'awwal) or not (mushtarak). The author omitted mu'awwal and included jami' munkar instead, the reason to come later. The second division: by usage, also yields four: haqiqah, majaz, sarih, kinayah. Proof: The word is used either in its original (subject) meaning or in a non-original meaning; first haqiqah, second majaz. Then each either clearly conveys the speaker's intent or hides it; first sarih, second kinayah. The third division: yields eight: zahir, nass, mufassar, muhkam, and their opposites khafi, mushkil, mujmal, mutashabih. Proof: If the meaning is apparent from the word, then either it accepts ta'wil (interpretation) or not. If it accepts ta'wil, then either its clarity is due to the wording itself (zahir) or not (nass). If it does not accept ta'wil, then either it can be abrogated (mufassar) or not (muhkam). If the meaning is hidden, then either the hiddenness is due to something external (khafi) or the wording itself. If due to the wording itself, then either the hiddenness can be removed by deep reflection (mushkil) or not; then either it requires clarification (mujmal) or not (mutashabih). The fourth division: yields four: 'ibarah al-nass, isharah al-nass, dalalah al-nass, iqtida' al-nass. Proof: The meaning is either established by the order of speech or not. If by order, then either the speech is expressly made for it ('ibarah) or implied (isharah). If not by order, then either the ruling is established by linguistic implication (dalalah) or by necessity (iqtida'). The basis of all this is induction (istiqra'), but this is a way of organizing it. Then a question: It is a settled rule that the categories of a division must be mutually exclusive, but here they are not—e.g., a word can be both 'amm and mushtarak. Answer: The rule applies within a single division; across different divisions, exclusivity is not required. Within each division, the categories are exclusive. For example, a name can be mu'rab versus mabni, and also ma'rifah versus nakirah; a single name can be both mu'rab and ma'rifah. Alternatively, if exclusivity across all divisions is intended, it is not essential but by consideration, so a restriction must be added.
Source pages p. 300

Commentary
This passage from Sharh al-Tawdih wa al-Talwih (by Taftazani) explains the fourfold classification of lafz (word) in Hanafi usul al-fiqh. The commentator begins by clarifying the correct order of the divisions as per the author (Sadr al-Shariah), contrasting it with the alternative ordering by Fakhr al-Islam. The order is: (1) coinage (wad'), (2) usage (isti'mal), (3) clarity/obscurity (zuhur/khafa), (4) mode of indication (kayfiyat dalalah). Fakhr al-Islam placed clarity/obscurity before usage based on the relationship with coinage; Taftazani explains and does not reject it outright. Then each division is detailed with a logical proof (burhan hasr) that enumerates all possibilities exclusivity. 

For the first division (by coinage), the types are khas (specific), 'amm (general), mushtarak (homonym), and mu'awwal (interpreted) according to the majority, but the author omits mu'awwal and includes jami' munkar (aggregate indefinite) instead. The proof uses a binary tree: one meaning vs multiple; within one: individual vs collective; within multiple: preferred meaning vs non-preferred. 

Second division (by usage): haqiqah (literal), majaz (metaphorical), sarih (clear), kinayah (allusive). Proof: used in original meaning or not; if original, then clear or hidden intent of the speaker. 

Third division (by clarity): eight categories: zahir (apparent, may accept ta'wil), nass (explicit, does not accept ta'wil but can be abrogated? actually nass is clear but may accept ta'wil? the text says zahir accepts ta'wil, nass does not—so nass is higher clarity than zahir?), mufassar (detailed, can be abrogated), muhkam (firm, cannot be abrogated), and their opposites: khafi (hidden due to external cause), mushkil (hidden due to wording but resolvable by reflection), mujmal (ambiguous requiring clarification), mutashabih (unclear and not resolvable). The proof is based on apparent vs hidden meaning, then within apparent: acceptance of ta'wil, then ability to be abrogated; within hidden: cause of hiddenness and resolvability. 

Fourth division (by mode of indication): 'ibarah al-nass (explicit indication), isharah al-nass (implicit indication), dalalah al-nass (alluded meaning via language), iqtida' al-nass (necessary implication). Proof: meaning established by speech order or not; if by order, express or implied; if not, by linguistic implication or necessity. 

Finally, Taftazani addresses a methodological concern: the categories across divisions are not mutually exclusive (e.g., a word can be both 'amm and mushtarak). He gives two answers: (a) exclusivity is only required within each division, not across different divisions, as each division considers a different aspect (e.g., a single noun can be both mu'rab and ma'rifah); (b) if exclusivity is to be maintained across all, it is not essential (dhati) but by consideration (i'tibari), requiring a qualifying restriction. This distinction is crucial in Hanafi usul to avoid confusion when classifying terms.

The passage is a typical expansion probe in which the commentator (Taftazani) elaborates on the matn of al-Tawdih, providing logical proofs and clarifying scholarly differences.

Key Terms
حقیقت (haqiqah): A word used in its original coined meaning
مجاز (majaz): A word used in a meaning other than its original, with a relationship and a deterrent to the original meaning
صریح (sarih): A word that clearly conveys the speaker's intended meaning without ambiguity
کنایہ (kinayah): A word that hints at the intended meaning, allowing for the possibility of the literal meaning

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the proof, what determines whether a word is sarih or kinayah?
A) Whether the word is used literally or metaphorically.
B) Whether the speaker's intent is clear or hidden.
C) Whether the word is general or specific.
D) Whether the word has one meaning or multiple.

Key Takeaway
The second division classifies words by usage: literal vs metaphorical, and clear vs allusive.

Amm and Khass — General and Specific
intermediate
15 min
Third Division: From Zahir to Mutashabih
Logical proof and categories of the third division by clarity/obscurity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify a word into one of eight categories of clarity/obscurity using the logical proof from the passage.

Core Idea
Clarity of meaning ranges from apparent (zahir) to utterly ambiguous (mutashabih), with intermediate stages based on acceptance of ta'wil and abrogability.

The third division analyzes the degree of clarity or obscurity of a word's meaning. Using a binary tree, Taftazani derives eight categories: zahir, nass, mufassar, muhkam, and their opposites khafi, mushkil, mujmal, mutashabih. This lesson walks through the proof and explains each category.

Guided Path
1
First split: apparent vs hidden meaning
Is the meaning apparent from the wording? If yes, go to step 2; if hidden, go to step 5.
2
If apparent: does it accept ta'wil?
If it accepts ta'wil (interpretation that is not obvious), then it is either zahir (clarity from wording itself) or nass (clarity from context? Actually nass does not accept ta'wil).
3
If apparent and does not accept ta'wil: can it be abrogated?
If it can be abrogated, it is mufassar; if it cannot be abrogated, it is muhkam.
4
If hidden: identify the cause
If hidden due to external reason (e.g., foreign term): khafi. If hidden due to wording itself: then can reflection resolve it? If yes: mushkil; if not: then either requires clarification (mujmal) or cannot be resolved (mutashabih).
5
Complete the eight categories
List: zahir, nass, mufassar, muhkam, khafi, mushkil, mujmal, mutashabih.

سے خالی نہیں یا تو علی الانفراد دلالت کرے گا یا علی الاشتراک بین الافراد دلالت کرے گا اول خاص، ثانی ، عام ہے اور اگر معانی متعدد پر دلالت کر رہا ہے تو دو حال سے خالی نہیں یا تو اس کے معانی میں سے کسی معنی کو تر جیح حاصل ہوگی یا نہ اول ماؤل ، ثانی مشترک ہے۔ والمصنف اسقط الماول یعنی مصنف نے ماؤں کو تقسیم اول کے اقسام میں شمار نہیں کیا اس کی جگہ جمع منکر کو داخل کیا اس کی وجہ آگے آئے گی۔ والتقسيم
